==PAGE:3==
ABSTRACT
FairFishing is a Danish-Somali non-governmental organisation that works to expand fisheries and improve
working and living conditions for their partners, fishers and mongers, in the horn of Africa. FairFishing has
teamed up with DTU in order to identify viable options to utilise fish residues generated from their Somali
partners in the city Berbera, in the state Somaliland. The aim of the project was therefore to suggest viable
fish residue management options for FairFishing to implement, in order to generate a surplus to benefit
livelihood and quality of life for their local partners. The objectives were to collect data on fish residues,
review possible treatment technology options, compare and select two suitable options through a ranking
scheme, and perform a financial analysis, to determine the viability, sensitivity and risk of the two options. It
was found from the study that FairFishing’s fish residues originate predominantly from tuna. In 2021, from
FairFishing Berbera based partners, a projected average daily fish residue generation reached two tonnes a
day spread out over all of Somaliland. Hereof, 640 kg was projected to be generated at Berbera station and
market. Seven treatment options were identified and screened: current management; fish meal production;
fish silage production; black soldier fly larvae production; biogas production; biochar/charcoal production;
and compost production. Two were selected based on a multi-criteria ranking: dried fish silage and biogas
production. A ”proof-of-concept”-scale scenario for each options was formed, meaning a small, local scale
solution. A cost-benefit analysis for each scenario was carried out, showing that the net present value
(NPV), with and without funding, of the dried Fish silage scenario was larger than the NPV for the Biogas
scenario with co-digested fish residues for biogas production. However, the Fish silage scenario was found
to have a higher NPV deviation compared to the Biogas scenario. As the Fish silage scenario has a higher
chance of generating a larger NPV, it is therefore the solution that most likely will economically benefit
FairFishing’s partners most. To improve accuracy of results, it is recommended to further investigate and
analyse the fish residue flow and composition, and to either investigate animal feed demand or quantities
available of other organic waste streams and wastewaters.
RESUMÉ
FairFishing er en Dansk-Somalisk ikke-statslig organisation, der arbejder for at udbrede fiskeri og forbedre
arbejds- og leveforhold for deres partnere, fiskere og sælgere i Afrikas horn. FairFishing ønsker at udnytte
fiskeaffald genereret af deres partnere i Berbera, i staten Somaliland, Somalia, for at producere et værdi-
fuldt produkt. Fairfishing har derfor indgået et samarbejde med DTU for at undersøge deres muligheder.
Formålet med projektet var derfor at finde og anbefale højest to holdbare løsninger til FairFishing, som kan
generere et overskud til økonomisk fordel for deres partnere. Målsætningen for projektet var hermed: at
indsamle data på fiskeaffald, sammenligne og udvælge to passende løsninger til håndtering af fiskeaffald
gennem et rangeringssystem, og at udføre en økonomisk analyse. Studiet fandt, at det genererede fiskeaf-
fald hovedsageligt stammer fra forskellige tunarter og at der i 2021 gennemsnitligt vil genereres 640 kg
fiskeaffald dagligt ved Berbera station og marked. Af de syv identificerede og foreslåede håndteringsløs-
ninger; nuværende affaldshåndtering; produktion af fiskemel; produktion af fiskeensilage; produktion af
sort soldaterflue-larver; produktion af biogas; produktion af biokul/kulbriketter; og produktion af kompost,
blev to løsninger fundet mest passende; produktion af tørret Fiskeensilage og Biogas. En finansiel cost-
benefit-analyse af de to foreslåede løsninger viste en højere nettonutidsværdi, med og uden finansiering,
ved produktion af tørret fiskeensilage i forhold til produktion af biogas ved hjælp af fiskeaffald forrådnet
med andet organisk affald. Fiskeensilage-scenariets nettonutidsværdi har større spredning sammenlignet
med Biogas-scenariet.
Da Fiskeensilagescenariet har større chance for at generere en højere nettonu-
tidsværdi, vil dette scenarie højst sandsynligt skabe en større økonomisk fordel for FairFishings partnere.
For at mindske resultaternes usikkerhed anbefales det, at yderlige undersøgelser af fiskeaffaldsstrømme
og -komposition bliver foretaget, og at enten efterspørgsel på dyrefoder eller at tilgængelighed af andre
organiske affaldsstrømme samt spildevand bliver undersøgt nærmere.
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
ii
==PAGE:4==
EXECUTIVE SUMMARY
Background and aim
Somalia today faces multiple challenges. On of them is their current waste treatment method, landfilling.
Due to unsustainable waste management, they miss out on possible revenues from recycling waste and
conversion of organic waste to e.g. animal feed, fuel or fertiliser.
FairFishing is a Danish-Somali non-governmental organisation that works to expand sustainable fisheries,
to create jobs and economic development in the horn of Africa. Thereby they seek to improve working
and living conditions for their Somali partners. FairFishing has the vision to utilise fish residues generated
from their partners (fisher and mongers) in the city Berbera in the state Somaliland, Somalia, rather than
wasting it at local landfills. For this reason, they have initiated the ”waste-to-resource”-project and teamed
up with DTU in order to identify viable options for fish residue utilisation. FairFishing’s vision is to utilise
all fish residues generated from fish caught by their partners. However, to proof the viability of such a
project, FairFishing as a first step implementation wants a solution that converts fish residues in a ”proof-
of-concept”-scale. A ”proof-of-concept” solution was here defined as a small centralised solution. They
prioritise a method that may help cover an internal supply of fuel and thereby generate economic savings.
Alternatively, they desire a solution that converts fish residues to sellable products to generate revenues.
Revenues and savings are expected to benefit their partners e.g. by improved fish cooling. Proper fish
cooling is one of the steps in FairFishing’s vision of expanding sustainable fisheries in Somalia.
The aim of the project was thus to suggest viable management options for FairFishing to implement that
may generate a surplus to benefit livelihood and quality of life for their local partners. To achieve the aim,
the objectives of the project were:
1. to create a data inventory about fish residues, current waste management and demand of potential
products in a local present context through surveys of relevant stakeholders and communication with
FairFishing staff;
2. to screen for suitable fish residue treatment options through a technology review and then select two
options for further assessment, through a self-produced and comprehensive ranking system;
3. to form a detailed ”proof-of-concept” functional unit and scenario with a final setup based on a
combination of results from the data inventory and technology screening;
4. to assess economic viability of the two most suitable options through a full techno-economic assess-
ment;
5. to present and inform FairFishing about possible options and economic benefits through reporting,
recommendations and presentation.
Methods
Initially, relevant stakeholders were identified and analysed for importance and influence to understand
the local context of the project. Thereafter, to create a data inventory, data collection and fish residue
analysis was performed in order to obtain sufficient and up-to-date information on: fish distribution; filleting
percentages; identification of conventional products in the country; demand of potential products from fish
residue treatment, and waste management in Somaliland; fish residue composition, generation, projection
and mass-flows. The data collection was performed through literature review, local data collection with
surveys of local stakeholders, pictures and videos of Somaliland relevant environment and communication
with Copenhagen and Somali based FairFishing staff. In a technical review, seven possible management
options to treat fish residues were identified, described and relevant aspects as e.g. production yields were
calculated based on literature values. The seven identified treatment options were compared and ranked
through a comprehensive ranking system, developed by the authors of the thesis, in order to select the
two suitable options for treatment of fish residue generated by partners in Somaliland. The technologies
iii
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:5==
were ranked on aspects of Technical challenges, Capital and maintenance costs, Operation cost, Revenue,
Sustainability and Demand. Once two options were chosen (a feed and an energy option), a ”proof-of-
concept” functional unit was formed. The functional unit was based on the data inventory that indicated
where largest fractions of easy accessible fish residues may be generated within a short distance. A scenario
for each option was formed to treat the projected residues and the scenario design and setup was specified
to the given situation and functional unit. With a financial cost-benefit analysis, the two scenarios were
assessed for feasibility by determining each option’s net present value (NPV). A sensitivity and a data
quality analysis was used to identify critical parameters (parameters that are both sensitive and of poor
quality) in the scenarios. The critical parameters were, in a risk analysis, propagated in a Monte Carlo
simulation to determine the scenarios’ risk of generating a negative and unfavourable NPV. Based on the
economic assessment, recommendations were given to FairFishing to support decision making and lay the
foundation to initiate their ”waste-to-resource”-project.
Important results and findings
The study found that fish residues in Somaliland originate predominantly from tuna. A daily average of 470
kg and 170 kg of fish residues were projected to be generated in 2021 from Berbera station and market,
respectively.
Seven technologies for fish residue treatment were identified and compared in the screening assessment:
current management (landfilling/dumping); fish meal production; fish silage production; black soldier fly
larvae production; biogas production; biochar/charcoal production; and compost production. Hereof dried
fish silage and biogas production from fish residues in Somaliland were found most suitable based on the
ranking system: The fish silage technology offers a simple option to produce fish silage that can be used
as animal feed. Fish silage production may meet the occasionally high demand for animal feed by local
livestock farmers during droughts. However, the disadvantage of fish silage production is that the demand
is uncertain outside drought periods and that it requires a fresh input of fish residues, which implies an
efficient collection system. On the other hand, the biogas technology is able to treat not only fish residues,
but also other organic waste streams (e.g. wastewater, food waste from the market, etc.) and can be
used as energy source internally by FairFishing. However, biogas production through anaerobic digestion
is a relatively complex technology, has a high water demand, and requires skilled labour and import of
equipment in the given scale. Both options are more sustainable than the current management of fish
residues in Somaliland.
The functional unit for scenario formation and later techno-economic assessment was set to be ”treatment
of 640 kg fish residues in the year 2021 from Berbera station and market, with a projected increase from
year 2021-2030”. A scenario for each technology was formed: In the Fish silage scenario, the fish residues
were converted to dried fish silage and it was assumed all to be sold to farmers; in the Biogas scenario, the
fish resides were converted to biogas by co-digestion of fish residues with wastewater and organic wastes.
The biogas was here estimated to mainly be used internally by FairFishing for energy production instead
of diesel.
The financial cost-benefit results of the two scenarios (Figure 1a and Figure 1b) showed that the Fish
silage scenario has lower capital and start-up costs and higher running benefits than the biogas scenario.
The biogas scenario has lower operation costs, however the net cash flows are substantially lower than in
the Fish silage scenario. The Fish silage scenario had a considerable higher NPV compared to the Biogas
scenario (170,000 USD > 700 USD) and its revenues would pay back the capital investment within the
first project year in contrast to the Biogas scenario’s payback period of 10 years. The biogas scenario, with
an NPV of 700 USD after 10 years project lifetime, is not found convincingly viable. For both scenarios
different options exist to improve the feasibility. An option to increase NPV substantially for both scenarios
is by addition of a waste collection fee. Different additional scenario changes were identified to increase the
NPV of the Biogas scenario, that otherwise was close to zero. Possible options are to fund all capital and
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
iv
==PAGE:6==
start-up costs or for the Biogas scenario to purchase used and local generators. If all capital and start-up
costs were funded, the Fish silage scenario was still found to be the most viable option (170,000 USD >
35,000 USD), with a factor 4.7 difference to the biogas scenario.
(a)
(b)
Figure 1: Cumulative annual present value (APV), and annual present distribution of costs and benefits for the Fish
silage scenario (a) and the Biogas scenario (b) without funding. The net present value is marked with a star on the
cumulative APV curve.
Several critical parameters (sensitive and rely on poor quality data) were detected for both scenarios
based on the sensitivity and data quality analysis. A following risk analysis showed that when including a
quantitative uncertainty of the critical parameters, the Fish silage scenario had a higher NPV deviation,
due to higher uncertainties of critical parameters, but with a chance (app. 90 %) of producing a NPV
higher than the most likely NPV of the Biogas scenario. The major causes for the high uncertainty of the
Fish silage scenario’s economic results relied on the uncertainty of the production volume, demand and
selling price of dried fish silage. All may fluctuate with season, draught occurrences, economic situation etc.
Biogas, on the other hand, to a large degree depends on the biogas potential, diesel price, and parameters
affecting the digester size. Labour need and fish residue input were critical parameters for both scenarios.
As the Fish silage scenario generated the largest NPV in the performed study, it is found to be the solution
that may benefit FairFishing partners most economically, besides supporting farmers and agriculture in the
region.
v
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:7==
Conclusion & recommendations
Two options are found most suitable for fish residue utilisation in Somaliland for FairFishing’s ”Proof-of-
concept”-solution; biogas and fish silage production. The Fish silage solution generates a substantially
larger revenue, indicated as a higher NPV, but with higher uncertainties compared the Biogas solution.
This is to a large degree caused by the uncertain demand of animal feed. It is concluded, that fish silage
production from Somaliland fish residues may offer the most economically favoured option for improving
working and living conditions for FairFishing partners. Biogas, however, satisfies FairFishing’s wishes of
internal use.
Based on the project’s findings, before implementing any fish residues management options, it is recom-
mended to:
• further investigate fish mass-flows (waste generation) by sample collection over a longer period;
• to experimentally analyse fish residue composition in order to determine specific biogas reactor design
and fish silage substitution potentials;
• to either gather more information on the local animal feed demand (for fish silage) or on the quantities
of possible organic waste streams and wastewater (for biogas).
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland

REFACE
The following paper about treating fish residues in Somaliland with the title ”A techno-economic assessment
of a waste management system for fish residues in Somaliland”, is conducted and written by Silje Kærn
Berggreen and Sidsel Grabow Olesen. The paper is the result of a master thesis done with supervision by
Alessio Boldrin, Associate Professor at DTU Environment, and co-supervision by Susanna Andreasi Bassi,
PhD student at DTU Environment, at the Department of Environmental Engineering at DTU. The thesis
was written in the spring semester of 2020 and done in collaboration with FairFishing, an international
non-governmental organisation working to create jobs, economic development and regional stability by
forming a sustainable fishery industry and value-chain in Somalia.
We, the authors, would like to express our gratitude to FairFishing for teaming up with us on the ”waste-to-
resource” project, to FairFishing’s project manager Carolina Obara for defining FairFishing’s project goals,
to FairFishing’s Somalia based Yusuf Gulled, Yasmin Nur and Abdrikariim Ali for their help in collecting
local data, and to Alessio Boldrin and Susanna Andreasi Bassi for their thorough guidance throughout the
project.
The intended audience include academic experts, FairFishing and local stakeholders. The reader is assumed
to have a basic knowledge in the area of waste management in low-middle income countries.
Silje Kærn Berggreen (s154156) and Sidsel Grabow Olesen (s153974)

A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
xiv
==PAGE:16==
1
INTRODUCTION
Somaliland, a poor state in the horn of Africa, faces amongst other, food scarcity, severe droughts and
high unemployment rates. As a consequence of more urgent needs, this has resulted in insufficient waste
management. Today’s waste treatment in Somaliland is of poor quality where all waste, both organic
and inorganic, ends up at local dumpsites and landfills and thereby valuable resources are wasted. By
utilising wastes in more efficient manners there are possible economic and environmental benefits to gain.
Furthermore, the local community that is already facing multiple challenges may not be aware of the value
that is lost when e.g. their organic residues end at the landfill. To further understand these issues, data
and information from the region is needed, however up-to-date academic literature from Somalia is scarce,
yet alone on how their organic waste fractions are treated.
FairFishing is a Danish-Somali non governmental organisation (NGO) that works towards expanding fisheries
in the horn of Africa to improve working and living conditions for their partners, fisher and monger.
FairFishing has a vision to expand their activities, but one obstacle hereof is the availability of sufficient
cooling and cold storage for their partners fish catch. FairFishing has therefore initiated the ”waste-to-
resource”-project where they hope to solve some of these problems for their partners by utilising fish residues
to generate revenue. In these manners, they want to help dispose the locally generated fish residues in
more sustainable manners rather than wasting it at local landfills.
Fish residues can be utilised in different manners to create value, e.g. through conversion to animal feeds
in the form of fish meal as shown by Ponnusamy et al. [98], fish silage as investigated by Rurangwa et al.
[106], or larvae as studied by Nguyen et al. [84]; to energy in the form of biogas as shown by Heeb [60];
and to fertiliser in the form of compost presented by López-Mosquera et al. [75]. For conversion to cooking
fuel, charcoal production is an additional considered fish residue treatment option that has been shown
possible from animal carcasses, as displayed by Ma and Matsunaka [78].
FairFishing’s vision is to utilise all fish residues generated from fish caught by their partners all over Somalia.
However, to proof the viability of such a project, FairFishing as a first step implementation wants a solution
that converts fish residues in a ”proof-of-concept”-scale, here defined as a small centralised solution.
This study aims at presenting a local ”proof-of-concept” solution to treat and convert fish residues into
valuable products. By producing revenue for FairFishing from fish residue treatment, their surplus can help
facilitate their vision of expanding fisheries and improved livelihood and quality of life for their partners in
Somalia. Furthermore, this project offers support for FairFishing to make a decision on a final option for
later implementation. The main objectives for the present project are therefore to:
1. create a data inventory about fish residues, current waste management and demand of potential
products in a local present context through surveys of relevant stakeholders and communication
with FairFishing staff;
2. screen for suitable fish residue treatment options through a technology review and then select two
options for further assessment, through a self-produced and comprehensive ranking system;
3. form a detailed ”proof-of-concept” functional unit and scenario with a final setup based on a com-
bination of results from the data inventory and technology screening;
4. to assess economic viability of two suitable options through a full techno-economic assessment;
5. present and inform FairFishing about possible options and economic benefits through reporting,
recommendations and presentation.
After presentation of the project results, FairFishing’s next step is to act on the recommendations and
results to decide on a final treatment option.
1
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:17==
2
BACKGROUND
Somaliland is the focus of the report and background information is provided for the specific region. As
Somaliland is a state in Somalia, certain information is presented for Somalia as well. In certain topics
there is limited data available for Somaliland, and in those cases, data is only presented for Somalia.
2.1
Somalia and Somaliland
2.1.1
Geography and climate
Somalia is located in the horn of Africa, has an area of 637,657 km2 (almost 15 times the size of Denmark)
[27] and a 3,025 km coastline [63] through the Gulf of Aden and the Indian Ocean. The coast surrounds the
country from the north western part to the most southern point [54]. As seen in Figure 2.1, the country
borders Djibouti in the north western part, Ethiopia to the west, and Kenya in the southern part [54].
Somalia is split into three regions: Somaliland in the north western part, Puntland to the north east, and
Central Somalia in the south [63].
Figure 2.1: Geographical placement of Somalia and the states within
Somaliland is a former British protectorate from whom they gained full independence from in June 1960.
In July 1960, Somaliland voluntarily joined a union with the rest of the Somali speaking people, to form the
republic of Somalia. After years of civil war with Somalia, Somaliland withdrew in 1991 and self-declared
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
2
==PAGE:18==
BACKGROUND
its independence, an independence that is yet not recognised by Somalia and the international community
[55, 73]. Somaliland is a democratic state with a multiparty system, and the people of Somaliland has
taken part in inoffensive elections on both national and local levels [55]. The region is mainly inhabited by
the Isaaq clan, one of six large clans distributed over the Horn of Africa. It is relatively poor with about
half of the population being semi-nomadic or nomadic, and the other half being distributed between urban
and rural areas.
Somalia’s population in 2020 is estimated by United Nations [135] to be 15.9 million persons, with an
annual growth rate of 2.83 %. Somaliland’s population was in 2018 estimated to 5.0 million [99]. Anno
2017, around one million people lived in Hargeisa, the capital city of Somaliland [55].
The climate in Somalia is tropical to sub-tropical and arid to semi-arid [63]. With equator crossing the
southern part of Somalia, temperatures in the southern parts are relatively stable all year round with average
day and night temperatures around 30 and 25 ◦C respectively over the year. Temperatures at the north
cost of Somalia (Somaliland and Puntland) are similar to the southern parts most of the year. However,
average day temperatures in the summer months reaches 40 ◦C. The mountainous areas in the north
(i.e. Hargeisa, Somaliland), on the other hand, have temperatures below the average Somali temperatures:
25 ◦C in the winter months and 30 ◦C in the summer months, with night average temperatures in the
winter months down to 12 ◦C [29, 30]. The relative humidity varies with geographic location and with
seasonal changes. In the areas near the northern Somalia coastline it may be as high as 65-70 % whereas
the lowest relative humidity values in Somalia are found in Western Somalia (down to 40 %) [83]. Winds
from the Arabian Peninsula, called Kharif, blows over the northern regions from June to September with
very dry and strong winds, that keeps the air moisture content down in those periods [83]. Precipitation
is relatively scarce, with least rainfall along the coast and in the north eastern regions (50-150 mm per
year), and most in the mountainous highlands to the north (500 mm per year), divided mostly between
two rainfall seasons; Gu and Deyr. Gu starts in late March and intensifies in April and May. Deyr, which is
shorter and less intensive than Gu, starts in October and ends in late November [63, 150]. Annual rainfall
varies from year to year and precipitation often occur as short, heavy rainfall and thunderstorms. Somalia’s
climate is influenced by the El Niño Southern Oscillation and as a consequence, Somalia experiences cycles
of severe flooding and droughts. El Niño years brings more rainfall and flooding, while La Niña years brings
more droughts [150]. The last couple of years, severe flooding and droughts have been more frequent,
which have had a big impact on agriculture’s livestock and crop production, the biggest economic sectors
in Somalia [46].
2.1.2
Economy
Somalia is one of the poorest countries in the world: In 2018, Somalia’s GDP per capita was 310 USD,
slowly growing from 300 USD in 2013 [147]. In 2019, about 69 % of Somalia’s population lived on less
than 1.9 USD per day, with the rural population being poorer than the urban population [148]. According
to World Bank [146], in 2013, a household in Somaliland needed approximately 1.0 USD per adult in urban
areas and 0.90 USD per adult in rural areas, to cover basic needs. Households with less money than this
are considered poor, resulting in poverty rates of 30 % of the urban population and 37 % of the rural
population in Somaliland in 2013. As for Somaliland, the rural population in Somalia is poorer than the
urban. In 2013, the unemployment rates amongst 15 to 55 year olds in Somaliland were 67 % and 74 %
in urban and rural areas, respectively [146].
The agricultural sector employs 46 % of the working population in Somalia [46]. It consists of four sub-
sectors: livestock, crop, fishery and forestry. The sub-sector livestock and crops employs most people and
are the most important sources of economic activity and export. Agricultural export grows and counts for
about 75 % of Somalia’s GDP, mainly due to livestock. In Somaliland livestock is also the largest economic
sector with large export rates, counting for 65 % of the GDP. Livestock animals are mainly cattle, camel,
goat and sheep, predominantly grazing in the Haud region near the border to Ethiopia [55].
3
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:19==
BACKGROUND
Somalia is highly dependent on food import from other countries, and due to both a massive population
increase and a drastic decrease in crop production, import rates are increasing. Local crop production only
counts for 22 % of the domestic needs [46] and is limited mostly to areas around the Shabelle and Jubba
rivers (see Figure 2.1, where the greatest irrigation potential is found [63]. The majority of food crops
produced in Somalia is maize, sorghum, millet and rice [63]. One of two areas in Somalia with high crop
cultivation potentials is located in Somaliland, west of Hargeisa [44]. Crop production in Somaliland is
dominated by the dry-land farmed crops maize and sorghum, mainly cultivated in the highlands of Hargeisa.
The marine environment along the long coastline of Somalia, is rich due to nutrient up-welling, thus fisheries
holds a promising potential. As for Somalia, Somaliland with its 850 km coastline, has a big potential for
fisheries and in 2016 an agreement was made to expand the port of Berbera [55]. Regardless of the big
potential, Somalia does not have a fishing tradition and the country’s fish consumption is one of the lowest
in Africa [63]. Even if the fishery sector shows an increasing trend [63], it employs only 4 % of the working
population [46]. The sector is in general characterised by limited knowledge due to Somalia’s waters being
uncontrolled and unsupervised. However, annual potentials have been guesstimated to around 835.000
tonnes fish. With estimates of current on- and off-shore fishing levels, only one-sixth to one-fourth of the
annual potentials are exploited, making room for a growing on-shore fishery sector [46]. Because off-shore
fisheries are subject to a lot of illegal and unregulated fishery, Somalia works to establish sustainable and
more locally owned artisanal fisheries [63].
2.1.3
Energy
Access to electricity is limited in Somalia, where only 16 % of the population uses electricity [100]. The
energy consumption per capita is amongst the lowest in Africa [151]. Electricity is mainly produced by
diesel driven generators, and limited data is available on the amount of electricity produced from renewable
sources as biofuels and waste, solar, hydro and wind energy [5].
Energy for cooking is mainly based on burning of charcoal (in urban areas) and firewood (in rural areas)
[3, 136]. As a consequence Somalia has faced severe deforestation rates. Deforestation rates have however
been lowered substantially since 2012, due to increasing environmental awareness as well as charcoal bans
by federal and regional governments, and by introducing gas stoves for cooking [46].
The fossil fuels; oil (diesel), charcoal and firewood all together covers close to the whole energy consumption
in the country [5]. At the moment, there is, as a consequence of deforestation and current electricity
situation with e.g. high electricity prices, a higher need for alternative fuel sources. Some suggested ideas
of alternative energy sources are biofuels from animal waste, marine biomass and crops [136].
2.2
FairFishing
FairFishing is a non-profit, NGO founded in Denmark in 2011. It is a Danish-Somali organisation with
offices in both Copenhagen and Berbera and its main activities in Somalia. It was born from a dream by
Jacob Johannsen of turning pirates in Somalia into fishers by providing them an alternative opportunity to
create income. The idea and aim of FairFishing’s activities is to establish a sustainable industry and value
chain of fisheries that can generate local jobs, foster economic development and create regional stability
in the country. In this way FairFishing works on reducing poverty and create opportunities for the local
community [41, 86, 132]. The last couple of years, Somalia has experienced severe droughts which have
led to food insecurity in the country [46]. An expansion of the fishery sector thus also has the possibility
to improve food and nutrition security. FairFishing collaborates with their local partners; individuals who
are often boat- and car owners, who hire fishers and are responsible for the logistics and administration
related to fishery and distribution of fish, or who themselves are single fishers and mongers. Through
communication with local FairFishing staff, it has been estimated that there are approximately 50 partners
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
4
==PAGE:20==
BACKGROUND
and more than 450 fishers [57]. A key point in FairFishing’s activities is the ”Station” in Berbera, yet other
FairFishing stations along the cost exists. Services at the Station in Berbera, available for FairFishing’s
partners, include affordable ice, cooling, storage, cleaning and weighting of the fish. Another important
part of FairFishing activities, is a Partners Supply Unit, where registered partners can buy cheap fishing
gear, nets, and other fishing equipment and materials at cost price [86]. From November 2019, the Ministry
of Livestock and Fisheries Development has been in charge of the station and Partner Supply Unit that
used to be run by FairFishing. FairFishing today still works towards expanding fisheries in Berbera and the
rest of Somaliland, now in collaboration with the ministry.
All fish caught by the partners is delivered at the station, where the fish is prepared and stored, after which
it is sold at local markets in Berbera or by car distributed to markets and restaurants in Hargeisa, Burco,
Gabiley, Wajaale and to a small degree other small towns. Other important objectives of FairFishing are to
increase awareness about; how to handle fish (i.e. securing fresh fish from sea to plate), the health benefits
associated with eating fish, and to change the cultural perception around eating fish that is traditionally
considered a food only for poor man.
5
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:21==
3
MATERIALS & METHODS
The techno-economic assessment of a waste management system to treat Somaliland fish residues consisted
both of qualitative and quantitative approaches. This allowed a combination of both numerical analysis and
in-depth discussions. A fieldtrip to Somaliland and experimental analysis of fish residues from Berbera were
scheduled for April 2020 but due to the Covid-19 situation that globally unfolded during spring-summer
2020, the trip was cancelled and alternative data collection was arranged.
As the project affects multiple stakeholders and rely on them for successful future implementation, they
were identified and analysed based on literature review and communication with FairFishing. An objective
was to obtain data on filleting percentage, conventional products in the country, the local demand on
specific products and to gain information on the current waste and fish residue management in the area.
This information was obtained as part of the data inventory collection and further analysed to obtain fish
residue composition, generation, projections and mass-flows of fish residues in Somaliland. Thereafter, a
screening process of possible technologies for fish residue management followed with an assumed daily fish
residue generation and input of one tonne. The screening process included a review section, and a ranking
and selection section. For technology review, general information on technologies was found, and rough
estimations on design, processing time, yield, substitution potentials, facility size, labour need etc were
made. A ranking system was used as support-tool for decision making on selecting two final technologies.
The selection limit of two options was set due to a wish of having in-depth results with the limited time
given for a master thesis project rather than uncertain results of more options. It was later in the process
decided to choose one feed and one energy option for the economic assessment. The wish of one feed
and one energy option was made to show the span of options. Based on the information gathered in the
data inventory, a final functional unit was formed to illustrate a ”proof-of-concept” scale solution. For
each option, a scenario was set up by using the newly formed functional unit and information gathered
from the review of the suggested technologies. The two technologies were further assessed and specified
more deeply for e.g. optimal setup and system boundaries. The system boundaries and hence production
scales were made in accordance with FairFishing’s wishes for a ”proof-of-concept” solution, meaning a
first step implementation of a small scale solution, to proof the viability of such project. A maximum
of 50,000 USD was estimated by Carolina Obara [88] to be the funding limit for the proof-of-concept
solution. New information found at this point was not iterated back in the previous screening process, as
illustrated in the information flow of Figure 3.1, but differences were instead discussed. In the following
techno-economic assessment, a cost-benefit analysis was made to obtain information on the scenarios’
viability, where improvements are needed to make the result more robust, and to make an estimation on
the certainty of the results. Based on the economic assessment, recommendations were given to FairFishing
to initiate their ”waste-to-resource”-project. Figure 3.1 displays the aim and methods used in the project,
along with the information flow. In the following, the methodology for each section is further explained
along important initial assumptions. As large efforts were made to plan a field trip for data collection and
determination of experimental methodology for fish residue composition, the final section explains the work
that was done but not used for the project due to the outbreak of the Covid-19 pandemic.
All calculated numbers reported have been rounded to two significant digits. In some cases this implies
that values does not add up. Numbers from literature have been reported as original found, however when
presented in tables and figures rounded to two significant digits.

3.1
Stakeholder assessment
In accordance with L. Smith [116], the stakeholder assessment included a stakeholder identification and
a quantification of their interest and influence on the project.
The stakeholder assessment was done
by communication with FairFishing’s staff and by own judgement. Both stakeholders currently involved
and stakeholders that could potentially be involved later in the process, were identified, described and
mapped in an interest-influence matrix. As the political situation in Somaliland, and who therefore affect
FairFishing projects, change rapidly with shifting balance of power, stakeholder assessment was essential but
complicated. Malin Schmiedel [112], Copenhagen based FairFishing Program Manager, was consolidated
to confirm the findings.

MATERIALS & METHODS
3.2
Data inventory
The aim of the data inventory was to gain knowledge on following in Somaliland:
• Fish distribution,
• fish filleting percentages,
• conventional products to potentially be substituted,
• demand of potential fish residue based products,
• current municipal solid waste management,
• current fish residue management,
• other organic waste streams of relevance,
• fish residue composition, generation, projection and mass-flow.
The data inventory was split in two: Data collection and Fish residue analysis.
The methods to perform data collection were through FairFishing communication, literature review and local
data collection. How these were performed, are further elaborated in the following sections. Fish residue
composition, generation, projection and mass-flow in Somaliland were extracted from this information
through further fish residue analysis, explained in following sections.
3.2.1
Data collection
FairFishing communication
Ongoing communication was established with FairFishing, as they are the NGO managing the project in
Somaliland. Obara [88], Copenhagen based FairFishing Project Manager, was the main contact, and helped
with the overview of project context, organising data collection in Somaliland and she answered all questions
related to FairFishing, or, if needed, forwarded to the right person. FairFishing’s Regional Program Manager
in Berbera, Yusuf Abdilahi Gulled [57] and Local Public Awareness and Market Development Lead based in
Hargeisa, Yasmin Nur [87], answered questions that required local knowledge. Gulled [57] was the expert on
Berbera office and station affairs, and therefore provided information on operation and costs in Berbera. An
interview meeting with Gulled [57] was arranged to obtain data relevant for a description of the local context,
along with relevant and up-to-date values for the economic assessment. Nur [87] additionally performed the
farmer surveys in Hargeisa. Adbirakiim Ali, Berbera based student, was hired to conduct the data collection
in Somaliland and helped providing local knowledge and context. Mohammad Ashour [16], station manager
in Berbera, contributed with confirmation of some of the local findings. Malin Schmiedel [112], Copenhagen
based FairFishing Program Manager, aided with the political and organisational aspects and stakeholder
questions in the project. Maiken Woller [145], Copenhagen based Fundraising & Communication Officer,
provided information regarding funding options and aided in promotion of the project. Pablo Goitia, a
Roskilde University graduate who collaborated with FairFishing on his master thesis, helped and provided
advise on how to conduct the field trip and data collection in Somaliland, as he himself previously had been
through the same process as part of his thesis [53].
Literature review
Throughout the project, extensive literature research was performed. As the field trip to Somaliland was
cancelled, literature review was used as one of the alternatives, however literature and academic articles from
the specific region were scarce, and therefore supplemented by the survey and FairFishing communication.
The dominant search tool applied during the literature review was the university provided www.findit.
dtu.dk. To a large extent, www.google.com and www.youtube.com were used to display concrete and novel
stories and business cases. Online articles, books and reports of newer dates (2015-) and geographical
locations close to Somalia were preferred, however often scarce, therefore the searches were often expanded
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
8
==PAGE:24==
MATERIALS & METHODS
to include East Africa or low-middle income countries and older articles. Academic articles were preferably
used, however, as these were scarce in the region, news article as well as company and organisation websites,
academic exports and companies were used to support assumptions.
Local data collection
In order to obtain novel data, realistic values, estimates to reduce uncertainty related to the project, and to
cover areas that could not be found in literature, some data had to be collected directly from the source;
Somaliland. Due to the Covid-19 situation, a planned field trip was cancelled, and a local Somalilander,
Abdikariim Ali [8], was hired by FairFishing to conduct the surveys and to take pictures that were originally
planned to be performed by the Danish authors themselves. The data collection in Somaliland included:
surveys of different stakeholders in Berbera and Hargeisa, photos and videos of the local environment,
including their current waste treatment and local landfills, measuring and weighing fish residues for density
determination, determination of conventional products and animal feeds sold on markets, and identification
of industries producing waste streams that could potentially be included in the waste treatment system.
A thorough description and time plan for nine days work was produced and provided to Ali [8], and
communication and feedback was scheduled after every day’s work. If data was not obtainable by Ali [8], it
has not been presented later in the data inventory section. Nur [87] was in charge of an additional survey
made for the farmers outside Hargeisa. In Appendix A.1.2 and Appendix A.1.3, the provided data collection
guides can be found.
The surveys were intended as a quantitative method to collect broad data on e.g. filleting percentages,
geographical waste generation points for the mass-flow analysis, prices on current management and conven-
tional products, demands for potential products and thoughts on current products that may be substituted.
The different stakeholders were asked different questions, and the range of questions varied from 4-15
dependent on stakeholder type. Questions were kept short and simple, to be easy understandable. In order
to ensure minimum misunderstandings of the questions, all surveys were read through by Obara [88] and
Woller [145] before initiating the data collection. The questions in the survey were orally translated to
Arabic when conducting the face to face interviews, where Ali [8] and Nur [87] would travel around to find
the respective stakeholders in Berbera and Hargeisa. A number of 4 to 12 stakeholders within each category
were interviewed, but due to limited time and availability of nearby stakeholders, no further specifications
of e.g age were set up. A number of 64 stakeholders were surveyed in total. See Appendix A, Table A.1 for
number of stakeholders surveyed in each stakeholder group. The limited number of surveyed stakeholders
in each category were also due to time constrains. As the sample sizes were small and insufficient for sta-
tistical analysis, the data therefore functioned as best estimates. Only information from the cities Berbera
and Hargeisa was collected, since these are the main hubs in Somaliland for FairFishing activities and as
the majority if the project stakeholders lived in these cities. The surveys were originally the same for each
stakeholder type independent of city, meaning that e.g. all fish mongers in both cities would be asked
the same questions. As there was scheduled a daily feedback, this made it possible to continuously assess
the quality of the data and thereby adding or removing questions of new or no relevance, and therefore
stakeholders of same type, e.g. fish mongers, ended up having a different number of questions in the two
cities surveyed. The original surveys are attached in Appendix A.1.3. Occasionally, the interviewee missed
some questions or translated it differently, which affected the data quality of the surveys. Additionally,
some results were reported back in unclear manners and were therefore, in the report, interpreted to best
ability according to context. As information was collected through secondary sources and not the authors
themselves, there was a risk of varying quality of collected data since information could have been lost
when shared.
The pictures and videos taken were intended to provide qualitative insights into the local environment, and
functioned as support and verification of data, e.g. did the pictures and videos show what the quantitative
data or literature was telling. Furthermore, the pictures and videos were to function as the author’s ”eyes”
and describe the area, the living standards, the cleanness of the streets etc. In this way the pictures and

videos helped the authors asses how suitable the suggested technologies may be in the given community
without the authors observing it first-hand themselves. It was specifically asked to provide pictures and

trucks.

3.2.2
Fish residue analysis
Fish residue composition
As it was not possible to go down to the FairFishing station in Somaliland and sample the exact residues
generated, literature values of waste compositions originating from 100 % tuna species, in alignment with
the findings from Goitia [53], were used. The fish residues’ composition: fish body parts (tuna by-products,
meat and skin) and macro-nutrient content (fat, protein and carbohydrate), was estimated by combining
several studies with estimates of what components there are present in the waste, and how much of each.
The chemical composition of fish residue had to be estimated due to the lack of specific information in
literature. The chemical composition of the fish residues was composed based on the previously determined
macro-nutrient composition of the fish residues and known chemical formulas of these macro nutrients.
For chemical formulas see Appendix A.1.4.
The fish residue density was determined experimentally on the actual fish residues generated at the station
in Berbera by Ali [8], who performed data collection in Somaliland. He was instructed to measure diameter
of a bucket, and then to weight it with and without fish residues. The density was calculated from the
measurements.
Fish residue generation
To estimate fish residue quantities in the system, data sets of fish caught and delivered to the station in
Berbera from 2015 to 2019 was combined with a total waste estimate of the fish. The data sets on fish
caught and delivered to the station in Berbera was provided by FairFishing, and relevant data have been
collected in Appendix C.3. The 2015 and 2016 data sets were only half and these data sets were hence
partially estimated. The average monthly waste quantity for the existing data was used as an estimate for
the rest of the months. From end November 2019, the Ministry of Livestock and Fisheries Development
became in charge of Berbera station. No data was therefore available for the last 19 days of November,
for December and onwards. In order to estimate fish quantities for a full November month, the given
November data was multiplied with 2.7 to obtain a full month. December was estimated in the same way
as the missing data for 2015 and 2016.
Fish residue projections
A prediction of the coming years fish and residue quantity in the system was estimated with a logarithmic
regression of the data from 2017 to 2019 (Appendix C.3). As the 2015 and 2016 data was partially
estimated, it was decided not to include these years in the regression.
A two-sided Spearman’s Rank
Correlation was used to determine if the regression made was significantly correlated to data values, by
comparing r with r critical [102].
Fish residue mass-flows
Mass-flows in the system at different locations were estimated based on: data on fish quantities staying
in Berbera and the quantities of fish distributed out of Berbera (provided by FairFishing, see Appendix
C.3); a distribution estimate for rest of Somaliland made by Gulled [57]; and residue estimate for different
locations in the system based on estimated filleting percentages obtained by stakeholder surveys.

3.3
Technology screening
A screening process, that included technology review, and ranking and selection of suitable technologies,
followed. Here the results of the technology review of the suggested technologies formed the basis for a
comprehensive ranking system. From the ranking system two technologies were selected for further analysis.
The screened and suggested technologies were:
• Current management
• Fish meal production
• Fish silage production
• Black soldier fly larvae production
• Biogas production
• Biochar/Charcoal production
• Compost production
Current management is only included for comparison reasons. If will not be further assessed after the
screening section. The following sections elaborate on methods for the technology review, and ranking and
selection.
3.3.1
Technology review
The found technologies were suggested for FairFishing, and a technical review for treatment of one tonne
fish residue input was made. The review boundary for what was assessed in the technical review is provided
in Figure 3.2.
Figure 3.2: The technology review boundaries applied for assessment of different fish residue treatment options
The review included general description of the technology and rough application estimates of: design
and processing time; yield and substitution potential, and facility size and labour need. A quantity of
one tonne fish residue was used on application estimates for comparison reasons before real fish
mass-flow values were in fact estimated and a ”proof-of-concept” scenario formed.
11
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:27==
MATERIALS & METHODS
General description of technologies
A general description of each suggested technology was sought and was mainly produced through literature
review, see section 3.2.1.
Industries using similar technologies or selling the suggested technologies were contacted for first-hand
elaboration on specific technical aspects and experiences. This included information on fish meal production
by FF Skagen; a biochar solution from MASH Energy; The Danish Aquaculture Organisation, and Marine
Ingredients Denmark for information on common fish residues’ treatment procedures.
Experts within different fields were additionally contacted, if needed, to obtain up-to-date information,
practical aspects, issues related to the technology or to elaborate on their articles or their findings. All
communication was either by meetings, through e-mail or Microsoft Office Teams meetings.
Applied Technology
Design specification and processing time
A design for each technology was suggested based on what was found possible from literature. Estimations
on how long processing time to produce final products from one tonne daily fish residue input, was similarly
investigated through literature review.
Yield estimations and substitution potentials
Yield estimations were either calculated or estimated based on literature review. The amounts of other
needed inputs, e.g. other wastes or chemicals were calculated as part of yield estimations. For the feed
options; fish meal, fish silage and dried black soldier fly larvae, their nutritional and feeding potentials were
additionally given. The nutritional potentials were calculated as amount of digestible crude energy, fat,
carbohydrates and protein in the feed. Feeding potentials tell how many tropical livestock units (TLU),
equivalent to number of camels, the options could feed (TLUF) based on the livestock’s daily protein
or energy requirements. The calculations performed for nutritional and feeding potentials are given in
Appendix A.2.1. As biogas yield is expressed as amount of methane produced, and since this parameter
highly depends on chemical composition of the input, the biogas theoretical yield was based on theoretical
biochemical methane potentials (TBMP), given in L CH4/g volatile solids (VS), of the input. Equations
herefore given in Appendix A.2.1.
Fertilisers are applied on soils for nitrogen (N), phosphor (P) and
potassium (K) addition. Fertiliser potentials were calculated for technology suggestion outputs that can
be applied on soils. Their fertiliser potential was calculated as N:P:K weight percentage
The primary products from the suggested technologies include animal fodder (fish meal, fish silage and
black soldier fly larvae), fuels (biogas, biochar) and fertiliser (compost). The amounts they can replace
of conventional products: Rhodes grass hay (animal feed), diesel and conventional charcoal (fuels), and
ADNOC UREA (nitrogen fertiliser), were calculated. The equations for how substitution potentials were
determined is given in Appendix A.2.2.
Facility size and labour need
Facilities of the suggested options in the screening section were all assumed to include an area for waste
collection vehicles, waste delivering, storage etc. and an office area including toilet facilities etc. This area
per one tonne daily fish residue input was set to be 25 m2 and 30 m2, respectively, based on qualified
estimates from Dortmans et al. [34]. In addition, each technology would have an operation area. The
specific operation area for each technology was based on literature review, communication with experts,
and, if needed, qualified estimates.
The facility size of biogas production depends on the size of its
digester, which was calculated. Elaboration on how those calculations were performed are provided in
Appendix A.2.3.
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
12
==PAGE:28==
MATERIALS & METHODS
When possible, labour needs were estimated based on literature review. However, due to scarce studies on
labour need of the suggested technologies, they were predominantly estimated based on expected physical
work, related to operation of the technology.
3.3.2
Technology ranking and selection
The seven suggested treatment options for management of Somaliland fish residues, together with the
current management, were ranked on a self-produced ranking tool for decision making support, in different
categories based on the results obtained in the technology review section with a reference value of one
tonne daily fish residue input. The different categories were chosen, as they show different aspects to
consider when evaluating the suitability of the different waste technologies. The categories were divided
into themes: Technical challenges, Capital and maintenance costs, Operation cost, Revenue, Sustainability,
and Demand. Within each category, the technologies were ranked on a scale from 1 to 5, where 1 is best
and 5 is worst. A short description of each category is presented in the following. Keep in mind that the
themes Capital and maintenance costs, Operation cost and Revenues are graded, based on who will bear
the costs or receive the revenue, in this case FairFishing.
Technical challenges
• Climate sensitivity: It assesses whether there can be potential operation problems due to the climate
conditions and also future climate change.
• Experience an interest in East Africa: It assesses if the technology is already found in Somaliland or
in similar geographical contexts.
• Need of co-collection: Some technologies are not suitable to handle fish as the only input. For those
facilities other organic waste streams will need to be found, accessed and collected.
• Nuisance from facility: Problems associated with: odours, vectors, aesthetics.
• Technology level: The level of technical expertise to operate the facility. Some of the technologies
are more complicated than others, which means a different level of knowledge is needed.
• Transportability of output: Some of the technology products are easier to transport. For those that
cannot be transported long distances, potential future export is difficult.
Capital and maintenance costs
• Construction, material and maintenance costs: Accessibility and type of materials, and levels of
knowledge for construction of the facilities, are both associated with costs. If materials are not
accessible in the country, these will need to be imported and most likely be associated with higher
costs. If foreign technicians are needed, this will also be associated with higher costs than if it can be
handled by the local community. Maintenance costs are assumed to be a set fraction of construction
and material costs.
Operation costs
• Labour needs: The technologies need different amounts of labour. This will affect the operation
costs, but at the same time create job opportunities.
• Need of continuous fuel input: Some technologies has a continuous fuel input. This are associated
with ongoing costs as well as the logistics of getting the fuel.
• Need of continuous other inputs: If other inputs ancillary materials are needed, that are not fuel,
water or wastes, this will also create operation costs associated with the logistics of getting a specific
input.
• Need of continuous water input: Some technologies have a great need of continuous water input.
This could potentially be a problem in a land suffering of periodic draughts.
• Space requirements: The space required for the different technologies varies from small to large
areas from less than 100 to greater than 1,500 m2. This means that different amount of land needs
13
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:29==
MATERIALS & METHODS
to be rented.
Revenue
• FairFishing savings and sales income: How much money the technology can generate (here only sales
income, not all the costs associated with the technology) and hence profit generated for FairFishing.
• Time before first revenue: The technologies have different retention times, and hence the time
between construction and generation of the outcome (time before first income is generated) will
differ. The technologies with high retention times (up to 4 months) will need external funding for
operation in this ”lag” period.
Sustainability
• Waste hierarchy (sustainability): The suggested technologies are placed differently in the waste
hierarchy, indicating that some of the technologies may be more sustainable than others based on
an evaluation of what the final product produced substitutes, e.g. animal feed, energy or nutrient
recycling.
Demand
• Internal use in FairFishing: This category was made to reflect the internal use wish. FairFishing as
a first priority wish to use the fish-residue based products internally, secondly to sell them externally.
• Local demand: Local demand was looked into to see if the products, if sold, would be attractive for
locals.
• Market volume: Indicate how big a market, thus, the chance that all produced products can in fact
be sold.
The different categories have naturally varying importance for successful implementation and operation.
Therefore, each category was given a weight score from 1 (low weight) to 12 (high weight) that was
multiplied to the scores in each category. The weight for each category was based on FairFishing’s judgment
(Appendix A.2.4, Table A.2) and authors knowledge. Equation 3.1 summarises how the weighted sum is
calculated;
Weighted sum per technology option i =

s is the score, i is the specific technology, and w is the weight score of the individual category c (from
1-18). After multiplying the weighing scores to each category, the weighted sum of the scores formed the
initial grounds and support for selecting two technologies to further assess. At this point, it was decided
to select two options with different use to further assess, based on FairFishing wishes and to show
the span of fish residue utilisation options.

3.5
Techno-economic assessment
A nominal financial cost-benefit analysis served as a basis for the evaluation of the economic feasibility
of each scenario. The nominal financial cost-benefit analysis performed in the report aimed at showing if
chosen project scenarios were financially viable for the NGO’s perspective and whether they were beneficial
at all. The financial assessment did not include costs and benefits of external effects to society [32]. The
current fish residue management is not a cost for FairFishing, hence the results of the economic assessment
will show direct savings or costs for FairFishing compared to current management.
In the financial cost-benefit analysis, costs and benefits of all included processes and materials (the scenarios’
inventory), were modelled and divided into the categories: total capital investment, here called capital and
start-up costs; running costs, which were divided into operation and maintenance costs; all financial benefits
of the project (sales income or savings), and lastly demolition costs. Figure 3.3 displays where the different
15
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:31==
MATERIALS & METHODS
considered costs, or sales income or savings were placed within the scenarios. Based on calculations on
costs and benefits of all included processes and materials, the total annual costs, total annual benefits,
annual cash flows (ACF), and annual present value (APV), and the feasibility indicators: net present value
(NPV), internal rate of return (IRR) and payback period were determined. If relevant for the treatment
option, it was evaluated how the results may benefit relevant FairFishing stakeholders. For each scenario,
results were compared with other studies to support the reliability of the findings. A sensitivity analysis
followed. The sensitivity analysis was further split in two; a scenario and parameter sensitivity analysis.
The scenario sensitivity analysis investigated scenario change’s affect on NPV, whereas in the parameter
sensitivity analysis, known as a pertubation analysis, sensitive parameters were detected. The sensitive
parameters were further assessed for qualitative uncertainty in a data quality analysis. To assess the final
NPV uncertainty, the critical parameters (parameters both sensitive and rely on poor data quality), were
in a risk analysis, quantitatively assigned uncertainties and propagated in a Monte Carlo simulation. A
comparison between the two management options’ final results was made in order to highlight where one
scenario performs better than the other and vice versa. They were compared first of all on NPV, but also
factors as capital costs, running costs, payback period etc. was taken into account in the comparison.
Results from the economic assessment showed that some categories in the ranking could weight differently.
This was not iterated back in the ranking section but instead differences were discussed.
The following sections further elaborate on the information gathered needed to perform the cost- benefit
analysis: modelled prices; inflation and discount rate; project lifetime; and funding, amortisation and
depreciation.
Thereafter, the calculations of the key cost-benefit equations, ACF, APV are explained,
followed by description of the applied feasibility indicators: NPV, IRR and payback period. Lastly, how the
sensitivity, data quality and risk analysis were performed are further described in detail.
Modelled prices
Prices on materials and processes (the scenarios’ inventory) were to a large degree modelled based on
prices from foreign web pages, if local prices could not be collected or found online. Shipping prices were
therefore initially included for products assumed not accessible in Somalia. If no price data was found,
qualified guesstimates were made. The currency used throughout the project is USD, as this is one of the
common currencies in Somaliland. Benefits from sales of any fish residue-based products were assumed
not taxed as all revenues by FairFishing were assumed used internally to benefit local stakeholders, for e.g.
lower ice prices.
Inflation rate and discount rate
A nominal approach was used in the analysis, where all prices were first inflated to represent each year’s
nominal costs and benefits, and then discounted with a nominal discounting rate.
Inflation is the result of a higher nominal value of cash flow and a lower purchasing power of money
[22]. Once found and determined for Somalia, the nominal cash flows of the scenarios were determined by
multiplying all previous years inflation rate (1+inflation rate) to the current year’s real cash flow. This is
illustrated in Equation 3.2.
A method to compare cash flows over time is by discounting the cash flows to present values. To discount
cash flows, a nominal discount rate was determined. A nominal discount rate represents the interest rate
of possible bank loans and is the one typically used for a financial analysis [22]. Equation 3.3 illustrates
how discounting is evaluated for the cash flows.
The discount rate and inflation rate were as site specific as possible based on known Somalian interest
rates and Somalian costumer price index, respectively. The inflation rate used was based on best available
Somalian Consumer price index, where historical values for 2014-2018 and estimated values from 2019-2024
were provided by Statista [122]. The inflation rate in the remaining project years was based on the average
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
16
==PAGE:32==
MATERIALS & METHODS
value of inflation between 2014 and 2024 [122]. Inflation rates are shown in Appendix F.1. An up-to-date
Somalian interest rate was hard to obtain, yet one Somalian project from 2019, African Development Bank
Group [4], used a discount rate of 12 %, which was here adopted. This value is consistent with interest
rates in the neighbouring countries; 7 % in Ethiopia [131] and 13 % in Kenya [149]. The value is also in
line with the World Bank, who typically uses a discount rate of 10 % in sub-Saharan countries [89].
Project lifetime
In order to discount cash flows, a lifetime of the project was be determined. The project life was determined
based on several factors; what have been found for other economic assessments on technologies in low-to-
middle-income countries, stability in the country and FairFishing’s own assessments. A project lifetime
of 10 years was used, starting year 0 to year 9. Year 0 is the year of construction, which is here
set to 2021. Construction was assumed to take few weeks and therefore benefits would start generating
already the first year (year 0). As some components may in fact have longer or shorter lifetimes, this speaks
for applying other lifetimes, which was investigated in the later scenario sensitivity analysis. For the two
selected technologies, the same lifetime was used for fair comparison.
Funding, amortisation and depreciation
All capital and start-up costs were showed calculated as a one year cost, as FairFishing plans to seek
external funding to cover all capital and start-up costs within this category rather than taking a bank loan.
In the scenario sensitivity analysis, it was later investigated how it would affect NPV if all capital and
start-up costs were in fact covered by funding. A funding limit of the project was estimated by Obara [88]
to 50,000 USD. For this reason, amortisation of the capital and start-up costs was not considered because
they were assumed to be funded by the NGO prior to implementation. Amortisation can be relevant in
case the capital investment is covered by a loan that has to be repaid with interest in the following years,
thereby possibly affecting NPV as interest rates are added.
Depreciation is the loss of asset values over time. Materials, as they all are used on a daily basis and
therefore from wear and tear were by the end of the project assumed a value of 0, even though some
components might still be functional at the projects end.

Viability indicators
The measures used for assessing project viability was mainly the NPV, however IRR and payback period
was also looked into.
The NPV of a project is the sum of the APV [22] and was calculated as shown in Equation 3.4. In cases
where NPV > 0.0, a given scenario or project is profitable. For comparisons, the scenarios with the highest
NPVs, were found to be the ones where the present benefit is highest.

(3.4)
The discount rate was set based on current interest rate in Somalia and may, as interest rates do, fluctuate
dependent on inflation and financial risks. The IRR represents the discount rate that is as minimum needed
in order to generate an NPV of zero, meaning at what discount rate the project starts producing overall
benefit and become viable taking into account the whole lifetime of the project. If IRR > k the project is
found viable from a financial point of view [22]. The higher the IRR compared to k, the more robust the
project is. The IRR was found by MS Excel’s IRR function, that solves for a discount rate that produces
an NPV=0.

Payback period is the time it takes in years for the plant to repay investment costs and start generating an
income. It was here calculated as the year where the accumulated sum of APV became zero.
3.5.1
Sensitivity analysis
A sensitivity analysis was performed to assess the robustness of the scenario results and to identify sensitive
parameters.
The sensitivity analysis was split up in two parts; a scenario sensitivity analysis and parameter (perturbation)
sensitivity analysis, as described by Clavreul et al. [28]. The latter was performed for high contributing
parameters: Parameters that constitute more than 5.0 % of the overall NPV. The threshold value was
based on the fact that all parameters contributing less than 5.0 % were found insignificant.
Scenario sensitivity analysis
As there for the selected fish treatment options were various possible scenario options meaning many
different processes and materials to include or exclude besides alternative governing parameters, variations
of the scenario setup and governing parameters were assessed for each scenario for relative NPV change,
see Equation 3.6.
%Relative change =
∆NPV
NPVinitial
= (NPV −NPVinitial)
NPVinitial
(3.6)
For both selected fish residue treatment options following variations of governing parameters were evaluated:
a 5 and 15 year lifetime rather than a 10 year lifetime; and a 5.0 % rather than a 12 % discount rate.
One of the scenario changes that was investigated, was inclusion of 100 % funding of capital and start-up
costs, as FairFishing plans to seek external funding, rather than loaning from a bank, to cover capital and
start-up costs. Another scenario change investigated, was addition of waste collection and treatment fees
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
18
==PAGE:34==
MATERIALS & METHODS
on the fish residues. It was here assessed, how it would affect the NPV if FairFishing, in both scenarios,
was paid 1.0 % and 2.5 % of the current fish fee annually paid to the local government by fishers. For
each selected fish treatment options, other relevant changes were additionally evaluated.
Parameter sensitivity analysis
For parameter sensitivity analysis, all high contributing parameters were individually altered 10 % to de-
termine how sensitive the NPV results were to that change. A sensitive parameter was defined as a high
contributing parameter with a normalised sensitivity ratio Sin above 0.30, meaning if a parameter is changed
with 1.0 %, it is sensitive if NPV changes with more than 0.30 %. For calculations of Sin, see Equation 3.7,
where ∆NPV is the change in NPV and ∆input is the parameter change (±10%).
Sin = (∆NPV/NPVinitial)
(∆input/input)
(3.7)
Lastly, all high contributing parameters were assessed for maximum change to reach a NPV of zero, this
referred to as the switching values. Switching values, hence, represent the value of the specific parameter
that would result in the scenario no longer being financially viable (NPV=0).
3.5.2
Data quality analysis
The data quality analysis functioned as a mean to detect which parameters to include in the risk analysis.
Uncertainty of the sensitive parameters was qualitatively assessed through a data quality analysis, as the
majority of applied values were not accessible from historical sample values with quantitative uncertain-
ties. The data quality was analysed by using the pedigree matrix by Weidema et al. [141]. Here each
parameter was given as a score from 1 to 5, where 1 is high and 5 is poor quality, in the given categories:
Reliability, Completeness, Temporal correlation, Geographical correlation, and Further technological cor-
relation. Parameters that had an average data quality score above 2 were found to be of poor overall
quality and therefore highly uncertain. Critical parameters where here defined as the sensitive parameters,
Sin >= 0.30, with an average data quality score of > 2. These parameters were the ones associated with
the largest risk.
3.5.3
Risk analysis
In order to determine the risks, here uncertainty of the NPV result and the risk of NPV to be below 0, the
critical parameters were assigned an expected distribution, based on expected highest realistic variations
and allowed variations of the parameters.
This allowed the study to evaluate the NPV with a Monte
Carlo simulation. Critical parameters were either assumed normal, uniformly, or triangularly distributed.
For equations applied for distribution of the parameters, see Appendix A.3. Once the distributions were
estimated, they were propagated as suggested by Clavreul et al. [28] and Conservation Strategy Fund
[32]. The uncertainty propagation was performed with a Monte Carlo simulation with 1,000 iterations
in MS Excel.
Each critical parameter were simulated to form 1000 random values within their given
distribution. Random combinations of these critical parameter variables yielded 1000 simulated NPVs for
each technology option. The more iterations made, the more precise distribution fit and determined risk
of the NPVs. Favourably, 10,000 or more iterations had been done, but as it was too computationally
demanding for MS Excel to process. Instead 1,000 iterations were applied and assumed fair to determine
the risk. It must, however, be noted that each time the simulation was run, the simulated NPV values
varied a bit and thereby each run would result in a small change in the risk.
Once the Monte Carlo NPV simulation was made, all simulated NPV results were grouped and counted
in order to insert the data in a histogram, where the distribution of NPV could be observed. Once a
19
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:35==
MATERIALS & METHODS
suitable fit was found, the risk of certain NPV values was evaluated based on the NPV’s cumulative density
function, see Appendix A.3.
3.6
Cancelled work due to Covid-19
The initial months of the project were to a large degree focused on literature review and planning of a field
trip to Somaliland, which would include conducting surveys, taking pictures and videos, and collect fish
residue samples for chemical analysis in Denmark. Furthermore, three days were put into applying funding
to cover travel expenses. Due to Covid-19, the field trip was cancelled. It was possible to convert the field
trip plan and surveys into a guide for data collection by a local in the area, which reduced the time needed
to replan. The planned methodology that was cancelled due to Covid-19 will not be further elaborated,
however is suggested for future work to obtain better results, see Section 5.0.2.
