4.1
Stakeholder assessment
To understand the landscape the project has to be accepted and manoeuvre in, all stakeholders to the project
were evaluated. A stakeholder identification was carried out, where currently involved stakeholders, as well
as potentially future involved stakeholders were identified. This to obtain a basis for analysis of importance
and influence of each stakeholder.
The stakeholders identified were; FairFishing; FairFishing Partners;
Fishers hired by FairFishing partners; Local non-FairFishing fishers; Local mongers; Local households; Local
chefs and restaurant owners; Farmers; Local waste collectors; Local city council; Somaliland government.
A description of each stakeholder can be found in Appendix B together with a table, Table B.1, where
all relevant stakeholders to FairFishing’s ”Waste-to-resource”-project, are presented together with the
organisation level, how they are affected by the project and how they can potentially contribute to the
system.
For the sake of clarity, a diagram displaying how the stakeholders interact as well as a description of
how they contribute to the system is shown in Figure 4.1. A blue line indicates communication between
connected stakeholders. Not all stakeholders, purple boxes, are involved in the system and currently in
communication with FairFishing.
FairFishing is the project responsible and communicates some of the identified stakeholders. These can
split into three groups: legal entities, stakeholders affected by FairFishing’s activities at the moment, either
by using the fish station or by selling or buying fish caught by FairFishing’s partners, and stakeholders not
affected by FairFishing’s activities at the moment. As indicated on the figure above, there is also some
internal communication between stakeholders affected by FairFishing’s activities at the moment.
This
includes distribution, selling and buying of fish.

4.1.1
Importance and influence
For each stakeholder, their influence and importance for the project to succeed was identified, as summarised
in Figure 4.2. Here importance translates into their positive engagement and interest into the project,
whereas influence is to what degree they can impact the project in terms of decision making that can
accelerate or stop the project, how much money they can fund the project with or to which degree they
can donate technical skill. Stakeholders in the upper right quadrant show stakeholder that are both of
high importance and of high influence, thus a stakeholder that has a high interest themselves in the project
but also have money and power. FairFishing is here placed in the upper right corner, indicating that they
have money (funding) and power. Partners are placed in the middle of the right quadrant. The reason
behind this is that the project is very important for them as they are direct beneficiaries of the project.
At the same time, they are involved in FairFishing decision making through an informal union called the
Producer Organisation FairFishing, which gives them influence and power, yet no capital to invest in the
project. Fishers hired by FairFishing partners are placed just under partners in terms of influence, as they
are generating the most important waste stream, fish residues, in the system, but has no other power.
The projects are as important for the fishers hired by the partners as for the partners themselves, as they
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
22
==PAGE:38==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
will benefit in the same way. Local mongers and farmers are placed just under fishers hired by FairFishing
partners, as they possess important waste streams to be included in the system. The project is relatively
important for farmers if animal feeds or fertilisers are produced. If it is decided not to produce animal
feed or fertiliser, the project is less important for farmers. In the upper left quadrant, stakeholders are
placed that have power, either in term of law and decision making, money or technical skills, but have low
interest in the project. Here, the legislative and administrative bodies of cities and region are placed; the
city council and Somaliland government. They are both too big organs to be directly involved and affected
by NGO internal projects, however both can easily restrict any movement of a foreign NGO. Finally, in the
bottom quadrants, stakeholders with low influence are placed, thus few means to affect the project, but
they have to a varying degree an interest in project results and benefits. The local waste companies are
placed in the bottom left corners, with no influence and no interest in the project. They will in the first
stage of the project not benefit from the project and has no power. Local chefs and restaurant owners
and house owners possess a potential fish and organic waste stream to include in the system. They are
however not included in the ”proof-of-concept” solution and hence not currently influencing the project.
The project is to some degree important for them as there is a potential for improved fish cooling, and
hence better quality of fish delivered to the restaurants. Local non-FairFishing fishers have no influence
but are secondary beneficiaries from the output of the project, and the project is hence to some degree
important for them.

4.2
Data inventory
In the following section, results of the data collection and fish residues analysis are presented. The data

in the following themes: fish distribution; fish filleting percentages; conventional products; demand of

organic waste streams of relevance. Original wording of presented surveys, and registered answers by Ali

consist of: fish residue composition, generation, projections, and mass-flows.

4.2.1
Data collection
Fish distribution
In 2019, 26 % of all fish caught stayed in Berbera, while the remaining fraction left to other parts of
Somaliland. It is estimated by Gulled [57] that at the moment, 70 % of what leaves Berbera is delivered
to Hargeisa and that the remaining 30 % ends in Burco, Gabiley, Wajaale and remaining small towns. The
percentage of fish staying in Berbera has been decreasing over the years concurrently with an increase in
the total quantity of fish caught. Is has been estimated that over the next 15 years, 25 % of all fish caught
will stay in Berbera. Assuming the same distribution between Hargeisa and the rest of Somaliland, means
that 53 % will go to Hargeisa and 23 % will be distributed over Burco, Gabiley, Wajaale and remaining
small towns.
Fish filleting percentages
Filleting percentages in Somaliland were estimated from surveys and are shown in Table 4.1 and used to
estimate waste generations and mass-flows in the system. At Berbera station and at markets, all guts,
heads and viscera were assumed removed, here referred to as gutting. Removal of remaining fish residues
(bones, skin, and some meat) was defined as filleting. Filleting takes place at markets, households, and
restaurants.
Table 4.1: Filleting and gutting percentages obtained by surveys performed in Berbera and Hargeisa in 2020. The
filleting percentage at Berbera Station represents gutting, as no filleting takes place here, only removal of guts, fins
and heads. The fraction that is not gutted at Berbera station is assumed gutted at markets. Note all values are
rounded to two significant digits.

Remaining
By surveying fishers at the station, a gutting percentage of 76 % was found. This percentage is according
to the Copenhagen based FairFishing staff questionable. Their experience and knowledge regarding to
gutting at the station, is that only a minimal amount of gutting takes place here. In a best case scenario,
all fish would be gutted at the station in order to remove microbes and parasites from fish intestines, which
will otherwise spoil the fish [21]. According to FairFishing, cultural and economic aspects however stand
in the way. The economic aspect is that fish is sold per weight, which means that fishers prefer to include
as much weight as possible. The cultural aspect relates to an understanding in peoples mind; if the fish is
gutted in front of costumers, the fish seems more fresh. Figure 4.3a and Figure 4.3b shows pictures from
2018 of a car loaded with fish ready to leave the station in Berbera. It is seen here how heads and fins
are still present on the fish, and it does not look like guts have been removed. It is hence not expected
that the gutting percentage is as high as the surveys indicate. FairFishing does, however, efficiently work
towards a higher gutting percentage at the station, and the found percentage from the data inventory has
therefore been used as an ideal scenario when the waste treatment facility is implemented.

Conventional products
A part of the local data collection, was to investigate what conventional products are used today that may
be substituted by the products from the suggested fish residue treatment options: fuel for cooking or power
generation, compost/fertiliser or animal fodder. Table 4.2 summaries the currently used products and their
prices in Somaliland of relevance for the coming techno-economic analysis.
Charcoal was found to be the main source of heat for cooking at restaurants, but to a minor degree
liquefied petroleum was is also used. The power supply was indirectly found from the questions related
to how the restaurants fuelled their freezers, where electricity from the grid and diesel were individuated
as energy source. Freezers in Berbera were more likely fuelled with grid electricity compared to Hargeisa,
see Appendix C.1. From communication with Gulled [57] and Obara [88], ice machines at Berbera Station
run on electricity from a diesel generator, whereas the FairFishing office runs on electricity from Berbera
electricity grid. Only occasionally, when there is power outage, a small diesel generator is taken into use
at the office.
Fodder and fertiliser products were found by surveying farmers. Three surveyed animal farmers owned
livestock( goats, camels and cows) and relied on mostly grass from surrounding pastures, but also to a
less extend other crop residues to feed their animals. Only during droughts, the farmers purchase hay or
oats for feeding at local markets or at nearby farms. The same farmers, along with three crop farmers,
were asked about how they fertilise their soil. The dominant fertiliser used is manure, free of cost, but
homemade compost was also mentioned to be used for fertilising. Two of six farmers mentioned that they
buy commercial fertiliser. Two types of commercial fertiliser were identified by Ali [8] at local markets, one
mineral fertiliser in Berbera and one nitrogen fertiliser in Hargeisa. The nitrogen fertiliser, ADNOC UREA
(46N:0.0P:0.0K), was found in Hargeisa market and were also mentioned by the farmers as a nitrogen
supply.
As water addition will be necessary for multiple of later suggested management options, the price of tap
water was additionally obtained.

The surveys show that both coal, diesel and ice cubes were used by stakeholders (farmers not asked) in
all groups in both surveyed cities. Coal was used daily in all surveyed restaurants in Berbera and in most
of the surveyed restaurants in Hargeisa, but never, or few times a month, by fishers and fish mongers in
Berbera and Hargeisa. Diesel was used daily by most of the surveyed fishers, fish mongers and restaurants
in Berbera and by the majority of the surveyed fish mongers and some of the surveyed restaurants in
Hargeisa. A minority of the surveyed stakeholders never use diesel.
Ice cubes were used daily by all surveyed fishers and fish mongers in Berbera, and only by a small part
of the restaurants in Berbera. These numbers were lower in Hargeisa, where a minority of fish mongers
and restaurants respectively used ice cubes every day.
Ice cubes were used least in restaurants.
The
reason for this is that many restaurants have freezers instead of using ice.
Of restaurant owners, the
majority in Berbera and all in Hargeisa, have their own freezer. It must be noted here that there are some
inconsistencies in the answers, when asked in restaurants, whether ice cubes is used, and then in an other
27
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:43==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
question, how many times a day ice cubes are used. Nevertheless, it can still be concluded, that ice cubes
are used less in restaurants than by fishers and fish mongers, and that restaurants in Hargeisa use less ice
cubes than restaurants in Berbera.
The surveyed stakeholders were asked about what their business would prefer to get a lower price on,
choosing between: coal, diesel and ice cubes.
It was found (Table 4.3) that the demand is different
depending on the stakeholder group and the city. Most fishers prefer a lower price on ice cubes, most fish
mongers prefers diesel, restaurant owners prefer coal and diesel, and most fruit/meat/vegetable mongers
prefer to buy coal at a lower price.
The findings supports FairFishing’s wish of producing diesel in order to lower prices on diesel. If a fish
residue treatment facility is build in Hargeisa later on, it must however be looked into whether or not it
makes sense to produce diesel for ice cubes, as it at the moment is not a wish for stakeholders in Hargeisa.
Livestock feed and fertiliser
The animal feed and fertiliser demand was investigated by surveying farmers near Hargeisa.
Nur [87]
performed six surveys of farmers. Full survey answers are found in Appendix C.1. It must be noted here
that it was only possible to get three livestock farmer surveys. Surveying three farmers does not necessarily
show the general demand, but the answers were used as best estimate and found as a fair estimate of the
general demand, as all three surveys showed the same tendencies. The answers were also confirmed by
both Nur [87] and Gulled [57] to be representative for livestock farmers near Hargeisa and Berbera. The
same holds for the crop farmers.
It was found from the surveys, that the livestock farmers only buy feed during droughts, when it is available
and they can afford it. Two out of the three farmers mentioned, that animals often die during droughts, as
they can not afford additional animal feed. Yet when affordable, all three animal farmers buy animal feed.
Farmers thereby experience a great obstacle in feeding their animals during droughts, due to availability
and affordability of fodder. The conclusion is therefore, that there is a high demand for affordable animal
feed in drought periods.
As stated in section 4.2.1, fertiliser in the form of manure and compost is available for free, why only two of
the six surveyed farmers buy commercialised concentrated fertiliser. The demand for compost does thereby
not seem to be high, if not given away for free. Producing compost will thereby most likely not generate
any or very limited income, and only function as a waste minimisation as well as benefit farmers and locals
interested in compost in nearby areas.
Current municipal solid waste management
Information on the current municipal management helped understanding how fish residues are handled
today, and thereby illustrates how suggested fish residue management options in this report differ from
current management.
Hargeisa MSW management
From literature review, only one older article was found that state how Hargeisa treats municipal solid waste
[31]. From the article, it was found that anno 2011, Hargeisa had two companies, DHIS Waste Management
and Sabawanag Waste Management, that collected waste from households and transfer stations located in
the city. At the time, DHIS covered the northern part of the city, and Sabawanag the southern part of the
city. In 2011, they each collected and transported approximately 20 loads of solid waste per day to transfer
stations, from where the waste was loaded onto tipper trucks and transported to its final destination at
remote landfills (Figure 4.4a). The companies together had minimum three compacter trucks, one truck
with a crane to empty and replace skips (Figure 4.4b) placed around in the city, and seven tipper trucks
[31]. From newly taken pictures, Figure 4.5 and Figure 4.4d, it is suggested that both tipper trucks and
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
28
==PAGE:44==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
compacter trucks are still used and are used for both collection and unloading at the landfill. Another
older source tells that the municipality has four main landfills, placed about seven kilometres outside the
city [125]. Due to improper disposal and overfilled dumpsites, the companies have been instructed to
purchase additional transfer stations (temporary dumpsites, designated and controlled). A survey from
2010 indicates that purchase has not been initialised [142]. Figure 4.4c illustrates an uncontrolled dump
site in the urban center of Hargeisa close to food markets; hence, the need for designated land for transfer
stations may still exist.
The entire city of Hargeisa generated in 2011 at a daily basis approximately 150 tonnes MSW (0.30
kg/capita/d) but only 50 % of the population was served by the collection system [31].
To have waste collected, the industries and households in Hargeisa pay a varying price; Up-to-date in-
formation obtained from surveys showed that 2/3 of restaurant owners in Hargeisa pay 1 USD - 5 USD
per day for waste collection, whereas 1/3 of the restaurant owners do not pay a fee or is not served by
the collection system. All surveyed fruit, meat and vegetable mongers pay a fee which is 7-10 USD per
month. According to local Hargeisa based Nur [87], households in Hargeisa all pay a fixed price; 5 USD
per household per month. As the waste collection is run by local companies, they deliver a monthly bill.
The local staff furthermore comment that a common procedure for households that are unable to pay the
fee, is to dump the waste at nearby riverbeds, where it is removed ”naturally” by the occasional rain that
flushes waste out to the sea.

Berbera MSW management
According to Styles [125] the local municipality in Berbera organises, collects and brings the waste to two
main landfills outside the town. Only at the port, a public-private partnership is responsible for collecting
waste from in-going and outbound ships [39]. Collivignarelli et al. [31] reported that in 2010, Berbera
29
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:45==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
municipal waste was collected in wheelbarrows and moved to a transfer station, consisting of a levelled
floor area in the urban area. Here the waste was manually loaded onto secondary collection vehicles and
transported to landfills for final disposal. In 2010, there were 48 collectors and five drivers employed in
Berbera to handle the 22 tonnes MSW daily produced in the city (0.39 kg/capita/d). At that time, 65 %
of the population in Berbera was serviced by the formal waste collection. From the surveys performed in
the present project, data indicates an upgrade of the municipal collection system to include compactors,
as shown in Figure 4.5.
According to Ali [8] and Ashour [16], Berbera municipality now collects and
transports waste twice a day to the local dumpsite. The collected waste will be transported to a large
landfill three kilometre outside Berbera town, where it is then burned on a weekly basis for waste reduction.
A waste collection twice a day sounded surprisingly high, however pictures of Berbera showing empty road
trash bin and clean streets supported the findings. Similar to prices in Hargeisa, the surveys showed that
the industries, business and households pay varying prices for waste collection and treatment; Restaurant
owners state a price ranging from a daily fee of 0.1 USD to annual fees of 25-190 USD independent of waste
mass or volume. According to Gulled [57], household collection fees are paid through taxation. According
to the survey, Berbera fishers pay 25 USD per year for waste collection of their waste, however this was
questioned by Gulled [57], who states that waste collection at the harbour is payed through a fish tax
(Appendix C.2) payed directly at the harbour by boat and car owners to the Somaliland Government. He
furthermore adds, that all fisher waste (fish residues along other organic and inorganic waste) generated at
Berbera harbour is disposed in large waste tank directly at the harbour side, and is emptied 1-2 times a day.
The tax includes waste removal among all other activities performed by the government at the harbour,
such as harbour protection and restoration. The tax is dependent on the mass of fresh fish caught; 300
Somaliland shilling (0.52 USD) per kg fish caught.

”INCLUCITY”, 22.5 million euros are divided between Mogadishu and Berbera for support for sustainable

Ministry of Planning and National Development, Municipality of Berbera and UN-Habitat have initiated
the Berbera Urban Development Project. In the project, they have already planned a new well-designed
landfill site and a recycling facility [40].
Challenges with Somaliland MSW management
The common waste treatment today in Somaliland is landfilling with occasional burnings. An older article
states, that Somaliland has room for improvements when it comes to their landfilling management. Ac-
cording to Bella and Vaccari [17], there was, anno 2014 among others, a lack of machinery and technology,
missing communication and collaboration between local stakeholders, and both environmental policies and
monitoring of current waste legislation were inadequate. The lack of land and ownership disputes has
made expansion and designation of land for landfills problematic. Collivignarelli et al. [31] furthermore
argued, that waste collection and disposal is perceived as a luxurious service by the broad population. The
willingness or ability to pay taxation or fees were, at the time given, low, and technical challenges of the
waste collection had put constraints to the growth of the waste sector and inclusion of a larger populations.
Figure 4.4a illustrates how the large landfills outside the urban areas in Somaliland are managed in 2020.
As there is no cover on the landfills, the organic waste fractions that end in the landfills, attracts animals
(Figure 4.4d). In 2011, 50 % - 65 % of the population in Berbera and Hargeisa was served by the formal
waste collection Collivignarelli et al. [31], whereby it can be expected that large quantities of waste have
historically been abandoned in urban areas, along drainage and canals, as well as in open plots. This may
to a large degree still be the case, as supported by pictures taken in Hargeisa in 2020, Figure 4.4c and
Figure 4.4d, and confirmed by Nur [87]. At present, Somaliland does not have a recycling program in place
[125], as it can be seen both by the large variety of waste at the landfill, and the temporary dump sites
displayed in Figure 4.4. This is in line with the information found from stakeholder surveys (Table 4.4) which
show that current stakeholders (fishers, mongers and restaurant owners) do not separate any waste, yet
alone organic waste. Instead all waste ends in the same bins for waste collection and landfilling. However,
as found in Hargeisa and Berbera, there is willingness from the community to separate waste fractions,
however a lack of incentives and benefits from the extra work it results in for the citizen, has hindered any
action so far.

Current fish residue management
Figure 4.6:
Open waste truck that
collects municipal waste along with
fish residues in Berbera.
Photo by
Obara [88] (2018)
A part of data collection in Somaliland was to survey different stake-
holders in Hargeisa and Berbera about how they treat fish residues.
It was here found that there is no sorting of fish residues and all in-
terviewee; fishers, restaurant owners and fish mongers, confirmed that
all fish residues end in the bin that is collected for waste treatment
and thereby receives same treatment as stated in Section 4.2.1. This
is supported by Figure 4.6. As mentioned in previous section 4.2.1,
and as Berbera station is where all fish is transferred through, large
amounts of fish residues can be expected at that site, and thereby be
part of the fishers waste that is treated through payment of fish tax to
the local government.
The current fish residue management in Berbera and Hargeisa works
as a waste reduction as well as a way to transfer waste away from the
urban area to improve the local urban environment and reduce pests
in the streets. However, no valuable end product or yield is generated.
The time it takes to treat fish residues with the current management,
includes collection time and the time until fish residues are burned,
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
32
==PAGE:48==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
which may vary from one day, to several weeks. If assuming a one
tonne fish residue generation a day, and that waste is burned once a week and not compressed, the
potential seven tonnes accumulated fish residue during a week will take up 13 m2 with a pile height of 1.5
m (Appendix D.1 for calculations). After burning, the waste piles are, at optimal conditions, reduced to
their ash content. In practise, as all municipal waste is mixed and as there is no control or turning of the
waste, the waste is presumably not completely combusted, which causes risk of methane emissions from
the degrading organic waste buried in oxygen free piles. As the landfills have been in operation for years, as
ash does not diminish, and due to limits of staking piles, the landfills in Berbera and Hargeisa are expected
to expand in the future.
Other organic wastes streams of relevance
Different organic waste streams may be available for incorporation into a suggested fish residue treatment
solution, for example the food and organic market residues generated at restaurants and markets, see
Table 4.4. An analysis of each stakeholders mass generation of different organic waste was not carried out,
but Collivignarelli et al. [31] found that organic fractions of municipal solid waste, may be as high as 21 %,
thus with 2010 values be equal to 4.4 tonnes and 32 tonnes organic waste per day in Berbera and Hargeisa,
respectively. These numbers do not include the large amounts of fish residues generated from FairFishing
Partners, as FairFishing started their operations in 2012 where no organised fishing infrastructure was
present in the country. To procure the organic waste streams, separation must be initiated. For separation
to work, spreading awareness and information on a functioning recycling facility and possibly economic
incentives can be introduced, as enquired by local stakeholders (Table 4.4). Additionally, it was found
that large quantities of livestock manure is periodically available at Berbera harbour, as Berbera is the
main export port of Somaliland livestock. Informed by Gulled [57], FairFishing Regional Program Manager,
the livestock shipping may vary with praying periods, however, livestock is kept in quarantines for export
outside Berbera where manure will be continuously available. From meeting with Gulled [57], see Appendix
C.2, it was informed that wastewater and sewage water is easy obtainable in Berbera. Berbera sewage
water is today not treated, but lead directly out the ocean from a sewage system ending by the harbour.
From Berbera station, wastewater is generated from washing of fish, and is lead directly to the sea as
well. The specific amounts of wastewater generated is not quantified, but according to Gulled [57], it is
substantial amounts. For these two wastewater streams, redirection and incorporation into a local fish
residue treatment facility close to the harbour, is an option.
4.2.2
Fish residue analysis
Fish residue composition
The fish residue composition was based on information from literature. In 2019, Goitia [53] in collaboration
with FairFishing collected data on fish types caught and brought to the station in Berbera. The results
showed that in the period 01/03/2019 to 11/11/2019, the majority of the catch was different tuna species;
Skipjack tuna, Longtail tuna and Yellowfin tuna, and a small percentage albacore tuna. The tuna species
together summed up to 83 % of the total catch. The remaining was a mix of billfish, mackerel and sharks.
For the composition of fish residues, it has been assumed that all fish residues therefore originate from tuna
processing, as tuna constitute the majority of the partners’ catches. The fish residues’ composition: fish
body parts, macro nutrient content, and chemical content, generated by FairFishing’s partners was esti-
mated and calculated by combining several studies on tuna waste composition. The estimated composition
is shown in Table 4.5, with ”Total comp.” being the consolidated data set used in calculations throughout
the report. Calculations to determine ”Total comp.” are found in Appendix D.1. In the following section,
the data found in literature and the estimated total composition and chemical characteristics of fish residues
are discussed.

Aqb [14] and Gamarro et al. [50] reports that 36 and 38 % respectively of tuna consists of fish heads
(13-17 %), skin (8-10 %), bones (4-6 %), viscera (5-8 %) and fins (1-2 %). These parts are categorised
as by-products, which means that at least 36 to 38 % of the total fish caught is by-products and that this
share, as a minimum, is wasted. In reality, not only the fish by-products ends up as waste. A share of the
fish meat is as well wasted. This is clear from Figure 4.7, which shows a bucket with wasted fish residues.
The figure shows how a substantial amount of meat is wasted as big pieces of meat are thrown out.

Figure 4.7: Picture illustrating waste generated
at the station in Berbera. Picture was taken by
Obara [88] in 2018
Waste quantities generated in the whole value chain (from the
fish is caught until it is served) is guesstimated by FairFishing
to be somewhere in the range of 30-60 %, depending on fish
type, filleting skill and tools available. Combining FairFish-
ing’s guesstimate with literature values on fish by-products
and considering the picture of wasted fish (Figure 4.7), total
waste fractions is estimated to be in the range of 37 to 60 %.
From this estimate, the total share of fish meat wasted (ex-
cluded that in the by-products) must thereby be in the range
of 0.0 - 23 % of the fish. In order to take into account the
share of fish meat in the waste, an average meat waste frac-
tion of 12 % ( 23 % /2.0 ) of the whole fish has been used in
the composition estimates of fish residues. This means a total
waste fraction of 49 % (37 % + 12 %). From now on, this
percentage is referred to when the waste fraction is mentioned.
The fraction of fish residues was estimated to consist of 69 %
tuna by-products (heads, fins, viscera and bones) excluding
skin, 6.9 % skin and 24 % meat.
The actual waste composition varies at different locations, due
to the information that gutting is only taking place at the station and at markets, see Section 4.2.2.
The waste composition, which is presented in Table 4.5, is the average composition of waste in the whole
system and the one that has been used in all calculations. It is assumed that guts, heads and fins are removed
from all fish either at the Berbera station or at markets. As the ”By-product excl. skin” composition which
includes guts, heads and fins does not deviate by large amounts from the ”Total comp.”, see Table 4.5,
with exception of the ash content, it was assumed to be a fair assumption.
Fish residue generation
Between 2015 and until November 2019, all fish caught by FaiFishing’s partners, delivered at the station in
Berbera, was weighted. In several month of 2015 and 2016 and last months of 2019, data was missing. For
those months, data sets has been partially estimated as average values of available data in the given year.
The total amount of fish caught from 2015 to 2019 is shown in Figure 4.8. All fish caught by FairFishing’s
partners in Berbera, is distributed to Berbera, Hargeisa, Burco, Gabiley, Wajaale and to a small degree
other small towns. The total waste generated over the entire value chain (from fisher to consumer) is hence
distributed over a large area in Somaliland. It is evident from the figure that the trend is up-going, and
that the amount of fish caught over the years has been increasing by large amounts. From the years 2015
to 2016 however, there has been a decrease in the total fish quantity caught. The reason for the decrease
is not known, but as half of the 2015 and 2016 data is estimated, this could be part of the explanation.
Between 2015 and 2019, the fish quantity caught increased by 250 % and reached in 2019 almost 1,300
tonnes, meaning an average daily capture of 3.5 tonnes in 2019. Big uncertainties exist regarding the
percentage of fish caught that is discarded as waste. Assuming that 37 % and 60 % of the fish in 2019
becomes waste (see above section), the average waste generation in the system was 1.3 to 2.1 tonnes per
day, respectively. Daily waste quantity in the system in 2019 is therefore assumed to be in that range.
Using the average waste fraction of 49 % yields a total of 1.7 tonnes fish residues on an average day in
2019. The generation of fish residues over the years, using the estimate of 49 % being wasted, is shown in
Figure 4.8 together with the total amount of fish caught.

Fish quantities delivered to the station in Berbera in each month of the year, varies. Assuming same waste
fraction over the year, means that waste quantities varies as well. Waste quantities in each month of the
year, assuming 49 % of fish goes to waste, are shown in Figure 4.9 from 2017 to 2019. Data for 2015
and 2016 were excluded here, as only half data-sets were available for these years. Figure 4.9 shows that
waste quantities varies substantially over the year, with great variation between the month with most and
least fish caught. In 2017, there was a five-time difference between the month with most and least fish
caught. In 2019, the variation over the year was a factor 2.0. The data indicates peak flows from July to
September and again from December to January. The period with least fish caught are seen in May and
June. These trends have been confirmed by Gulled [57], see Appendix C.2, who tells that during winter,
there are lots of tuna available. During summer, there are more bottom fish available which is harder to
catch and requires better skills than the fishers currently have. In 2019, the estimated fish residue quantity
over the year varied between 1.1 tonnes per day up to 2.1 tonnes per day, assuming a constant waste
fraction of 49 %. The data hence indicates that waste quantities varies over the year, and the treatment
technology suggested must therefore be able to deal with fluctuating waste flows.

Fish residue projections
Many factors may affect the future amounts of fish residues in the system. The following section show the
estimated projection and discusses factors affecting future fish residues.
Fish quantities caught over the coming years has been estimated from an LN function presented in equation
4.1. The r value (0.979) was under r critical (1.000) for n = 3 with a significant level of 50 % [102] and
was hence not significantly related at the given significant level. As there is only three years of data, it does
not make much sense to look at statistical correlations. There, however, does not exist better data sets,
and as the logarithmic regression was the best fitted regression with the highest R2 value, it was considered
the best estimate of coming years fish quantities caught.
f(x) = 366.36 · LN(x) + 912.59
(4.1)
Where f(x) is tonnage fish caught per year and x is years from 2017. The first year in the regression is
hence 2017. Fish residue quantities hereof are found as 49 % of f(x). Both fish and fish residue projections
from 2020 to 2030 are shown in Figure 4.8.
A factor affecting waste generation is filleting skills. One of FairFishing’s focus areas is to reduce waste
by providing fishers and restaurant owners with knowledge and equipment on how to efficiently fillet fish
to reduce waste quantities. Filleting skills were as an example taught by FairFishing in a public awareness
campaign ”Cool, Clean & Tasty”, conducted in 2018 [132]. Filleting tools and skills are however, at the
moment, of poor quality. If FairFishing succeeds in their campaign, future waste fractions may be lowered,
compared to what is projected. This will mean that the real overall waste quantity in the system is lowered.
Unpredictable factors such as a sudden changes in the amount of FairFishing partners and fishing vessels,
as well as more and better equipment, could potentially change fish and fish residue quantities substantially
from one year to another. According to FairFishing, the demand of fish has been increasing substantially
over the years, as fish is getting culturally more accepted as part of peoples diets. Fish used to be considered
a poor food and fishers used to be poor men, but this is not the case anymore.
According to Obara [88], the limited fish catch by FairFishing partners in years 2018 to 2019 being could
be caused by an increasing competition from other companies and private fishers. This means that fish
residues are generated outside of the considered system by other non-FairFishing fishers. At present, it has
been confirmed by the local manager at the Berbera office, Gulled [57], and the Local Public Awareness
and Market Development Lead based in Hargeisa, Nur [87], that fish quantities seems to be greater in 2020
compared to the same months in 2019. The fish quantity is thereby assumed to increase in the coming
years. A known factor which FairFishing expects a potential increase in fish quantities from, is the planned
expansion of Berbera port. It has not been possible to find data on when it will be due, but it is stated on
the official website, DP World [35], that the port should be ready three years after satisfaction of terms
and conditions for the agreement. FairFishing furthermore hopes for expansion of the station in the coming
years and are looking into possibilities of expanding with another building.
Fish residues mass-flows
In order to find the geographical distribution of fish residues in Somaliland, and thereby the optimal
placement of a waste treatment facility, the fish residue mass-flow was determined. The fish residues are
to a large degree dependent on the fish flows around the system. All fish caught by FairFishing’s partners
is known to be delivered to the station in Berbera where it is weighted, before distributed out to Berbera,
Hargeisa, Burco, Gabiley, Wajaale and to a small degree other small towns in Somaliland. After leaving the
station in Berbera, fish is delivered to fish mongers at different markets in the named areas of the country.
At markets, fish is bought either by restaurant owners or private persons. Fish residues are hence generated
at the station, at markets, in restaurants and in private households. All losses have been estimated to

but looking at the numbers, it is evident that Hargeisa could be relevant for fish residue treatment as well, if
the project becomes a success in Berbera. Due to the low generation of fish residues in the remaining towns,
more information and highly probable higher quantities of fish residues are needed in order for the treatment
facility to be viable from FairFishing’s point of view. In the future, more fish may be distributed to the
remaining towns and thereby generate larger amounts of fish residues there. Therefore, it is recommended
to monitor fish flows in the coming years, as it may be relevant to introduce a similar treatment in the
other towns.
For simplicity and since Berbera will be the focus area for the ”proof-of-concept” solution, waste flows for
Berbera are described in the following. All waste flow calculations and filleting percentages for Hargeisa
can be found in Appendix C.3. No stakeholder surveys were conducted in Burco, Gabiley and Wajaale, and
filleting percentages for these locations were not estimated. Only total waste fraction has been estimated
and are presented in Table 4.6.
By surveying fishers at Berbera station, an average gutting percentage of 76 % was found at the station.
Gutting of fish includes removal of heads, fins and guts. Gutting is estimated to constitute 15 % of the
whole fish. Using the gutting ratio found in the survey gives a total of 470 kg fish residues per day at
the station. Fish that is not gutted at the station is assumed to be gutted at markets, which is the next
step in the fish distribution chain. The remaining 24 % of fish that is not gutted at the station, is hence
gutted at markets. About 40 % of all fish at markets in Berbera is filleted before it is sold. The waste
fraction originating from filleting, consists mainly of bones, skin and meat, which constitute 34 % of the
fish. This gives a total of 170 kg fish residues per day at markets in Berbera. Restaurants predominantly
buy whole fish. Only 21 % of what is bought by restaurants is filleted at markets, the remaining 79 % is
bought unfilleted, but without guts, heads and fins. The first distribution chain ends at restaurants, where
the reaming unfilleted fish is filleted. The total amount of fish residues at restaurants is estimated to be
140 kg per day in Berbera, which is distributed out on a large amount of restaurants. Private persons
predominantly buy filleted fish. The ratio of filleted and whole fish bought by households at markets is 59
% and 41 % respectively. Distribution chain number two ends at households, where the total amount of
fish residues is 70 kg per day distributed between a large amount of households.
4.3
Technology screening
4.3.1
Technology review
Production of fish meal, fish silage, black soldier fly larvae (BSFL), biogas, charcoal/biochar and compost
were reviewed as potential technologies and assessed for technological fit. In the following section, results
of suggested technologies applied to treat fish residues generated in Somaliland are presented. All results,
yields, size, substitution potential etc. are based on a one tonne daily input of fish residues for the sake of
simplicity. All calculations can be found in Appendix D.1. Current fish residue management in Somaliland
is described in Section 4.2.1.
Fish meal production
General technology description
Fish meal production is a multi-process technology, mainly used to handle large scalefish waste generation
and forage fish catch as input. During multiple processes within the fish meal plant, Fish meal(a dry
powder) is produced and if the fish input is high in oils ad fat, fish oil is also produced. Fish meal is today
used globally as a source of protein feed in fish farming and in agriculture to e.g. poultry. Figure 4.11
provides an overview of the processing steps for common fish meal and fish oil production.
39
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:55==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
Figure 4.11: Figure displaying the main processes of onshore fish meal and oil production (Inspired by [36])
The processing steps for fish meal and oil production is illustrated in Figure 4.11, and initially includes
leading the fish residues and forage fish to the mincer, e.g. by a feeding screw, where the input is minced
to smaller pieces. For pieces that are already small, this step can be excluded [36, 42]. In the following
cooking or heating process, a firm mass is formed from the input mass by denaturing the protein in the
input by heating. This ruptures the fat depots and liberates oil and bound water. Additionally, the process
sanitizes the input by killing microbes [36, 42]. The strainer, the next process, will separate the oil and
water (now called press liquid) that is released from the cooker, and the firm mass (now called a press
cake). Thereafter, a pressing process removes remaining oil and water from the press cake, commonly by
using a screw press. Often, separation of the liquid and solids from the press liquid takes part in decanters
which will centrifuge the input. Two types of decanters exist; the two-phased and the three-phased, where
the latter additionally separates oil [36]. If the oil content in the fish residue is more than 2.5 % WW,
there is no need to separate oil [42]. The stick water, the water from the centrifuge that may still contain
small amounts of oil and solids, undergoes evaporation to extract the last solids. The output from this
process (stick water concentrate) will be transferred to the drying process along with the press cake and
press sludge from the decanters [36]. In a drying process, the remaining water in the input (press cake,
press sludge and stick water concentrate) is reduced. The goal is a moisture content below 12 % [15, 36].
In the process cooling, the fish meal, which from the dryer has a high temperature (approximately 80 ◦C),
must be cooled, often by air blowing. If not cooled, the moisture from the air may be reabsorbed into
the meal [15, 36]. Lastly, grinding or milling takes place to produce a powdered or granular end product.
The process often includes an initial step with sieving, to avoid milling the small pieces as this can create
dust and yield loss [36, 42]. To ensure long storage capacity antioxidants may be added. Finally, the fish
meal and oil are bagged or put in drums for storage. The residue products, e.g. waste waters, will require
treatment prior discharge to local waters [42].
The machinery to produce fish meal is often bought as a package solution with all processes included in
one facility. Fish meal production today is highly complex, as described in the previous section and has
high demands in terms of fuel, electricity and fresh water to run the processes. It is therefore considered a
high level and costly technology.
An advantage of fish meal production is the treatment of large quantities of fish residues and the production
of a high quality and valuable animal feed that can be sold to global markets. A major disadvantage, is that
the technology is costly to purchase and run. Furthermore, as it consists of multiple technical processes,
skilled and technically-educated operators are essential to run the plant and requires a fresh input for feed
conversion.
Applied technology
The current smallest fish meal plant [74] is still too large to run at full capacity treating one tonne fish
residues a day. The technology is nevertheless suggested as it may be relevant in the future as fish landings
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland

content limit for separation of oil, fish oil production is excluded and only fish meal will be the considered
product. Fish meal is expected to be used as animal feed that can substitute hay. An overview of the
considered step of the technical review is shown in Figure 4.12.

Design specification and processing time
The smallest found facility manage two tonnes waste per eight hour [74] and is the one proposed in the
study. The plant contains a conveyor, cooking, pressing and drying unit, requires 35 kWh per tonne waste
input and has a plant capacity of six tonnes per 24 hour and no fuel input. For cooking, they use a heat
conducting oil as carrier and an electromagnetic heating method. For drying they use a steam dryer. A
teeth granulator (stirring teeth) will mill and dry the product.
Yield and substitution potential
Yields of fish meal are based on the assumption that all dry matter minus fat content ends as fish meal.
As Aqb [14] recorded a tuna meal water content of 10 % WW and a 3 % WW oil content, the same
values have been used and added to the total fish meal yield. The fat content in the meal is lower than
indicated in other found literature, where it is found to be as high as 12 % WW fat [154], however with
the determined content in the initial fish residues input to the plant (31 kg fat/tonne WW fish residues),
a 12 % WW content would not be possible as it requires a minimum 58 kg oil from the fish residues that
go to the fish meal. The reaming content, e.g. crude protein, sugar and ash, is assumed equal to the fish
residues’ content. The fish meal yield is determined to be 350 kg fish meal and if oil extraction had been
considered, 21 kg oil / tonne WW fish residues input would have been produced.
The amounts of tropical livestock that can be fed (TLUF) have been calculated based on the calculated
nutritional potential of fish meal given in Table 4.8.

The TLFU results, shown in Table 4.7, indicate that fish meal as a protein source is more advantageous than
as an energy source (64<490 TLUF). For full coverage of daily protein requirements, 490 tropical livestock
units can be fed, equivalent to 490 camels or 49,000 chicken. These are theoretical values. Practically, it
is not recommended to feed more than 10 % of poultry diets with fish meal with high fat content, as it
may taint the meat [103]. Furthermore, it has been observed that fish meal may affect eggs by taint [95,
139]. Protein substitution potentials of conventional feed in Somaliland, as shown in Table 4.7, indicate
that fish meal can substitute substantial amounts of hay (8.2 kg hay/ kg fish meal), but only 1.2 kg hay/
kg fish meal for energy substitution.
Facility size and labour need
The plant size treating the given yield is 670 m2 which includes the plant by LongTeng Machinery [74].
An additional 30 m2 for waste delivering, storage etc. and 25 m2 for office and employees facilities was
included in the facility. As indicated in the product information by [74], the process time is eight hours
per two tonnes input. It is guesstimated that for the given size facility, which to a large degree functions
automatically, only one employee per tonne fish residues is required to surveil the plant and bag the fish
meal. For ongoing maintenance a skilled and educated worker may be required as to repair and replace
part of the machinery requires technical knowledge.

Fish silage production
General technology description
Fish silage is a liquid or dry substance which consist of hydrolysed (broken down) fish proteins and micro-
nutrients. It is used as animal feed or as a fertiliser and is often made from fish residues. The process
is an enzymatic breakdown of the fish, initiated by addition of either an organic or inorganic acid or a
fermenting unit together with a bacterial culture. The processing steps are the same for both types of
silage production. However, using a fermenting unit and a bacterial culture requires closer control and
is more complicated than using an organic or inorganic acid [129]. Pérez [97] suggests addition of either
3.5 % acid or 20 - 30 % of a single carbohydrate source for fermentation, per tonne fish residues, see
Table 4.9. Most of the enzymes which drive the hydrolysis are contained in the fish guts, which make guts
essential to include in the process [129]. Silage production requires a low pH, as the enzymatic breakdown
is only occurring in acid pH ranges. An acidity below 4.0 furthermore keeps flies away and prevent fungi
and bacterial growths. Ideally, the pH is between 3.5 and 4.0 (for inorganic acids even lower; around 2).
Due to the acidic environment it is important to make silage in acid resistant containers, i.e. polyethylene
[127]. Hydrolysis enzymes are active between 5.0 and 40 ◦C and hence silage can be produced in that
temperature range. High temperatures speeds up the process and in tropical climates, as in Somaliland,
the process only takes a few days [127]. Fish silage can be produced as a wet or a dry product. For wet
use, silage is mixed with other feeds when served, whereas dry fish silage is dried at the facility after the
silage process, in order to obtain dry feeding blocks [7]. For a more detailed description of the technology,
see Appendix D.2.
Fish silage offers a way to recycle nutrients for animal feed or fertiliser use. It is a fairly simple process that
can be done by unskilled workers and which requires minimal equipment and maintenance [106]. It has a low
processing time and the facility thereby requires minimal space. Fish silage can be made without addition
of other organic fractions [127, 129] and the technology is thereby not dependent on other feedstocks
and the logistics related to that. Some disadvantages related to fish silage production are potentially high
transportation costs of wet silage [103] and the need to include guts. If fish silage is to be used as animal
feed, there is a need of the input material being fresh. The same standards do, however, not apply for use
as a fertiliser [127]. Another disadvantage related to fish silage production is the fact that it is not yet an
established commodity, which means that it requires marketing efforts to promote acceptance [103]. The
research done on fish silage production for this project verifies that the method is still not established, as
limited up-to-date and good quality data was found on the topic.
Applied technology
A simplified setup of fish silage production is chosen for technical review. The one tonne fish residues
and an unspecified acid or fermenting unit (both reviewed) was considered the only input. As a dry silage
process was assessed to be the most suitable one, due to transport costs of wet silage, dry processing was
from here on considered. Both wet and dry options were, however, initially calculated and can be found in
Appendix D.1. The output produced was dry fish silage. As fish silage application as feed is found more
valuable, it is suggested from here on and calculated in the following sections, however, it can be used as a
fertiliser as an alternative. Dry fish silage was expected to be used as animal feed that can substitute hay.
An overview of the considered steps of the technical review is shown in Figure 4.13.

Design specification and processing time
In Table 4.9 technical specifications have been listed for dry fish silage production. It should be noted that
the wet and the dry processes are similar. The dry process just has one more step to it - the drying.
The suggested processes for dry fish silage production for use in Somaliland is showed in Figure 4.13 and is
based on Pérez [97], Tatterson and Windsor [127], and Toppe et al. [129]. As showed in Figure 4.13, fish
residues are minced as the first step after arriving to the facility. The grinding is done to ensure that all
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
44
==PAGE:60==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
of the material (fish) will come into contact with the acid or the fermenting culture and is done with an
electronic mixer as the one found in [138]. This step is estimated to take a couple of hours. Afterwards,
the fish is put in a container where a degradation component, here either an organic or inorganic acid or a
fermenting unit together with a bacterial culture, is added. For the given setup the degradation component
has been kept unspecified and the two different options are investigated. Occasional stirring is important
to ensure uniformity in the mixture. pH is furthermore important to measure until it is stable.
After a couple of days (warm climate), the silage is ready. The silage process is here estimated to take
two full days. When wet fish silage is ready, the drying process can start. An idea here could be to press
out some water before the actual drying process, as the water pressed out could potentially be used for
irrigation during droughts. Pressing is not included when calculating substitution potentials. The drying
process suggested is a simple solar tunnel dryer process, utilising solar radiation. The drying process is
estimated here to take three days. Storing and packaging is the final step and is estimated to take a couple
of hours. The total process of dry fish silage production is estimated to take 5.5 days. This is hence three
days more than a wet silage process.
Yield and substitution potential
Most nutrients remain in the final product [129], hence a yield of almost 1:1 of the nutrients in the input
can be obtained. Yields can be seen in Table 4.9. The dry yield is 430 kg per tonne fish residues, assuming
a moisture content of 10 %. Fish silage has been shown to be a viable part of several Somaliland livestock
diets [106], including goats [96], sheep [7, 96], and poultry [66].

With the produced fish silage from Somaliland, TLUF was calculated based on the nutritional potentials
displayed in Table 4.10. In the drying process there may be leaching and thereby a small reduction of
nutrients, that has not been included. From the TLUF for livestock protein coverage, as shown in Table 4.9,
there is a feeding potential of 600 TLU, equivalent to 60,000 chickens. The energy feeding potential is 6.5
times smaller. There is a risk, as with fish meal, that it may cause taint of the product (e.g. meat, milk or
butter) produced from the animal the fish silage is fed to, if fed in too high concentrations. In order not
to cause taint, the oil percentage from fish silage must not exceed 1 % of the total diet [103]. Fish silage
is also normally fed as a fraction of the livestock diet, e.g. Widjastuti et al. [143] showed 4 % wet tuna
silage to be the optimal percentage in broiler diets. In cases where the oil content is too high, the silage
must be deoiled before being fed to the animal [103].

The total facility size needed for a production with the given yields is approximately 120 m2 for dry silage.

2.2 m2 and some space around, 30 m2 estimated for waste delivery, storage and packaging, 25 m2 estimated
for office and employee facilities, and a 59 m2 solar tunnel dryer (greenhouse) for drying. The retention
time in the solar tunnel dryer is estimated to be three days based on N. D. Palkar et al. [93] and Pérez
[97], and it must hence be scaled for three days of silage production. The solar tunnel dryer contains three

Black soldier fly larvae production
General technology description
One way of producing edible protein, mostly for animal consumption, is through feeding of Black Soldier
Fly Larvae (BSFL) on organic waste. Larvae can be fed many types of organic waste, e.g. manure, kitchen
waste, fish residue and will grow until they reach their prepupa phase. At that stage, they can be harvested
and are then ready for use directly as animal feed or can be dried for long term storage. As the technology
mainly requires feeding, monitoring and simple treatment of the larvae for storage, e.g. boiling and drying,
it is considered a low-tech solution. Due to the use of basic and easily available equipment, e.g. plastic
storage containers and nets, it is also considered low of cost.
Larvae production has different stages [69]: First in the oviposition, larvae-eggs are laid either by attraction
of naturally occurring adult Black soldier flies or by controlled adult rearing system for larvae egg production.
In the initial days, the larvae eggs are nursed and surveilled before they enter the growth phase. In the
growth phase the larvae will be fed with organic waste for 14-17 days with feeding every third day. Black
soldier fly larvae can feed on many different types of substrates [34], however, some pre-treatment may
be used to reduce the waste input particle sizes and thereby make it easier and faster for the larvae to
digest. Once the larvae have reached their growth limit, they prepare themselves for pupa stage by naturally
seeking higher grounds. For extraction this can be taken advantage of: by placing boards or tubes into
the larvae box at an angle and having a bucket at the end, the large migrating larvae are easily collected.
An alternative is sieving. Once harvested, the living larvae can be fed directly to poultry. Alternatively,
they can be made storable by first boiling and then either cooking or sun drying. It is recommended to
grind the dried larvae for easier digestion for animals. Due to high oil content, to ensure long storage
without spoilage, it is recommended to de-fat the dried larvae either by an oil press, chemical extraction or
a centrifuge, down to a 10 % oil content. The extracted oil may be used as for biodiesel production [67].
Pupa, larvae leftovers, and undigested organic waste can be used as soil amendments, composting or feed
into a biogas digester (when high in moisture and slurry-like) [34].
For continued production of larvae, some live pupa is collected and allowed to transform into flies in a
netted cage. A controlled adult rearing system for larvae egg production can hereby begin [34].
According to [128], larvae temperatures for growth and survival are best at 27 and 30◦C and a humidity of
30–90 % is sufficient [114] for mating and oviposition. Larvae require shade to grow but need sunlight for
flies to mate and lay eggs. The eggs prefer darkness to grow. Hence, the facility will need to control light
for the different cages. No nutrient requirements for the feed are yet established, however a suitable C:N
ratio range of 10-40 is found. Water content of the fed waste must be between 60-90 %, preferably 70-80
% [67].
Today, the most commonly used larvae type is the black soldier fly larvae (Hermetia illucens (L.) – Diptera:
Stratiomyidae) for animal feed in medium- and large-scale production systems; however, the house fly
(Musca domestica L. – Diptera: Muscidae), which is found naturally in many regions of Africa, can also
be used. Both types are currently used in small- to medium scale productions throughout Kenya, Ghana
and China. However, the household fly has one major disadvantage: as they feed during their fly stage, in
contrast to the black soldier fly, they can potentially spread diseases. The spreading of diseases is caused
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
46
==PAGE:62==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
by microbe transfer from waste via the fly that can move long distances and feed on both organic food for
human consumption and unsanitised waste. On the other hand, the advantage of household flies is that
they grow faster and are naturally present in most places in Africa. Today, the BSFL is preferred, as the
fly does not feed in its fly stage and therefore is not a course for concern for spreading diseases and it does
not bite either. Furthermore, the BSFL is able to produce a higher yield per dry substrate fed. [69]
One advantage that makes BSFL stand out from the other feeding options is that it is possible to mix
it with other organic wastes, including organic waste that is not directly suitable for animal feed without
conversion, such as manure. This enables it to expand and for the future, incorporate other organic waste
streams, thereby reducing the fraction that ends in landfills and may cause methane emissions. However, a
concerning issue with the larvae technology is that such a solution may be perceived as unclean and falsely
be associated with diseases etc.. Hence, convincing people to work with it requires information to the local
community to increase social acceptance.
Applied technology
A simplified setup of BSFL production is chosen for technical review. The one tonne fish residue was
considered the only input and for processing BSFL, rather than household flies, were chosen to grow on
the input feed due to their lower risk of spreading diseases.
The only considered output produced was dried BSFL and was expected to be used as animal feed that can
substitute hay. Undigested waste from larvae production is a sub-product not considered for the review,
but may be used for composting purposes. An overview of the considered step of the technical review is
shown in Figure 4.14.

Design specification and processing time
The design for larvae production was simple, and as suggested in Figure 4.14, only includes larveros, netted
cages and gear for boiling and sun drying. As the fish residues have a C:N ratio of 5.1, it may be advisable
to add a carbon-rich source. However, it is not a requirement as optimal nutrient contents are still not
investigated [67]. To obtain a C:N ratio of 10 it is possible to add 330 kg vegetable food waste per tonne
fish residue. For the study no addition of other organic waste is considered, as the limit C:N ratio needs
further investigation. As temperatures in Somaliland and the moisture content of fish residues are within
the desired ranges, no additional heating or water is considered.
A period of five days was chosen for nursing of the eggs in larveros before they hatch and for nursing of
the young BSFL. Thereafter, approximately 12 days is spend on feeding of the young larvae to harvestable
larvae in accordance with recommendations from [34]. Two days is assumed for drying of larvae after the
process.
Yield and substitution potential
Literature on BSFL fed on fish residue is scarce, however, it was found that St-Hilaire et al. [124] produced
BSFL from a mix of fish offal and cow manure substrate and obtained a prepupa weight of 150 mg WW
per larvae harvested. Nguyen et al. [84] similarly studied the growth of BSFL fed solely on fish rendering
and obtained a weight of approximately 140 mg WW per prepupa larvae harvested. Dortmans et al. [34]
states an assumed production of six million larvae per week per nine tonnes biowaste. Assuming a linear
correlation, this may result in 670,000 larvae from one tonne fish residue per week. Incorporating the larvae
weight fed on solely fish residue [84], daily yields reach 100 kg WW larvae per tonne fish residue. The dry
weight of larvae fed on fish rendering is 47 % [85], which was applied. When drying the larvae, a small
amount of water remains (<10 %). The value of 8.7 % WW [133] was applied as the moisture content
of the dried BSFL fed on fish residue in Somaliland, however, a moisture content down to 6.4 % WW has
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
48
==PAGE:64==

been observed of solar dried BSFL in Kenya [68]. With these assumptions a total yield of 53 kg dried BSFL
per tonne fish residue is obtained. Obviously, large amounts of the waste is not converted to larvae weight,
which is in accordance with the expected conversion efficiencies (1.3 to 33 % for TS [23]). The calculated
nutritional potential of BSFL, based on the crude nutrient content of BSFL fed fish rendering [85], is given
in Table 4.12.

BSFL has been found suitable as poultry fodder. By feeding BSFL as a protein source, the dried BSFL can
cover 54 tropical livestock units’ daily protein requirements, equivalent to 5400 chickens and substantially
less when considering it as a source of energy. In practise, literature indicates that broilers can be fed with
up to 50 % of their daily diet with BSFL without affecting daily body weight gain, feed conversion ratio,
aroma or taste of cooked breast meat, compared to feeding conventional imported soybean and fish meal
[92]. Studies on taint of meat or eggs from animal diets with BSFL, where the BSFL had been fed fish
residue, were not found. As shown by its protein substitution potentials in Table 4.11, it can substitute
substantial amounts of conventional hay (equivalent to 5.9 kg hay/kg dried BSFL), but less as a source of
energy (equivalent to 1.5 kg hay/kg dried BSFL).
Facility size and labour need
Dortmans et al. [34] recommends using 35 m2 to handle nine tonnes waste input/week, or 50 m2 for
the nursery and 100 m2 per tonne of incoming waste per day.
In a treatment box, called a larveros
(40x60x17cm), 10,000 larvae can be fed with 15 kg of wet waste (75 % water) for 12 days.
When
consuming in total 12 tonnes fish residue (not mixed with vegetable waste) during 12 days, there is a need
of 800 larveros. According to [34], a nine tonnes of waste input per week requires one worker three times a
week to maintain, thus a setup with one tonne input a day must be similar. The area needed to treat one
tonne incoming fish residue per day is estimated to be 220 m2, which includes space for waste delivering,
storage, packaging etc. (30 m2), office and employees facilities (25 m2) and space for BSFL handling (170
m2 [34]).
Biogas production
General technology description
Biogas, an energy source, and digestate, a liquid fertiliser, is produced during an anaerobic digestion, which
is the process of oxygen free degradation of organic waste. Many different organic waste streams may be
used as inputs, e.g. manure, fruit waste, vegetable waste etc.. The processing tank/ container for anaerobic
digestion is called a digester. The feedstock is fed to microbes that exist in the digester’s initially added
microbe mix (inoculum). By the inoculum, the feedstock is cleaved in different steps under anaerobic
conditions to smaller molecules or building blocks, thereafter converted to acetate, carbon dioxide and
hydrogen, that in the last biochemical step are merged into methane and carbon dioxide, the dominant
molecules in biogas. The biogas output contains a mix of methane (55-70 % volume), carbon dioxide
(33-40 %volume) and small amounts of hydrogen sulphide (2 %volume), water (2-7 %volume), nitrogen
(<2 %)volume, oxygen (<2 %volume), hydrogen (<1 %volume) and ammonia (<0.05 %volume) [138].
49
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:65==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
Methane is the only compound contributing to the energy content of biogas. The caloric value of biogas
(with a methane content of 55-70 %) ranges from 6.0–6.5 kWh/m3. The net caloric value, however,
depends on the efficiency of the appliances. There are multiple uses for biogas: if dewatered and scrubbed
for hydrogen sulphide removal it can run a biogas or a diesel engine (with modifications [104]) for electricity
production, it can be burned directly as a substitute for cooking gas [20] or it can be used as power in gas
mantle lamps to light up streets [138]. Further elaboration on dewatering and scrubbing can be found in
Appendix D.3. Biogas can, if stored correctly, be stored for long periods of time without losing its energy
content, however, if not compressed, the storage volume is large [138]. Depending on production and
usage rate of biogas, there are different options for storage. The common digester types used in low-middle
income countries already have low pressure storage incorporated as additional digester volume. Filling bags
with the biogas is another option that enables sale and transport of the gas [138].
Two operation modes for anaerobic digestion exist dependent on TS content; a wet and dry anaerobic
digestion. The wet operates at a low dry matter content typically below 15 % , whereas the dry mode
operates at dry matter content typically in the range 20-40 % [13].
Currently, the wet mode is used
frequently in low-middle income countries based on available literature, whereas anno 2014 no commercial
used dry digesters have been documented in low-middle income countries [138]. For these reasons, the
wet mode is the only considered mode further investigated. Elaboration on the differences of wet and dry
mode is provided in Appendix D.
Different types of anaerobic digesters for wet digestion have been developed for low-middle income countries
with different efficiency and energy requirements, costs, and maintenance needs. For this project it is
relevant to mention the Chinese fixed dome digester, the Indian floating drum digester and balloon (or
tube) digester. Further elaboration on differences between the digesters is provided in Appendix D. These
types are all used in warm climates, are of relatively low cost, have no or low energy demands, are made
of easily accessible materials, have no heating and stirring requirements and can be quickly implemented.
All three digester types have an inlet pipe, a digester that contains the digester fluid, wherein the chemical
processes for biogas production occurs, and an outlet pipe for both gas collection and one for digestate
collection. Digestate, or bio-slurry, is a liquid by-product from the wet anaerobic digesters which contains
remaining water, nutrients and fibers and is commonly used as a fertiliser if placed close to agriculture.
The digester fluid is sometimes dried to reduce transportation costs of the large water content. In an urban
context, the digestate can be applied in nearby parks and gardens, however is often discharged into local
sewer systems [138].
For biogas production, there are several critical parameters that must be maintained to ensure a high
methane production e.g. feedstock composition, feedstock accessibility, pH, Carbon:Nitrogen (C:N) ratio,
fat content, salinity, water content, temperature, microbe mix, Organic loading rate (OLR) and hydraulic
retention time (HRT) [109]. It is here of relevance to mention that the microbes that produce methane in
the last step of biogas production (Methanogenesis), need a C:N ratio of roughly 25–30 for optimal methane
production. Excess nitrogen can be converted to free ammonia and cause ammonia inhibition [109]. In
order to solve the issue of a low C:N ratio, an additional carbon source can be added in the form of e.g.
vegetables or fruit waste or the ammonia concentrations can be diluted to certain levels for inhibition not
to happen [60, 152]. Ammonium concentrations are dependent on pH value in the digestate [72], therefore
it is of importance to surveil pH during anaerobic digestion. High salinity of input material has too shown
to be disadvantageous for anaerobic digestion, however can be solved by microbe adaption and low OLR
[80]. The ORL is the amount of volatile solids that is feed into the digester daily. In a non-continuously
stirred reactor, it is recommended for the OLR to stay below 2 g VS/L reactor/day, otherwise overloading
of the system may occur[138]. The remaining parameters are elaborated in Appendix D.
A benefit of biogas production is that it does not require any energy input, and if scrubbed, it can be
incorporated in existing diesel engines, with modifications, for cheap energy production [104]. Furthermore,
if other organic waste streams are available they can be included, also manures, and it can thereby function
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
50
==PAGE:66==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
as a waste treatment for multiple types of organic wastes simultaneously. A disadvantage of anaerobic
digestion is that it requires surveillance and knowledge on the chemical process and adjustable parameters
to obtain maximal biogas yields. Furthermore, import of materials may be needed for the setups, and
experts may be needed for design and skilled construction.
Applied technology
A simplified setup of biogas production is chosen for technical review. An overview of the considered step of
the technical review is shown in Figure 4.14. The one tonne fish residues and required water was considered
the only input for anaerobic digestion. A wet processing mode was applied and the only considered output
was biogas, which was expected to be used as either cooking fuel or fuel for electricity production, thereby
either substituting diesel or liquefied petroleum gas. Digestate was not a considered output for the review
section, but calculations hereof are provided in Appendix D, Appendix D.3.
