Design specification and processing time
There are multiple options for process design, e.g. different digester types, different water contents and
using co-digestion or not. An overview of included processes considered in the biogas technology review is
shown in Figure 4.15. Electric grinding is included as parts of the fish residues may be of larger particle
sizes, e.g. the fish heads. A floating drum design is initially suggested and is fed semi-continuously with
the grinded fish residues and unspecified water or wastewater with app. 100 % moisture content once a
day. The temperature in Somaliland suits methane production well as specific types of digesters operate
efficiently at mesophilic ranges (30–40 ◦C) and there is therefore no need for additional insulation or heating
of the floating drum. As recommended in tropical climate with an average ambient temperature of 25–30
◦C, as in Somaliland [138], the HRT is set to be 30 days for the floating drum. A scrubbing unit is added
since it is necessary for fuel production, along with a biogas engine, which could be substituted by their
own modified diesel engine. Digestate is not considered in the review. However, it may be offered free of
charge as wet fertiliser to nearby parks and agriculture, however, main focus is on biogas and its yields.
Yield and substitution potential
The theoretical methane potential of the fish residues, based on Equation A.8, is for pure fish residues
0.54 L CH4/ g Volatile solids (VS). Theoretical methane potentials are, however, rarely never reached in
reality. This can be caused by the high protein content of fish residues (Table 4.5), thereby a low C:N ratio
and from this a risk of producing large concentrations of free N-ammonia when the protein is degraded
in the anaerobic digester. Literature was scrutinized to determine realistic methane production from fish
residue anaerobic digestion. This indicated that the practical methane yields from anaerobic digestion of
fish residues were substantially below the theoretical values.
Mono-digestion studies of fish residue anaerobic digestion indicate that when the C:N ratio is below recom-
mended range, the lower the TS %, the higher the methane potential from fish residue anaerobic digestion.
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
52
==PAGE:68==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
However, none of the mono-digested fish residue studies reached the theoretical potential calculated: A cur-
rent functioning floating drum in India, Heeb [60] found a specific gas production of 0.27 L CH4/g VS when
fish waste along with small amounts of vegetable waste was digested with total solids (TS) concentration
of 1 % and when digestate was recycled. In a experimental setup, Eiroa et al. [37] reached a specific gas
production of 0.18-0.28 L CH4/g VS and a VS removal of 66-81 % when tuna residues were mono-digested
with a C:N ratio of 6 and a TS concentration of 5 % and 1 %, respectively.Co-digestion of fish residues with
other carbon-rich wastes streams showed more promising results than if mono-digested. Akshaya and Jacob
[6] studied the experimental methane potentials of mono- and co-digestion of fish residues with vegetable
waste and found that mono-digestion produced 0.373 L CH4/g VSadded when digested with a C:N ratio
of 11 and TS concentration of <1 %. With co-digestion the production instead reached 0.463 L CH4/g
VSadded when co-digested 1:1 with a C:N ratio of 27 and TS concentration <1 %. Bouallagui et al. [24]
found in their experimental setup, that fish residues co-digested with fruit and vegetable waste at a C:N
ratio of 27.6, and a TS concentration of 2.8 % , produced a 0.32 L CH4/g VSadded and removed 73 % VS.
Mshandete et al. [82] furthermore found that fish waste landings from Tanzania experimentally co-digested
1:2 with sisal pulp, reaching a TS of 17 % and a C:N ratio of 16, obtained a methane yield of 0.62 L
CH4/g VSadded, which was substantially higher than their found methane production of mono-digestion of
fish waste at 5 % TS and C:N ratio of 9 (0.39 L CH4/g VSadded). Thus, is possible to digest pure fish
residues if they are diluted, however, with a lower methane potential compared to co-digested fish residues.
Salinity of fish residues and problematic pH caused by its anaerobic digestion were not considered problem-
atic for the present project, as Heeb [60] and Mshandete et al. [82], who fed fish residues to the digester
from fish landings presumably from the sea, did not registrar issues. A high pH was observed by Heeb [60],
but could be caused by large amounts of digestate re-circulating, thereby salt and ammonia accumulation.
Additionally, Mshandete et al. [82] did not report issues with salinity concentrations from the fish residues.
The setup by Heeb [60] was used for inspiration for the setup reviewed as it is the only example of anaerobic
digestion of fish residues in real life. Furthermore, their yields align with experimental findings by Eiroa
et al. [37]. If the fish residue generated by FairFishing’s partners are diluted to 1.0 % TS and assuming a
specific gas production of 0.27 L CH4/g VSadded, and a methane constitution of 67 % in the biogas, 130
m3 biogas can be produced daily from an input of one tonne fish residues, as summarized in Table 4.13.
If diluted to 1.0 % TS, the water mass demand is 37 times higher than fish residue mass input. This
can be problematic in a country that periodically suffer from severe droughts. Instead of using clean fresh
water, water can be obtained from other waste sources, that are not chemically contaminated. Another
option is to occasionally re-circulate for shorter periods to save water. Re-circulation is, however, not a
sustainable long-term solution without co-digestion, as the ammonia will accumulate in the digester. If
using co-digestion this results in less excess nitrogen in the digestate and reduces the need for dilution as
excess nitrogen is reduced. It is possible to increase the TS concentration to 15 % if the feedstock has no
inhibitory affect. In that case only app. 1.5 m3 water should be added daily. It is therefore advised to look
into if there are other waste streams in the area with high carbon content that can be co-digested with the
fish residues and help reducing the water demand for dilution.
The overall substitution potential for the given setup equals 78 L diesel per day or 27 m3 liquefied petroleum
gas per day. Digestate was not the focus of this study and will not be considered further in the following
sections, however, rough calculations hereof were made and can be found in Appendix D, Appendix D.3.
Facility size and labour need
In the following section, a floating drum setup is evaluated to determine facility size and labour needs.
Applying a hydraulic retention time of 30 days, adding water, or potentially wastewater with close to 100
% moisture content, to reach dilution to 1.0 % TS will require 37 m3 water daily, and a digester volume
of 1,600 m3 when 25 % is incorporated for biogas storage. Assuming a height:diameter ratio of 2 and
distributing the required volume between eight floating drums, this will take up 160 m2 just for ground
area digester space thereafter additional 60 m2 for space between the digesters and extra 55 m2 designated
53
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:69==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
for e.g. unloading waste and for staff facilities, e.g. toilet and office building, this sums up in a total area
requirement of 280 m2. Digesters of these sizes are rarely used in low-middle income countries [138] and
floating drums in those sizes may not be obtainable. Ways to reduce the size include increasing the TS
concentration to maximum 15 % however with the risk of producing inhibitory concentrations of lipid and
nitrogen.
From Heeb [61], one full time person was hired to do ongoing operation and guard a 25 m3 floating drum
in India [60]. If multiple drums are placed adjacent to each other, it is here assumed that one person
can surveil and operate two drums. Thus, a drum quantity of eight required for one tonne fish residues
treatment requires four workers.
If large amounts of fish residues are produced centrally or all collected to be treated at one location, a large
scale commercial design will provide a better option than multiple small digesters. The small scale solutions
are not suitable as they require large space demands and additional costs for building and loading into each
digester. Tube digester units combined to reach the desired digester volume digester may also be an option,
as they are the only type that are scaleable and where units can be removed and added as pleased. One
single digester may lower the construction costs but suppliers must be contacted for specialized volumes.
Biochar/Charcoal production
General technology description
Biochar and charcoal are solid amorphous (non-crystalline), carbonised materials formed from pyrolysis of
organic compounds containing lignin and lignocellulose [76, 107]. Charcoal can be used as a fuel for e.g.
cooking and biochar as a fertiliser. The difference between the product names is due to their different
application (end use) and physio-chemical properties- biochar is desired to have small pores in order to retain
capillary soil water and charcoal is desired with a low ash content for optimal fuel efficiency. Carbonisation
of fish residues to produce charcoal, requires that the material is mixed with other organic waste streams
as e.g. food waste (vegetables), as the ash content will otherwise be too high and not suitable for burning
[43]. A carbonisation process using a drum kiln, converts biomass into a carbonaceous material during slow
pyrolysis on a dry material, under oxygen limited conditions [105]. A range of different pyrolysis reactor
types and conditions exists, which gives different outputs and have different advantages and disadvantages.
The different types of pyrolysis technologies have a higher or lower level of technical requirements [107].
A low tech pyrolysis method, which can be handled by unskilled workers and which is used in low- and
middle income countries, e.g. Africa, is pyrolysis by use of drum kilns [118, 123]. In the drum kiln, dry
biomass is converted to char by heating of the material under absence of oxygen, hence no burning of the
material. Emissions from pyrolysis processes is mainly methane, hydrogen, carbon monoxide and carbon
dioxide [107]. Using simpler and less controlled processes as drum kilns, one should be aware that poisonous
emissions as e.g. carbon monoxide are formed. This should be considered and proper ventilation is hence
important [43]. After the pyrolysis process, the materials is minced and mixed for thereafter to be formed
into char briquettes by adding a binding unit.
Charcoal or biochar offers a way of utilising organic material for energy (i.e. cooking) or fertiliser use. It
is a simple technology that can be handled by unskilled workers and it has relatively low space demands.
The facility is not sensitive to variations in waste quantities (i.e. due to yearly variations) and can easily
be up- and down scaled by adjusting the number of drum kilns in operation.
Some disadvantages of
charcoal/biochar production are that it requires input with low ash content, additional fuel and a binding
unit.
Applied technology
A simplified setup of charcoal production is chosen for technical review. An overview of the considered step
of the technical review is shown in Figure 4.16. The one tonne fish residues, vegetable waste and a binding

unit was considered as input. From the pyrolysis process both biochar and charcoal can be produced.
Only charcoal was considered in the review, as charcoal has the highest value for substitution. The output
charcoal was expected to be used as cooking fuel to substitute conventional coal, that commonly are wood
based in Somaliland.

RESULTS & DISCUSSION
Design specification and processing time
The suggested process design for use in Somaliland can be seen in Figure 4.16. The key process is a low
technology drum kiln where biochar is formed during slow pyrolysis. The input to the drum kiln must be dry,
hence the first step is drying of the fish residues. The suggested technology for drying is by a solar tunnel
dryer where energy from the sun is utilised in a greenhouse for drying. The drying process is estimated to
take three days including the delivery and placement of fish for drying. After the drying process, the dry
material is carbonised in a simple pyrolysis process by using drum kilns. As suggested in [43] this process
takes six hours in total, including three hours pyrolysis and three hours cooling. One drum kiln can hence
be run twice a day. The output from the pyrolysis can be used directly as biochar. As it is suggested to
be used as charcoal, the biochar is grounded, put in a rotating drum or a press where a binding unit (e.g.
molasses) to form charcoal. This is in total estimated to take half a day. After the charcoal is formed it
needs drying in the sun before it is ready to use [108]. It is estimated that the charcoal’s last drying process
takes two days including packaging. The total processing time of charcoal making is thereby estimated
to six days. Note that if biochar is produced instead, the charcoal drying step can be excluded and the
process only takes 3.5 days.
Yield and substitution potential
In order to use the output material from the pyrolysis as charcoal, the ash content must be between 3.0
and 10 %, optimally in the low range [43]. The calculated ash content in fish residues exceeds this value
(Section 4.2.2) and mixing with other waste streams is required. In order to obtain a total ash content
of 8.1 % TS in the final charcoal, mixing with vegetables with an ash content of 5.2 % TS [26] was
considered. Here, a mixing ratio of 1:4.2 of fish and vegetables waste was obtained. It must by noted here
that, as discussed in Section 4.2.2, the actual ash content in the waste in the ”proof-of-concept” solution
may be lower than the one presented in 4.5 which has been used in the calculation. This suggests that less
vegetables in reality is needed in the mixing. Drum kiln pyrolysis that generate yields in the range 23-33
% TS are considered good quality [43]. Assuming a yield of 33 % gave 130 kg TS charcoal per tonne fish
residues or 440 kg TS char per day, when other organic materials were included in the ratio 1:4.2. In reality,
charcoal will absorb some water from the surrounding air and the actual weight will be a little higher than
the TS yields presented. FAO [43] suggests a water content of 10 % in a commercially used charcoal used
in Brazil. With a water content of 10 %, the actual total yield is 490 kg charcoal per day.
Facility size and labour needs
The facility size is estimated to be approximately 170 m2 in total, including 30 m2 of fish residue drying,
43 m2 of drum kilns space, 15 m2 for rotating drums, 30 m2 of charcoal drying, 30 m2 of delivery, storage,
packaging, as well as 25 m2 for office and employee facilities. FAO [43] estimates that one full time worker
can produce 300 kg TS charcoal per day from drum kilns. Using this estimate, between 1.5 and 2 workers
are needed to load, control and monitor the pyrolysis drum kilns to produce the 490 kg char per day. It
is estimated that 1.5 workers in total is needed for both placing the fish residues for drying, serving the
rotating drums, placing the final charcoal for drying and lastly pack it. It is thereby estimated that a total
of three to four workers are needed per day.
Compost production
General technology description
Composting is a degradation and stabilisation of organic matter in solid state performed by aerobic mi-
croorganisms.
The active microorganisms in composting are bacteria, actinomycetes and fungi, but other animals as
composting worms and beetles are also active in the process. These microorganisms thrive best under
different environmental conditions, as temperature, oxygen levels and pH and degrade different parts of
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
56
==PAGE:72==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
the organic material. An extensive amount of different nutrients are important in order to obtain optimal
degradation, but the most important factor is the carbon (C) and nitrogen (N) balance. Too much N (low
C:N ratio) leads to ammonia formation that can cause odours and means loss of nutrients. Lack of N
(high C:N ratio) leads to inhibition of the process (i.e. slow process), as N is essential for microbial growth.
The optimal starting range varies, but is in literature suggested to be 20-35 [26, 58]. Composting is an
exothermic reaction, which means that energy is released from the process. The energy that is not utilised
by the microorganisms is released as heat, which leads to heating of the compost piles during the process.
During the composting process, organic matter is sanitised and stabilised. The input material which is
often heterogeneous, unsanitised, smelly organic waste with a high water content will be transformed to
compost; a more homogeneous, sanitised, non smelly and dry substance [26].
Compost can be used as fertilisers as it can be beneficial for plant growth [26]. It can provide the soil with
organic nitrogen, phosphorus, and potassium as well as essential micronutrients. It increases the water
holding capacity, increases aeration and lowers surface crusting.Lastly, it is able to strengthen the microbial
population. Compost can furthermore substitute the use of chemical fertilisers and pesticides [113].
Various composting technologies exist, varying in scale from backyard composting, to simple windrow
composting (piling of organic matter in long rows), to more controlled windrow composting, which can
either be open or enclosed with different degrees of complexity. The level of complexity depends on the
given technology used (i.e. mechanical turning of piles by manpower or machines) and which parameters,
such as moisture content, oxygen supply, and temperature, that are controlled [26]. It must be evaluated
which technology is the most suitable depending on the situation. A way to easily control odours is by
substantial control of the process in e.g. enclosed facilities. Highly controlled and enclosed facilities imply
higher operation costs, which is generally not preferred in less developed countries as i.e. Somalia. Here
simpler and more labour intensive solutions will often be the preferred solution [26].
In order to ensure a good quality compost that can be used as a fertiliser, the material must live up
to some defined standards for sanitation, and content limits of heavy metals and organic pollutants [26,
81]. Standards vary between countries, where less regulations are often the case in developing countries.
Validation of compost quality can however prompt a higher value of the product [137]. If non-mature
or non-stabilised compost is applied to agricultural land, it may be phytotoxic (poisonous) to crops [90].
Hence, it is important that the maturity of the compost material is well monitored. As by suggested by
Ofosu-Budu et al. [90], an effective way to monitor maturity of a compost material, is by germination index
tests.
Compost offers a way of recycling nutrients while improving water holding capacity of soils. The technology
level is easily adjustable to specific needs and can be made very simple with low maintenance and investment
costs. It can furthermore easily be scaled up and down depending on the input quantity. A disadvantage is,
though, that potentially other waste streams needs to be added to increase C:N ratio and that composting
in general is relatively space and labour demanding.

For compost production from fish residues in Somaliland, a simplified setup was chosen for technical review.
An overview of the considered step of the technical review is shown in Figure 4.17. The one tonne fish
residues, along with bark and seaweed were considered as input. From the composting process, compost
is produced as output. The compost was expected to be used as fertiliser to substitute the conventional
commercial fertiliser ADNOC UREA.

RESULTS & DISCUSSION
Design specification and processing time
Figure 4.17 illustrates the processing steps considered. Before the input streams are placed in the com-
posting piles, some pre-treatment is needed in order to obtain a properly prepared input material. Mixing
is here essential, but most certainly grinding will as well favour the process. This is assumed to take less
than one day, so that all material entering the facility is placed for composting the same day.
The composting technology suggested is open windrow composting with manually turned piles. In order to
prevent environmental pollution and nutrient washout, windrows should be placed on impermeable surfaces
and if possible be sheltered. Building a shelter to hold back water by decreasing evaporation is an option,
however excluded for the review section due to the large space demand, however flooring (plastic liner)
and drainage systems are included to reduce and control leaching. The facility should be placed up hill,
in order to avoid accumulation of water around the piles during heavy rain. This will prevent unnecessary
nutrient washout. In the case of fish residue composting, an important factor that should be considered is
potential odours. Yepsen [153] showed that odours from outdoor fish residue composting in open windrows
can be controlled and limited substantially by use of a biofilter; a soil layer placed on top of the windrows.
A similar method is here suggested for treatment of Somaliland fish residues, hence a simple biofilter made
of a thin compost layer which is placed over the fresh piles. During the composting process, monitoring
of oxygen, temperature and moisture content is important, in order to obtain an efficient conversion of
biomass [38]. A way to obtain a sufficient oxygen supply, is by regular turning of the piles. As suggested
by López-Mosquera et al. [75], the compost should be turned once a week in the first month and once
every second week the last two months of composting. The total composting process is assumed to takes
four months, including maturation, as suggested by López-Mosquera et al. [75]. Some post-treatment may
be relevant, i.e. for removal of potential impurities. When the final product is stable and ready, it can be
packed and sold for use as a fertiliser. Alternatively, if sold in bigger portions, the compost can be picked
up by companies or private persons in i.e. trailers, where no packaging is needed. A germination index test
is suggested for composting of fish residues in Somaliland.
Yield and substitution potential
Fish residues must be composted together with other organic materials, due to a too low C:N ratio in fish
residues, and the specific mixing ratio is thereby dependent on the C:N ratio of the composted materials.
López-Mosquera et al. [75] composted fish residues together with seaweed and pine bark in an open facility
with mechanically turned windrows. The study was performed in Spain with a volume based mixing ratio
of 1:1:3 of fish, seaweed and pine bark respectively. The technology used in their study is similar to the
composting technology suggested for treatment of fish residues in Somaliland, and the study was thereby
used to estimate yields and substitution potentials for the open composting facility suggested for fish
residues in Somaliland. The potential composting facility in Somaliland is likely to be co-composted with
vegetable waste, which has a C:N ratio corresponding to the one of seaweed, see Christensen [26] and
López-Mosquera et al. [75]. But seaweed is also a potential possibility, as Somaliland has a long coastline
from where seaweed can potentially be sourced. This has not been confirmed from local data collection and
thus needs further investigation. A material with a much higher C:N ratio than vegetables and seaweed is
needed in order to decrease the fraction of seaweed/vegetable waste in the final mix. This could potentially
be pine bark, but a material easily sourced in the area with a high C:N ratio should be used, and hence the
actual mixing ratio will depend on that. Yields have been estimated from the volume based mixing ratio of
1:1:3 and reduction volumes found in literature: López-Mosquera et al. [75] found a reduction volume of 70
%. Ofosu-Budu et al. [90] also composted fish residues. In this study fish residues were composted together
with banana peals, municipal solid waste (MSW) and slaughterhouse waste. Supporting the findings from
López-Mosquera et al. [75], Ofosu-Budu et al. [90] obtained reduction volumes of 50 % to 60 %. Applying
reduction volumes of 50 % - 70 % in present technology review resulted in a total yield of 4.3 m3 to 7.1
m3 compost per day or 130 m3 to 210 m3 per month. The fertiliser potential found in López-Mosquera
et al. [75] was 2.1 %, 0.6 % and 0.7 % NPK, and a similar potential is expected if the same materials in
the given ratios are used. The conventional Somaliland fertiliser, ADNOC UREA, contains 46 % Nitrogen,
59
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:75==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
and is thus much more concentrated than the compost. The substitution potential of ADNOC UREA was
therefore only 46 kg commercial fertiliser per one tonne compost. It is often difficult to sell compost and
there is a risk that this could also be the case for Somaliland.
Facility size and labour need
With the given mixing ratio, a reduction volume of 70 % and a composting time of four months suggested
by López-Mosquera et al. [75], the facility size is estimated to be 1500 m2. This includes 30 m2 for waste
delivery, storage, packaging, 25 m2 for office and employee facilities, 860 m2 windrow area for composing
the first two months, and 560 m2 windrow area the last two months. These areas are calculated based
on piles with a height of 2.0 m, and a width of 4.0 m.
The piles should not be placed in one long
line, and hence in reality additional area is needed between the piles. This area has not been included in
the calculations, but should be included when a final design is ready. Due to the degradation of organic
matter, the volume of the composting materials decreases over time. In order to reduce the total volume
of the facility, the composting materials are placed in new piles after two months. Due to the degradation,
the composing area for the last two months is less than the one for the first two month of degradation.
The windrow area has been calculated assuming linear degradation rates, meaning that after the two first
months, the reduction volume of the material in the piles is reduced by half of the 70 %, hence by 35 %.
Placing the composting materials in new piles every month instead of every second month means a total
reduction volume of the facility of 150 m2. Using a reduction volume of 50 %, suggested by Ofosu-Budu
et al. [90], gives a total facility size approximately 100 m2 less than if the 70 % reduction volume is used.
In conclusion, various parameters influence the total facility size.
Using a mixing ratio of 1:1:3 of fish, seaweed and pine bark, a reduction volume of 70 %, and placement
of compost in new piles after two months, means a total labour need of 49 persons per day, for receiving
of one tonne fish residues and turning of 32 tonnes of waste per day. It is estimated from the assumption
made by [33] that three workers can prepare and turn two tonnes of waste per day.
In order to determine the final design of a big scale composting facility for fish residue composting in
Somaliland, a pilot scale project is advised to be carried out with the available materials for co-composting
determining the exact mixing ratios and hence the total volume composted as well as the nutrient compo-
sition. The volume reduction, and the nutrient composition in the final compost must be determined for
this specific case, in order to calculate the total facility size, and the substitution potential. The actual
labour needed is dependent on the actual volume composted and the reduction volume, and must as well
be determined more precisely when a pilot scale project has been carried out.
4.3.2
Technology ranking and selection
For selection of two management options to further assess, all options were ranked and discussed as basis
for the final selection. The technologies were ranked on a scale from 1 to 5, where 1 is best and 5 is
worst, whereas weighing scores to each category were given on a range from 1-12, where 12 is given to
the most important category for FairFishing. Results of the ranking and weighting as well as the weighting
percentages are showed in Table 4.16. A large effort was made into ranking the technologies with scores
where justification of each ranking and weighting score is found in Appendix D.4.
For simplicity, the
technologies are in the following sections addressed as their individual outputs with capital letter: Current
management, Fish meal, Fish silage, BSFL, Biogas, Charcoal and Compost.

RESULTS & DISCUSSION
Technical challenges: Under the theme Technical challenges, as shown in Table 4.16, Biogas scores
highest (poorest), followed by Compost and Fish meal. In general, the categories under the mentioned
theme weigh low, except for Technology level. In the Technology level category, Fish meal and Biogas are
the most complicated technologies: Fish meal is an advanced electrical installation with multiple processes
included in a multi-unit machinery, whereas Biogas is a multi-chemical process where surveillance and
knowledge on the processes are required to ensure a steady output of biogas. The other categories under
the theme Technical challenges include Climate sensitivity, Experience and interest in East Africa, Need of
co-collection, Nuisance from facility and Transportability of output. Overall, Biogas and Compost are here
found most climate sensitive, as they have specific climate conditions for optimal production, e.g. biogas
production is affected by temperature variations, whereas composting with an outside open windrow setup
is easily affected by heavy rain, droughts and even high temperatures. All technologies are, however, used in
warm tropical climates, and therefore the climate sensitive category also weighs low, as all options are found
to function under the given climate conditions. Charcoal and Compost, as they need a specific composition
and structure that cannot be obtained solely from fish residues, depend on other waste streams too. As
there are additional logistics associated with accessing other waste, this was found disadvantageous for the
technologies. In contrast, Fish silage and Current management overall both received the least scores under
the theme Technical challenges; they are both suitable in the Somaliland climate and are found to be the
easiest technologies to operate. One parameter that make Fish silage stand out is, however, that it is not
an established technology and is not widely used, as shown in the category Experience and use in East
Africa, despite its clear advantages.
Capital and maintenance Costs: The theme Capital and Maintenance Costs covers the category Con-
struction, material and maintenance costs, and shows which technologies require largest initial investments
for FairFishing; Biogas and Fish meal, followed by Charcoal. High scores have been given to technologies
where import and special technicians are needed for setup. Only for Fish meal, Biogas and Charcoal was
import considered for certain materials, otherwise for all materials were otherwise assumed bought locally.
Fish meal, as it demands a roofed building, installation and purchase of foreign equipment and machinery,
has been estimated to have the highest construction costs. As biogas production and installation is not
common procedure in Somaliland, foreign hired technicians or consultants should be hired for setup. With
the suggested eight floating drums (200 m3 each), needed to treat one tonne fish residues daily, from
Section 4.3.1, each drum has to be bought, imported and installed, and therefore Biogas is considered
costly to implement at the given scale. They have therefore received same score. Current management,
that is overall one of the best options under the mentioned theme, is naturally the option with the lowest
capital costs for FairFishing, as it is an option that is already constructed and implemented.
Operation costs: Overall, under the current theme, Biogas scores worst, followed by Fish meal and Fish
silage. Current management scores best. Under the theme Operation Costs, the categories that have
highest weights are the ones that indicate if additional inputs of water and fuel are needed, as they are
associated with the highest additional cost and logistics. Lower weights are given to the categories Labour
needs and Space requirements. Biogas production demands a certain amount of water for dilution of one
tonne fish residues. According to Obara [88], and a natural result of a country frequently hit by draughts,
water is a scarce resource in Somaliland. Therefore, both for the sake of keeping costs down and using
human consumable water in a sustainable manner, Biogas is here a poor choice, unless other water resources
are found (black water, drainage water etc.), or if re-circulation can be performed without accumulating
the inhibitory nitrogen levels. However, even procuring wastewaters in the needed amounts can be an issue
in countries with poor sanitation and uncontrollably discharged wastewaters. Fish meal, Fish silage and
Current management are options that need purchase of external fuel input for operation (electricity and/or
diesel). Diesel is found a costly commodity in Somaliland. The conducted surveys during this project’s local
data collection, see Section 4.2.1, showed that the majority of the asked fishers, mongers and restaurants
wish a lower price on this specific product, which illustrates the dependence on the product and how the
prices are perceived. Labour needs here illustrate costs, and therefore, the lower the labour demand, the
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
62
==PAGE:78==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
better. A thing to be aware of though, which has not been considered in the score giving, is that a project
that can create jobs and secure income for the citizens is considered beneficial and desired for the local
community and government, as unemployment is high in Somaliland. This too can be beneficial for general
NGO reputation and cooperation with the mentioned stakeholders. In these manners, the category could
in fact be turned into a positive parameter, but was not included here due to the project’s aim of assessing
economic viability. All the options do as minimum require labour for unloading, surveillance and packing of
the products. The option with the highest labour demands is Compost. Compost, as the suggested option
does not include mechanical turning, but rather manual, asks for the highest labour force of 48 people
per tonne fish that daily enters the facility. Purchasing the space was not a large concern for FairFishing
according to their weighing scores. Therefore, despite Compost, due to long processing time, is only to
a low degree penalised for having large space requirement. In fact, after Current management, Compost
scores bests, as the categories it scores poorly in (labour need and space requirements), weight low.
When it comes to operation costs, landfilling, in the Current management, scores best. This since it is
low on labour demand, has low to no manual handling of the waste once that enters the landfill site,
despite occasionally burning, and does not need any additional inputs. The Current management has been
guesstimated to have the largest space requirements due to its current setup where the landfills do not
close, but expand with time. However, as Current management is not a cost for FairFishing, since they
do not manage the landfills in Somaliland, this options naturally reflects the best choice in terms of low
operation costs. Thus, for keeping ongoing operation costs for FairFishing low, not treating fish residues
in new manners is preferred.
Revenue: Revenue covers all aspects associated with earnings or savings from the generated products sale
or internal use at FairFishing’s station. It includes FairFishing savings and sales income and Time before
first revenue, with the former weighing highest. The revenues are rough estimates, where only up-to-date
unit selling prices in Somaliland were obtained for few of the commodities. Fish meal, fish silage, BSFL
and compost unit selling prices were either estimated or obtained from literature. From the estimates, Fish
meal and Fish silage can potentially produce the highest income for FairFishing. Internal use of charcoal for
conventional charcoal substitution, and external compost selling, offers a lower saving or income compared
to Fish meal and Fish silage. Internal use of biogas for diesel substitution and BSFL production sold as
animal fodder were found to generate savings or income, but lower compared to previous mentioned options.
As Fish meal and Fish silage in addition have a comparably short processing time, they offer the fastest
and highest potential incomes, with Fish meal scoring slightly better. Current management, as it does not
generate a sell-able product, is overall the worst performing technology in the categories associated with
revenue.
Sustainability:As FairFishing wish to make their activities more sustainable, which is one of the drivers for
this project, the category Waste hierarchy (Sustainability) is rated as the second most important category.
As mentioned in the score definition, see Appendix D.4, the ranking is not supported by a Life Cycle
Assessment study, but is instead based on the waste hierarchy which ranks waste reduction in the top and
landfilling in the bottom. Current management does not utilise any of the resources in the fish residues
and it is hence the least sustainable option. The feeding options are the most sustainable options and must
hence be preferred from a sustainability perspective. Biogas and Charcoal are the second preferred options,
and thereafter Compost, following the waste hierarchy.
Demand: Demand is considered the most important theme, as it includes Internal FairFishing use, Local
demand and Market volume. Those categories are therefore given high weighing scores. The driving force
for FairFishing to initiate this project is to use the product internally.
The weight of this category is
therefore the highest of all categories. Biogas is the technology best suited for internal use in FairFishing,
and as this category weights 20 % of all categories, this is one of the reasons of Biogas being one of the
favourable options in general and the reason why it is scoring best in terms of demand. Charcoal can be
used internally in FairFishing for cooking classes, but as this is only a small share of FairFishing’s energy
63
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:79==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
need, Biogas is still preferred over Charcoal. Both Charcoal and Biogas performs well in terms of Local
demand and Market volume. Current management, as no product is produced, is performing worst in all
categories under the theme Demand, followed by Compost which cannot be used internally in FairFishing
and is also scoring low in terms of local demand. The fodder options are not usable internally in FairFishing,
but the local demand by farmers is, however, very high during droughts, and the market volume is also
relatively large due to an extensive livestock sector. As the category Internal use in FairFishing is weighting
highest, the feed options, however, only performs averagely in terms of demand.
Selected management options
Of the suggested management options, two are selected for further specification and economic assessment.
It is evident from the weighted sums shown in Table 4.16, that three management options stands out;
Biogas, Fish silage, and Charcoal are ranked relatively even in the top three with 136, 137 and 139 points,
respectively. The top fourth ranked solution is BSFL with a score of 150. The weighted sum of Fish meal,
Compost and Current management are all scoring 160 or more and these are hence ranked as the bottom
three solutions and not selected for further analysis.
In the top four ranked solutions, two fodder and two energy options are placed. It was decided to go with
one energy and one feed option, in order to investigate two very different solutions; an energy solution
that can be used internally in FairFishing, and a fodder solution that is a more sustainable option and is
satisfying a local demand as well.
The weighted sum of Charcoal is three points higher than Biogas, and these two options are hence ranked
almost equally good, with Biogas performing slightly better. Biogas was selected as the energy option for
the further analysis, owing to different reasons: it is the top priority for FairFishing, because it satisfies the
need of internal use best; it ranks over Charcoal; and there is a high data availability about the topic. It
should, however, be noted that, choosing Biogas over Charcoal gives some technical challenges. The most
important aspects where Biogas performs worse than Charcoal, is: the sensitivity to the current climate
conditions; the complexity of the technology; the rather high set up and operation expenses; the high water
input; and the potentially lower income. Charcoal actually scores better or the same in most categories,
except of Need of co-collection, Need of continuous other non-feed inputs, Internal use in FairFishing and
Local demand. As the Internal use in FairFishing is the highest counting category, Biogas, overall, scores
better than Charcoal.
The two feed option candidates to choose between are Fish silage and BSFL. Fish silage is a clear favourite
between the two, considering the weighted ranking and the fact that Fish silage scores better or the same
as BSFL in all categories except Experience in east Africa and Need of continuous other non-feed inputs.
These categories, however, are not considered of high relevance by either authors nor FairFishing. One
point worth considering when deciding one of the fodder options is the yield, which is also reflected in
the sales income. The Fish silage dry yield is almost 10 times the dry yield for BSFL. As the purpose of
producing animal fodder for this project is to produce a valuable product, and not only to serve as a size
reduction, this difference is substantial. The fodder potential is hence much higher for Fish silage than
for BSFL. The only real obstacle in choosing Fish silage is the availability of good quality and up-to-date
literature, and the fact that Fish silage is not an established commodity. The many advantages of Fish
silage were found to weigh out the limited data availability. Fish silage was hence decided as the feeding
option to continue with, in the believe that enough high-quality data is accessible.
Differences between fish silage and biogas production
Fish silage and Biogas are two very different options, providing two very different outputs: Animal fodder
which is not usable internally in FairFishing but is highly needed by the local community in periods during
droughts, and energy that can be used internally in FairFishing. The two options are also very different in
terms of construction, operation, technical challenges, costs, and demand. Biogas and Fish silage scores
differently in all categories. Biogas is a worse option than Fish silage in all categories associated with
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
64
==PAGE:80==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
Technical challenges, except the experience and interest in the East Africa category. Biogas, hence, got
some more technical challenges than Fish silage, but is, however, well established and known, as well as
of high interest in East Africa. For categories associated with Capital and maintenance costs, as well
as categories describing Revenue, Biogas performs worse than Fish silage, except in the categories about
continuous other non-feed inputs and fuel, and labour need, as Fish silage needs acid or a fermenting
unit to initiate the process and external fuel for a grinding process. Biogas, however, requires substantial
amounts of water, has higher construction, material and maintenance costs, and generates less revenue.
Additionally, Fish silage is a more sustainable option than Biogas. For demand, Biogas performs better than
Fish silage in all categories, as it satisfies FairFishing’s needs as well as local demand all year round, and has
the largest market volume. The demand for fish silage is high in periods during droughts, but is, however,
less than the demand for biogas, due to the demand only being rare. After weighting the categories, the
two technologies are both ranked in the top, and these two very different options are decided to be the
best options for FairFishing.
4.4
Proof-of-concept scenario formation
The following sections show the results of combining the data inventory and technology screening to form
a scope, a functional unit, and two scenario options, in a proof-of-concept scale, for the selected fish
treatments technologies.
For simplicity, when referring to the technologies, they are addressed as the
capital letter of their output: Fish silage and Biogas.
4.4.1
Scope and functional unit
The scope was chosen based on fish residue mass-flow and sources; all fish residue generated at Berbera
station and Berbera market from 2021, as the implementation of the treatment options can at the earliest
start that year. The scope was centered around a small geographical area; Berbera station and Berbera
market, as FairFishing desires a ”proof-of-concept solution”. These two fish residue access points are the
ones which generate largest quantities in Berbera, see Section 4.2.2. As Berbera market is located in
close proximity to the fish station and is able to produce a centralised large quantity of fish residue, and
possibly other organic waste, it offers a solution with low collection needs from scattered sources. Collecting
organic waste from restaurants and households has been excluded for these reasons. FairFishing does not
manage Berbera market and does not currently manage Berbera station, therefore, the collaboration with
stakeholders in charge of these two areas (Mongers and Somaliland Ministry of Livestock and Fisheries
Development) is of high importance.
The selection of Berbera station and market for the ”proof-of-
concept” solution was hence made due to the large quantities of fish residue, which can be collected
within a manageable area, whereby costly labour and fuel demanding collection can be avoided. With the
chosen scope, the final unit was decided on. The final functional unit was set to be treatment of the
projected fish residue generation in the year 2021 from Berbera Station and Market (640 kg),
with the projected increase from year 2021-2030 as provided in section 4.2.2.
As only Berbera waste generated at the station and at markets will be considered in the ”proof-of-concept”
solution, the amount of guts, heads and fins are in reality higher than the amounts used in the calculations.
This since gutting mainly takes place at these two points. For the ”proof-of-concept” solution this means
that the ash content, which is originating mostly from fish bones, in reality lower than the one suggested
in Table 4.5, as filleting is dominantly performed at households and restaurants. For the selected options,
a reduced ash content of the fish residues is not expected to have substantial impact on the solutions.
The specific placement of a facility within Berbera was discussed and it has been assumed that the treatment
facility can be placed close to Berbera station and if not, close to the Berbera based FairFishing office. It
was assumed that a property size of precisely the square meters needed for the facility was available and
could easily be rented the moment the project starts. In Figure 4.18, Berbera station and FairFishing’s office
65
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:81==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
in Berbera are highlighted along with the land that by observation may be available. Gulled [57] confirmed
that open land close to the station and to the office likely will be available for rent. FairFishing further
discussed the option of renting an empty old DANIDA building at the harbour next to Berbera station
for possible fish residue treatment. The distance between the market and the station is, as illustrated in
Figure 4.18, approximately 150 meters and it is considered that this short transport distance for collection
can be covered by a cargo bike.
Figure 4.18: Modified Google maps screen shot of Berbera harbour, retrieved 14 May 2020. The map specifies where
relevant buildings are placed; a = Berbera station, b = FairFishing office, c = DANIDA building, and where land
may be available for construction of the facility.
4.4.2
The Fish silage scenario
A final scenario for fish silage production was formed. It included determination of the final setup, system
boundaries and inventory considered for the later techno-economic assessment. The following sections
elaborate on this.
Final setup
The Fish silage setup differs slightly from the previously presented setup suggested in Section 4.3.1. In the
final setup, the component added for fish residue degradation and conservation was decided to be formic
acid. Acid was added in 3.5 % by volume, meaning 350 ml acid per 10 kg fish residue [94, 106]. The
alternative, addition of a fermenting unit and bacterial culture, demanded approximately 160 kg of a sugary
waste product per day in the first year. As the accessibility of a fermenting unit and bacterial culture was
not known in Berbera and was not possible to find during the data collection, it was not considered for fish
residue degradation. Defatting was not included as it was not found necessary since the fish residues were
already low on fat content (8.1 % of TS).
The treatment solution was originally assigned 25 m2 for employee and office facilities (see Section 4.3.1),
but it was later decided not to include it, after discussion with FairFishing. Instead, the FairFishing office,
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
66
==PAGE:82==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
which is placed a 5 to 10 min walk away from the station, was considered available for use. A 30 m2 area
was still designated for waste collection vehicles, waste delivery, and storage, as suggested in the technology
review.
System description and boundary
The following section describes the analysed system for the Fish silage scenario and elaborates on the
chosen system boundaries displayed in Figure 4.19 and the flows within the suggested system.
Figure 4.19: Overview of the system boundaries applied to the economic assessment of the Fish silage scenario: Dry
fish silage production with Berbera fish station and Berbera market fish residues and acid addition. Green colored
text indicate where benefits are found within the system.
Before the actual silage process, the fish residues are minced in a grinder powered by a diesel fuelled
generator. The need of additional sorting was assumed limited, but it should be assessed before starting
up the facility. The degradation process, performed in plastic drums, is assumed to take two days with
continuously stirring and measurements of pH with a pH-meter until a stable pH is obtained in the mixture.
When the silage is finished, the material is placed for drying in a solar tunnel dryer for three days. Once
a moisture content of 10 % is reached, the fish silage is ready for packing in storable bags. Combined,
grinding, packing and waste delivery of a one day fish input is assumed to take up approximately four hours
of work. In total 14 hours (two workers) per 640 kg fish residue input is assumed. All fish silage is assumed
to be sold to farmers as animal feed.
Inventory
Table 4.17 provides an overview of the included processes and materials used in the scenario and whose
costs and benefits have been modelled as part of the later techno-economic assessment. All processes and
materials have been included that was found needed for dry fish silage production, even down the smaller
details of how many batteries to include. Despite of this, as shown in the below table, the need for materials
for fish silage production appears simple.
67

4.4.3
The Biogas scenario
A scenario for biogas production was formed that included determination of the final setup, system bound-
aries and inventory considered for the later techno-economic assessment. The following sections elaborate
on this.
Final setup
A final setup for the Biogas scenario was formed that included two tube digesters for anaerobic digestion
of fish residues mixed with wastewater and other solid organic waste streams to produce biogas. After
scrubbing and dewatering, the biogas is mainly used for electricity production and to a smaller degree for
cooking gas substitution. The paragraphs below elaborate on the chosen setup.
The biogas digester design differs from the previously presented design suggested in Section 4.3.1, as
the former design was based solely on literature review. The design of the biogas digester was updated
to a more optimal design, based on the information gathered directly from possible Biogas suppliers. A
wet co-digestion process was still chosen for biogas production in order to be able to lower the nitrogen
concentration. It was also chosen due to generally more experience with wet digestion, and as higher
methane potentials are often achievable, see Section 4.3.1. For the anaerobic digestion with fish residues,
it was decided to add wastewater to dilution to advisable ammonia-nitrogen levels, and to add additional
other organic waste to increase the C:N ratio. From Section 4.2.1, it was found that station wastewater
(referred to as wastewater from now on), other organic waste from Berbera market (from now on referred
to as market waste) and beef cattle manure from the port and/or quarantine (from now on referred to
as manure) are likely to be available near Berbera station and was therefore considered for above the
purposes. The composition of the additional waste streams was estimated based on literature and qualified
assumptions (Appendix E Table E.1). The ammonia inhibition threshold for acclimatised microbes is 4
g free ammonia-N/L digestate [152]. However, the fraction of total nitrogen that forms free ammonia
(and thereby the needed quantity of wastewater) can only be determined experimentally based on pH
measurement or with advanced modelling. Therefore, wastewater was added to the final mixture to reach a
precautionary nitrogen level of 4 g N/L and to bring the TS content below 10 % [12]. With the composition
of market waste and manure, it was not possible to reach the recommended C:N ratio range (25-30) of
the input mixture, due to the low C content of these two fractions. A low C:N ratio can potentially lower
the yield as discussed in Section 4.3.1. Small fractions of these two waste streams were instead added
to the final mixture. With the estimated composition of the mixture entering the digester (Appendix E,
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
68
==PAGE:84==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
Table E.1), the ORL is within an acceptable range (≤2 g VS/L reactor/day [138]) and the fat content
is low enough not to cause any inhibition [12]. As mentioned in 4.3.1 salinity was still not considered an
issue. The ratio between fish residue, wastewater, market waste and manure were calculated based on the
above considerations and kept constant throughout the years. Due to the nitrogen concentration, a six
months acclimatisation period for the microbes was included in the first year resulting in 25 % reduced
biogas production in year 0. Before implementation, it is advised to sample and analyse available waste
streams to determine the specific composition in order to create the best mixture for optimal performance.
In 2021 the biogas production rate was determined to 150 m3/d and reaching 190 m3/d in 2030, see
Appendix E Table E.1. The biogas production was based on a the determined adjusted TBMP of 0.43 L
methane/gVS (80 % conversion ratio of non-adjusted TBMP) and a assumed methane composition of 67
%.
The digester volume is dependent on hydraulic retention time (HRT) and volume of daily mixture input.
The HRT was assumed to be 30 days, which is found in literature to be common for digesters in tropical
climates [138]. To accommodate future input flows, the size of the digester was based on the last project
year’s (2030) mixture input (Appendix E, Table E.1). The total digester volume, excluding incorporated gas
storage within the digester, was calculated to be 220 m3, but corrected to 250 m3 after what was available
from suppliers. This would mean that there is excess space available for additional waste if desired.
Eiroa et al. [37] estimated the approximate degradation of volatile solids during digestion to be 66-81 %.
The calculation of the digester volume neglected the VS degradation due to low TS content. The digester
volume is relevant for land rent and investment costs for the biogas reactor. To potentially reduce the size
of the digester by lowering the quantity of added wastewater, market waste and manure, it is suggested
to experimentally determined the optimal HRT, how much free ammonia is produced from fish residue
digestion and the effects of a higher dry matter content on the methane generation yield. When the exact
values for HRT and dilution need or additional waste addition is determined, a more precise digester volume
can be proposed. After communication with suppliers [111], a 100 m3 gas storage bag was included in the
setup.
The suggested setup occupies an approximate ground area of 170 m2 that includes the 30 m2 area desig-
nated for waste collection vehicles, waste delivery, storage and machinery, as assumed in the technology
review. The 25 m2 for employee and office facilities (see Section 4.3.1) was excluded after discussion with
FairFishing, as FairFishing’s local office will be available for workers.
System description and boundary
The following section describes the system for the Biogas scenario and elaborates on the chosen system
boundaries displayed in Figure 4.20 and the flows within the system.
69
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:85==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
Figure 4.20: Overview of the system boundaries applied to the economic assessment of the Biogas scenario: Biogas
production from Berbera station and market fish residues, wastewater and beef cattle manure and other organic
market waste. Green colored text indicate where benefits are found within the system. *Anaerobic digestion with
electrical driven pumps and control panel. FF = FairFishing. LPG = Liquified Petroleum Gas.
Manure, market waste and market fish residues are planned to be collected on a daily basis with a small col-
lection vehicle (cargo bike). The treatment facility is assumed placed close to the station where wastewater
from the station is incorporated in the system by a pipe system. Yet, if the facility is not placed very close
to the station, an option is to include other types of wastewater. The digester is fed semi-continuously
every day with the solid waste, whereas the wastewater is fed continuously to a certain daily volume limit
detected by liquid level switches. Wastewater volumes should be monitored and suggested controlled with
a tap system. Fish residues, market waste and manure are minced in an electric grinder before entering
the biogas digester. Sorting is needed before this step, but is assumed to be limited and must be looked
further into when starting up the facility (same assumptions as for Fish silage). Operation (grinding, load-
ing monitoring) and collection is assumed to take seven hours (one worker) a day dependent on daily fish
residue input.
During the anaerobic digestion process, biogas is continuously produced. The produced biogas is extracted
from the digester tank and led through a scrubber and a dehydrator where hydrogen sulphide, carbon dioxide
and water moisture are removed. The final product is biogas which is suitable for energy generation to
substitute diesel or to be used as cooking fuel to substitute liquefied petroleum gas. All electrical equipment
(scrubber, pump, control panel and grinder) are fuelled with electricity powered by the produced biogas in
a biogas generator. It is assumed that a fixed 36 m3 biogas is collected annually in biogas bags for cooking
classes at FairFishing office and that 365 m3 annually is collected for FairFishing office cooking. Digestate
is produced continuously and is emptied from the digester on a regular basis. The idea is that the local
community can collect it free of charge for use as fertiliser and irrigation. The common procedure used
today for wastewater removal, however, is disposal into the harbour. If the demand for fertiliser is low,
there could be a risk of disposing the digestate in the harbour (as currently done for all the wastewater
generated in the city). Small amounts of digestate is manually mixed with fresh feedstock and recirculated
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
70
==PAGE:86==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
daily to the digester to ensure that the feedstock is properly mixed with the microbes, and to increase
passive mixing.

4.4.4
Effects on category scores for technology ranking
This paragraph aims at discussing the effect of the new technical setup (in terms of space requirements,
water added, etc.) on the category scores given in the previous technology ranking (Section 4.3). The
new and in depth results were not iterated back to the previous technology suggestion, Section 4.3.1,
due to fair comparison with equal level of detail, and as it was found in the following section that the
two technologies would still be selected for the techno-economic assessment. The ranking scores from the
technology screening of fish silage production was based on a dry processing with either acid or a fermenting
unit with bacterial addition. The only modifications in the final setup for the Fish silage scenario were the
choice of the acid addition and the import of the solar dryer tunnel instead of local purchase. As the changes
are minor, the only change in the ranking is in the category Construction, material and maintenance costs,
where the score would change from (2) to (3). This is due to a new assumption that the solar tunnel dryer
is not obtainable in Somalia. As Fish silage originally only marginally scored better than Charcoal (2 points
better), the change in score would move Fish silage down on the overall ranking, meaning that Charcoal
would score better. Since one feed and one energy option was intended chosen, this change would not
affect which technology was selected for scenario formation and techno-economic assessment.
71
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:87==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
For the Biogas setup, four changes were made in the ranking after choosing a final setup for the techno-
economic assessment: the TS content was increased from 1 % to 5 % , the methane potential was based
on the calculated composition instead of on values found in literature, and the size of the facility, and the
labour needed was altered due to change of quantity and type of digester. Categories that could potentially
be affected by the change are: Construction, material and maintenance costs; Space requirements; Need
of continuous water input; Need of continuous fuel input; and FairFishing savings and sales income. The
Biogas score in Construction, material and maintenance costs was set based on needed import of materials
and advanced construction work, which is still the case. For Space requirements, in the new specification,
the digester volume has been decreased, however, the digester type has changed to a laying tube rather
than a tall floating drum digester, whereby approximately the same ground area per tonne fish residues is
obtained. As office area is removed, and in the comparisons would be extracted from all options, a new
score is here not given to Biogas based on new information. In regards to Need of continuous water input,
Biogas is still the only solution with continuous high water demand compared to the other solutions, despite
an increased TS concentration. In the above-mentioned categories, Biogas does not change scores with
the new setup. Ranking categories that are affected, though, are Labour need and FairFishing savings and
sales income. Labour need scores are dependent on the number of workers and type of work (light/hard
physical labour). As the number of digesters has been reduced to two, only one worker for relatively hard
physical labour is needed in the final setup instead of four workers for eight floating drums. This change
will reduce the previous score from (4) to (3). As a higher biogas potential is used for the new setup, a
higher biogas production is also assumed per kg fish residue input. For the category FairFishing savings
and sales income, this would potentially mean a score of (2) instead of (3). The improved scores for Biogas
would not change the overall priority of the technologies as Biogas already scores best among the suggested
options.
4.5
Techno-economic assessment
4.5.1
Fish silage
The financial costs-benefit analysis was conducted for the Fish silage scenario, with a nominal 12 % discount
rate, the given inflation, a project life of 10 years, and the assumptions collected in Appendix F. Here it
is worth emphasising, that silage was estimated to be sold for 0.40 USD/kg and that a there would be a
constant high demand for animal feed. Additionally, it was assumed that all fish silage produced would be
sold, meaning a selling ratio of 1. This is a none-verified value.
The financial analysis of the fish silage scenario with above assumptions resulted in a net present value
(NPV) of 170,000 USD without funding. Figure 4.21 shows the obtained annual distribution of present
costs and benefits and the cumulative annual present value (APV) for Fish silage. It is evident from the
figure that the benefits outweigh the costs in all project years including project year 0, which includes
capital and start-up costs. This results in a positive APV from year 0, thus a payback period of 0 years.
The internal rate of return (IRR) is as a consequence hereof infinite.

marked with a star. Note all values are rounded to two significant digits.
In Figure 4.22 contribution of major processes are shown. It is clear from the figure, that benefits contribute

RESULTS & DISCUSSION
Other stakeholders economically affected
Stakeholders not included directly in the economic assessment may be affected by the waste treatment
system producing fish silage. Farmers can benefit from the system in terms of an alternative feed option
which has a substantial price difference compared to hay in periods where there is insufficient grass on
grasslands. Farmers can save 19 USD monthly or 0.64 USD daily per camel assuming that one camel eats
4.4 kg hay per day, that 10 % of their diets can be substituted with dried fish silage and that selling price of
fish silage was 0.4 USD/kg compared to the price of 1.5 USD/kg hay during droughts. This is calculated
using the found protein substitution potential (8.2 kg hay/kg dried fish silage) presented in Appendix D.1.
The total expenses for the farmers are unknown, however, knowing that the general salaries in Somaliland
span from 200-600 USD per month, an app. 20 USD saving per camel can be substantial.
Comparison with other studies
A comparison with other economic studies on fish silage production was carried out to support the financial
findings.
Economic assessments of fish silage production are scarce in literature. Only one was found that includes a
vaguely explained costs-benefit analysis. In the report by Rurangwa et al. [106], the costs-benefit analysis
was performed for processing of six tonnes fish residues daily with formic acid addition for degradation
in an unspecified area of East Africa. There was no drying of the silage included and only a six years
lifetime of the project. Inflation and discounting were not determined in their report and therefore direct
comparison with present project was not possible. Non-inflated and un-discounted values of their project
year 1, assumed to be the first year of operation after construction, and this project’s year 1, was used for
comparison. By normalising their costs and benefits per kg daily fish residue input, their fish silage sales
income (110 USD/kg daily fish residue input) was found to be almost twice as high as the fish silage sales
income estimated for this project (67 USD/kg daily fish residue input from non-inflated and not discounted
values in project year 1). Furthermore, the comparison study had a surprisingly large investment costs of
56 USD per kg daily fish residue input, which is a factor 7.6 higher than the investment costs per kg daily
fish residue input found in present project. The reason for the big difference could be due to the costs
associated with their construction of a building. Their investment costs were, however, not specified and a
direct comparison was for these reasons not possible. Similarly, the net cash flow of year 1 was higher for
the comparison study (47 USD/kg daily fish residue input > 34 USD/kg daily fish residue input), despite
a similar sales prices of fish silage (0.42 USD/kg fish silage). However, Rurangwa et al. [106] did not, as
it seems, dry the fish silage and therefore did not include rent for additional drying area and maintenance
of solar dryer. The fact that their selling price was so high despite having a high water content and lower
feeding value per kg fish silage, furthermore indicate that the present project’s selling price is debatable,
and could potentially be higher.
The higher operation and maintenance cost and thereby the comparable lower net profit in this project was
found realistic due to the inclusion of the drying process that demands more labour and materials compared
to wet silage production.
Scenario sensitivity analysis
The scenario sensitivity analysis investigated the scenario assumptions made and how sensitive the NPV
was on the governing parameters: discount rate and project lifetime. Furthermore, it was found relevant to
investigate variations in fish silage selling ratio, and how adding a waste collection fee and funding would
affect the viability of the Fish silage scenario.
From the analysis it was found that reducing the discount rate to 5.0 % would increase the NPV by 33 %.
This means that if a lower discount rate was chosen it could potentially substantially increase the NPV of
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
74
==PAGE:90==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
the project. However, with the given political and socio-economic situation in Somalia, a discount rate of
5.0 % is not found likely.
Furthermore, it was analysed what an altered project lifetime would mean for the project. By combining
a lifetime of 5 years and with an assumption that the solar dryer, grinder, diesel generator, freezer, and
collection vehicle still have a value of 10 % of the original purchasing cost and that they are sold at the
end of the project life, the NPV was reduced with 44 % but still overall profitable (NPV > 0). As the
Fish silage scenario generates a positive cash flow from year 0, reducing the project’s lifetime, naturally
reduces the NPV. On the other hand, when the project lifetime was increased to 15 years, where additional
maintenance costs were added in the last 5 years, the NPV increased by 31 %. This because 5 years of
additional cash flows thereby had been included in the NPV. Consequently, independent of project lifetime,
the Fish silage scenario was found feasible, and that the it would benefit from extending the project life.
It was initially assumed that all fish silage would be sold every year (sold fish silage ratio = 1) in the Fish
silage scenario. However, in reality, sales may fluctuate and be highly dependent on draught occurrences.
Therefore, for scenario sensitivity analysis, the NPV was determined based on the assumptions of a zero
sales ratio of fish silage every second year (from year 1), while keeping the operations costs constant. The
calculated NPV was 97 % lower than original NPV but still higher than zero. If the sales ratio was reduced
with 50 % in all years, this instead resulted in relative NPV decrease of 99 %, which is lower than if fish
silage was only sold every second year. The reason for the difference is found in the fact that money earned
in the future is less worth than money earned today, thus a reduced income in project year 0 has higher
impact than occasionally reduced income in the future. Hence, money earned the first year of operation
was most important.
Currently, the local government is paid 0.52 USD/kg fish caught and delivered to the station in Berbera
for waste collection, harbour security and maintenance etc. by the FairFishing partners (Appendix C.2).
It was considered, how it would benefit the NPV, if the local government was to pay some of that fee to
FairFishing. It was here found that when adding a share of 1.0 % and 2.5 % of that fee as a benefit, the
NPV was increased with 61,000 USD and 150,000 USD, respectively. Thus, a relative change of 37 % (to
230,000 USD) and 91 % (to 320,000 USD). The Fish silage scenario would hence improve substantially
by addition of such a fee. Governmental waste fees are thereby highly relevant for future work but is not
needed to obtain a positive NPV.
Lastly, the NPV was calculated assuming that FairFishing had all Capital and start-up costs funded. As
Capital and start-up costs are insignificant contributor to the overall NPV for the given scenario, funding
would only increase the NPV with 2.8 %, and was therefore found negligible and not included for further
analysis.
Parameter sensitivity analysis
A parameter sensitivity analysis was performed on the parameters that were used to model the most
contributing processes and materials to the NPV:
• Labour for processing and collection was modelled based on the parameters: first year annual fish
residue input, labour need and labour wage.
• Acid cost was modelled based on the parameters:first year annual fish residue input, acid price and
acid use.
• Fish silage income was modeled based on the parameters: first year annual fish residue input,
conversion ratio to dried fish silage, sold fish silage ratio, and selling price of fish silage.
From the parameter sensitivity analysis, the following sensitive parameters (contributing parameters with
a sensitivity ratio Sin > |0.30|) were detected, see Table 4.19: Selling price of fish silage, sold fish silage
