Two of the switching values were found realistic: The sold fish silage ratio should decrease to only 50
% of all fish silage being sold annually. As droughts play a major role in the fish silage demand, this
reduction may be within a realistic range. Similarly, selling price of fish silage, will with a 50 % reduction
start generating a negative NPV. As the original price setting is estimated, a 50 % decrease is not found
unrealistic. The price is, however, set relatively low compared to what hay is sold for during droughts.
The remaining high contributing parameter’s switching values were found unrealistic. For labour wage the
switching value was at a hourly wage of 7.3 USD/hr, thus a 200 % increase from applied labour wage. A
7.3 USD hourly wage equals a monthly pay of 1,200 USD, which is found very unlikely in Somaliland, where
the average salary pay per month is app. 400 USD, see Appendix C.2. For labour needs, the switching
value was similarly 200 % higher than the applied value, equivalent to a work load increase from 12 to 36
hours for treating 640 kg fish residues daily. A labour need increase in that dimension was found unrealistic
for collection, handling fish and fish silage processing which are simple processes. First year annual fish
residue input would need a 81 % reduction in order to lower the NPV to zero, similar to a first year daily
input decrease from 640 kg to 120 kg. This is found unlikely, due to the fact that the daily fish waste input
is estimated based on actual data of fish delivered to the station, that reaches more than three tonnes
fresh fish per day. For the conversion ratio to dried fish silage, the switching value was 53 % below applied
value, thus going from 0.42 to 0.20 kg dried fish silage produced per kg fish residue. As 0.42 is based on
a 100 % efficient conversion where all dry matter plus 10 % water goes to produce fish silage, this is the
optimal amount. Water content may easily fluctuate if poorly surveilled and therefore the fluctuations in
conversion ratio are possible, however a 50 % reduction in production would mean that a large quantity
of the solid matter would have been wasted as the dry matter percentages of fish residues was found to
be above 38 %. The switching value was therefore found unlikely. The parameters acid price and acid use
were both found not to be sensitive parameters, which was also illustrated in the high switching values (a
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
76
==PAGE:92==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
relative change of applied values above 600 %) that were found unrealistic since price data was obtained
directly from online web stores [59] and several articles confirm the applied acid use [97, 106, 127].
It was shallowly inspected how the low contributing parameters (contributing <5% to NPV), affected the
NPV. The limit of 5% was here found fair as these parameters’ sensitive ratio were well below 0.3.
Data quality analysis
Results of the data quality analysis are shown in Table 4.19. The data quality analysis of each sensitive
parameter, showed that the following sensitive parameters all scored an average data quality score equal
to or above 2.0, and where therefore found to be critical (the average data quality score is indicated by
red): labour need (3.8), selling price of fish silage (3.0), sold fish silage ratio (5.0) and first year annual
fish residue input (2.4). They were all to a large extent based on qualified none-verified estimates and are
therefore expected to be uncertain and potentially vary within a defined range. Individual pedigree matrix
category scores can be found in Appendix F.3.
Risk analysis
The risk analyses performed by propagating the expected distribution of the critical parameters (Sin > |0.30|
and an average data quality score equal to or above 2.0); labour need, selling price of fish silage, sold fish
silage ratio, and first year annual fish residue input, showed that a triangular distribution of the NPV
was the best fit. The distribution of the NPV together with the cumulative risk curve are presented in
Figure 4.23. An overview of all parameters with their probability distribution and the parameters used for
each distribution are presented in Appendix F.4, section F.4.1, and the Monte Carlo simulation can be
found in Appendix F.1.
The given simulation showed a most likely NPV value in the range 44,000-140,000 USD (average value
of 94,000 USD) and a NPV mean value of 250,000 USD. Mean and most likely value differ due to the
triangular distribution. The lowest obtainable NPV was fund to be -160,000 USD and the highest obtainable
was 1,100,000 USD. The Fish silage scenario’s maximum value thereby differs from the most likely with
almost one million USD. The simulation showed 7.9 % chance of the NPV being zero or negative, a 31
% chance of the NPV being equal to or lower than former determined NPV (170,000 USD), and a 69 %
chance of the NPV being 170,000 USD or greater. These results confirm that the NPV for the Fish silage
scenario is very robust and it is more likely to be higher than the calculated 170,000 USD than lower.
77
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:93==

Figure 4.23: Histogram of the Fish silage scenario Monte Carlo simulated NPV when including uncertainties of
identified critical parameters. The cumulative probability are showed for the triangular distributed NPV. The corner
figure displays the triangular distribution fit to the Monte Carlo simulated NPV. Note all values are rounded to two
significant digits. Cdf = Cumulative distribution function. Pdf = Probability density function. NPV = net present
value.
Discussion on major risks
The parameters selling price of fish silage and sold fish silage ratio, reflects the demand on fish silage. As
found from the scenario sensitivity analysis, a fluctuating sale has large impact on NPV, but the specific
current selling ratio and fish silage price is unknown, and highly depend on draught occurrences, which
too is both seasonal and dependent on La Niña events. Last registered severe drought periods were in
2016-2017. Nur [87], however, confirms that less severe drought periods occur every year. This indicate
that there might be a demand for animal feed in several months of the year. To reduce uncertainty of the
demand related parameters, it is recommended to further asses the demand on alternative animal feed by
gaining additional information from farmers on: The price they are willing to pay for one kg fish silage; how
often they are going to purchase fish silage; how often they are in the urban area and thereby close to the
feed market; and if there are animals willing to eat fish silage and in what quantities. These questions will
support making a more precise selling price of fish silage and sold fish silage ratio and thereby a stronger
business case. It will also help estimate whether or not there will be long periods potentially without any
income from sales.
In periods with maximal selling, as illustrated in the original scenario results, the Fish silage scenario
produces a substantial annual surplus. This surplus can potentially be used in periods of low demand to
balance out costs. However, if the surplus is continuously used to benefit internally rather than saving for
periods of low demand, there is a risk that there will be no money for operation in periods where fish silage
in reality is not possible to sell. It is therefore recommended to continuously monitor sales and prepare for
periods without draughts, and alternatively investigate other possible markets.
A requirement for the production of fish silage is that the fish input needs to be fresh, in order to obtain
a good quality feed. If this requirement is not fulfilled, produced fish silage is not suitable for animal feed.
An alternative for the spoiled fish residues is to produce silage for fertiliser use. Fertiliser, however, has
a lower value on the waste hierarchy and thereby also an expected lower sales price. The risk of the fish
residues not being fresh when delivered is relevant to consider as temperatures are high in Somaliland and
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
78
==PAGE:94==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
cooling equipment and ice is limited for waste transport. Potential loss of fresh fish residues has, however,
not been included in the economic assessment. It is advised, if the wish is to produce only fish silage for
animal feed, to further assess fish residue disposal and how to improve waste handling and flow to ensure
that all fish residues are delivered at the facility as fresh as possible.
4.5.2
Biogas
A NPV of 700 USD was obtained for the Biogas scenario, when a nominal 12 % discount rate, the given
inflation, a project life of 10 years, and the assumptions collected in Appendix F, were applied. The NPV
was hence only just positive and the scenario was not found robust, as minor unexpected discounted cost
greater than 700 USD would turn the NPV negative.
The weak profitability was also reflected in the IRR which was 13 %, only one percent higher than the
applied discount rate. The annual present distribution of costs and benefits and the cumulative APV are
showed in Figure 4.24 together with the NPV. The figure shows how the Capital and start-up costs were
relatively high compared to the yearly net benefits. This fact resulted in the payback period of 10 years.
Figure 4.24: Cumulative annual present value (APV), and annual present distribution of costs and benefits for the
Biogas scenario without funding. The net present value is marked with a star on the APV curve.Note all values are
rounded to two significant digits.
Figure 4.25 summarises most contributing processes and materials. It is evident from the figure that benefits
made up the largest contribution of NPV, followed by operation costs and then capital and start-up costs.
Benefits originated 100 % from diesel savings which corresponds to 2,600 L diesel per month the first year
assuming normal operation capacity. This corresponds to 60 % of the average diesel use in 2017 and 2018,
to fuel ice machines located at Berbera station (Appendix C.3). The whole diesel demand for ice cooling in
Berbera is thereby not covered by the biogas produced. Less than 0.50 % of savings originated from LPG
savings. The largest contribution to operation costs originated from labour for operation and collection
and land rent. Capital and start-up costs consisted mainly of components for the biogas reactor as well as
shipping. Maintenance costs and demolition costs were minor in the overall NPV as they contributed with
3.8 % in total.

Figure 4.25: Contribution of major processes and materials to NPV, along with the total NPV in the Biogas scenario
without funding. NPV = net present value. Note all values are rounded to two significant digits.
Comparison with other studies
Biogas production was barely found feasible (NPV close to 0) As biogas production from waste is a common
procedure in both low-, middle- and high-income countries, the results were compared with other literature
to support the reliability of the results.
The normalised capital and start-up costs to digester volume for the present project was found to be 140
USD/ m3 applied digester. Vögeli et al. [138] summarised capital costs of biogas digesters, mainly floating
drums, to be in the range 83-1,398 USD/ m3. This indicate that the capital cost found within the present
project is within the common range. When looking at more specifically comparable setups, the largest
observed digester, a floating drum digester of 150 m3, reached a capital cost of 786 USD/m3 [56]. Another
digester that treated fish waste, a floating drum of 25 m3 in India, reached a capital cost of 1,398 USD/m3
[138]. These setups show higher capital costs than found in the present project’s Biogas scenario. As the
size and type of the present project’s digester is larger and made of cheaper material, this project’s capital
and start-up costs are assumed fair but in the low range. For this project, a low capital cost per digester
size is needed, as there is a certain funding limit that would otherwise be crossed. However, a lower cost
on digester materials may also mean lower quality and a more fragile setup. The maximum capital and
start- up costs allowed for the present project is 200 USD/m3 (for a 250 m3 digester), if a funding limit of
50,000 USD/m3 is set.
Heeb [60] investigated a fish fed biogas plant in India that produces gas for street lightening. They found
that the given plant had large investment costs and large annual operation costs while only expected
revenues to cover one third of operation costs. Comparing with the present project’s Biogas scenario, their
annual operation costs were almost twice as high (45 USD/m3 compared to 105 USD/m3). This indicate
that there may be an underestimation of operation costs in the present project. The reasons could however
be due to a lower estimate of labour for this project compared to the Indian one, and due to differences in
local conditions such as salaries and land rent.
In 2011, Gaddisa [49] investigated the feasibility of a 120 m3 fixed dome for treating wastewater from an
apartment house to produce biogas for stove fuel replacement. With a 12 % discount rate and a project life
of 15 years, an NPV of approximately 300 USD/m3 was obtained in 2020 values (537,822 Birr in 2011).
Without funding, this project’s NPV reached 2.8 USD/m3 with a 10 year lifetime. The NPV is thereby 100
times smaller for this project. The normalised annual costs and capital and start-up costs for this project,
and the comparison study were similar, however large differences were found in the annual incomes. This
study had a 34 % smaller yearly income than found in the comparison study (70 USD/m3<110 USD/m3).
This along with the five year difference in lifetimes explains the NPV difference.
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
80
==PAGE:96==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
Scenario sensitivity analysis
Variations in the Biogas scenario setup was analysed for NPV sensitivity. The scenario sensitivity analysis
was made to investigate how sensitive the scenario was to the factors; discount rate, project lifetime,
modification of existing diesel generator for biogas utilisation, addition of a waste collection fee and funding.
The NPV was investigated for sensitivity of altering the discount rate to 5.0 %, which is common in high
income countries. The NPV here obtained a relative change of 1800 % to 13,000 USD. The setup was
hence found very sensitive to the chosen discount rate, however, a 5% discount rate in Somalia is not found
realistic.
Altering the lifetime to 5 and 15 years changed the NPV with -2,100 % and 1,500 %, respectively. With the
10 years lifetime in the original scenario, the NPV was on the verge to become unfeasible (NPV<0), and by
reducing the lifetime, the capital and start-up costs would consequently never be paid back by the annual
cash flows, which resulted in a the negative NPV. This also means, that if the project for unforeseeable
reasons is closed prior to project year 10, the NPV will become negative if the capital and start-up costs are
not covered by e.g funding. Increasing the lifetime to 15 years, though, ensures 5 more years with income.
With a 15 years lifetime, the NPV per m3 reached 45 USD. This is still approximately six times lower than
the 300 USD/m3 found by Gaddisa [49] which had a lifetime of 15 years.
The current Biogas scenario was not found convincingly feasible with its low NPV (700 USD) over a
project life of 10 years without funding. Therefore, it was of great importance to see where in the setup
modifications could be made to improve the scenario.
It was investigated how a waste fee payed by the government would benefit the Biogas scenario. Here it
was found that the NPV would increase by 8,700 % (to 62,000 USD) and 22000 % (to 150,000 USD) when
adding a fee of 1.0 % and 2.5 % respectively of what the partners pay per kg fish caught and delivered
to the station in Berbera. The Biogas scenario will therefore substantially benefit from additional revenues
generated from collecting waste fees. Cooperation with the local government on waste management is for
this reason advised for the Biogas scenario.
Lastly, it was investigated what funding would mean for the Biogas scenario. With the Capital and start-up
costs covering in total 35,000 USD in the original scenario and assuming all Capital and start-up costs are
covered by funding, the NPV would increase drastically with a relative change of 5,000 %. Thus, reaching
a NPV of 35,000 USD, equivalent to the capital and start-up cost. As the NPV of the Biogas scenario
without funding was close to unfeasible (due to the low NPV), it was decided from now on to include 100
% funding of capital and start-up costs, as FairFishing’s plan is to seek funding to cover all capital and
start-up costs. Thus, in the following analyses the Biogas scenario includes funding and has a NPV
of 35,000 USD. An idea to reduce funding need, thus capital and start-up costs, is by modifying the
existing diesel generator at the station to be able to convert biogas to electricity rather than purchasing a
new biogas generator. Assuming a 90 % reduction in biogas generator price, illustrating the modification
of existing engine, this reduced the funding need by 28 %. This was not considered in the further analysis
as the diesel generator type and capacity used at Berbera station is not specified. It is, however, advised
to further investigate the possibility to modify the local engine for biogas utilisation.
Parameter sensitivity analysis
In the Biogas scenario with funding, the parameter sensitivity analysis was performed on the parameters
(defined as high contributing parameters) that were used to model the high contributing processes and
materials to the NPV:
• Labour for operation and collection is modelled based on the parameters: first year daily fish residue
input, labour need and labour wage.

Two of the sensitive parameters had a switching value that was found realistic: diesel price and biogas
potential. As diesel prices in Kenya have been found to variate with 20 % in the last 4 months [52] (no
novel data could be found for Somalia), a diesel price reduction of 25 % was found possible. The biogas
potential was the most sensitive parameter and it was found that the potential should only decrease with 27
%, equivalent to a decrease from 150 m3 to 110 m3 biogas daily in year 0. As the actual biogas production
is highly sensitive, especially with the applied C:N ratio and water content, biogas production may fluctuate
greatly if not properly surveilled. Therefore, a 27 % reduction in biogas production was found possible.
The switching values of the remaining sensitive parameters were found unrealistic: The switching value of
labour wage would have to be almost doubled, equivalent to a monthly wage increase from 390 to 680
USD. The risk of needing to pay labour wages of 680 USD per month was not found likely as this is the
common salary for highly educated staff, see Appendix C.2, which is not needed for operation of the biogas
plant. Similarly, the labour need would have to be doubled to generate a unfeasible Biogas scenario with
funding, equivalent of a work load increase from six hours a day to 11 hours a day. An increase of biogas’
scenario’s labour need to 11 hr/day is not found likely either, as the only labour associated with biogas is
collection, electric grinding, digestate removal and surveillance and maintenance. From the switching value
of first year daily fish residue input, it was here found that the parameter should decrease from 640 kg to
45 kg to turn the scenario NPV surplus to negative. This was found unlikely as more than three tonnes
fresh fish daily is caught and delivered at Berbera station Appendix C.3.
For the facility size (per HRT and daily last year total input), the switching value of 0.0016 m2/kg
(calculated as 170 m2/HRT(d)/(last year total daily input(kg/d)) corresponds to a ground area increase
from 170 to 360 m2. The majority of the 170 m2 ground area was made up by the digesters (150 m2
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
82
==PAGE:98==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
as provided through communication with the supplier Schenzen Teenwin Environment Technology Co.
Ldt [111]). For a tube digester design, that already occupies the largest ground area of possible digester
designs, such an increase is not found possible unless more water is added to the fish residues to further
dilute nitrogen levels. As there are other options for nitrogen reduction, e.g. co-digestion, ground area
increase can be avoided. Alternatively, building a supportive wall around the tube digester to increase the
height and lower the ground area is an option, though at a higher capital cost (therefore not included).
The hydraulic retention (HRT) time of 30 days is a general recommended literature value [138]. It was,
however, also found in literature that in several cases the actual HRT was drastically above the recom-
mended values when waste inputs were below expected or when the digester was improperly maintained
[138]. There is therefore a risk, if not run in the advised manners, that the actual operation HRT will reach
the switching values. However, the facility will be built on a initially planned HRT and therefore a HRT of
60 can easily be avoided.
Finally, for the land rent price, a switching vale of 5.0 USD per m2, double the current land price, was
found. Berbera is, due to the planned port expansion, experiencing relatively large land and rent price
increases [51] (Confirmed by Obara [88]), however, the risk of a 100 % increase before project start is not
found likely.
As explained in Section 4.4.3, wastewater was added dependent on nitrogen threshold values of the digester
mixture, and nitrogen levels of digester mixture could affect methane potentials. The facility size was
based predominantly on the digester size, and as wastewater made up 80 % of total input volume, varying
nitrogen levels would in the end mean a varying need for land rent. Thus, the effect of a varying nitrogen
concentration was studied indirectly by studying methane potentials and facility size. As the NPV was
found sensitive to these parameters, the biogas system is too expected to be sensitive to nitrogen levels of
fish residues.
For the biogas scenario, it was too shallowly inspected how the low contributing parameters (contributing
<5 % to NPV), affected the NPV. The limit of 5 % was here found fair as these parameters’ sensitive ratio
were well below 0.3.
Data quality analysis
Results of the data quality analysis are shown in Table 4.20. From the data quality analysis on all previously
mentioned sensitive parameters in the Biogas scenario with funding, the following scored an average data
quality score equal to or above 2, and where therefore found to be critical parameters (average data quality
score is indicated with red): labour need (3.8), first year daily fish residue input (2.4) biogas potential
(2.0), diesel price (2.0) and HRT (2.8). These parameters were to a large degree based on general literature
values, theoretical calculations or information from few sources over a short time period and were therefore
expected to have a large degree of uncertainty. The individual pedigree matrix category scores can be
found in Appendix F.3.
Risk analysis
From the risk analyses performed on all identified critical parameters; labour need, first year daily fish
residue input, diesel price, biogas potential and HRT, a normal distribution of the NPV was found to be
the best fit. A histogram showing the normal distributed NPV together with the cumulative distribution
function are showed in Figure 4.26.
See Appendix F.1 for the Monte Carlo Simulation and Appendix
F.4, section F.4.1, for an overview of assigned mean and standard deviations for the assigned normally
distributed critical parameters.

range 26,000-36,000 USD with the given histogram intervals, however the mean value of the distribution

Discussion on major risks
A fundamental assumption made for the Biogas scenario was the accessibility of market waste, manure
and wastewater and the belief that it can be accessed free of cost. For manure and waste water this
assumption was considered fair based on the local data collection. For market waste, however, according to
the local data collection, additional effort and economic incentives may be needed in reality. The specific
quantities were additionally not known and there had not been established contact to potential donors
of the additional waste streams. It is therefore found important to investigate the accessibility of these
waste streams properly before a biogas plant is decided on and to investigate further what is needed for
stakeholders to sort and donate organic waste. It is also advised that other waste streams are sought
after, as other available waste streams may have the ability to improve conditions in the reactor, e.g. by
increasing C:N ratio and lower the concentration of nitrogen.
The composition of all waste streams, including fish residues, were estimated from literature values, in
order to estimate a chemical composition of the mixture within the digester. It must be emphasised that
small changes in the composition can mean a lot to the functioning of the reactor as well as the size. It
is therefore advised, before the project is started, that all relevant waste streams are sampled to determine
the chemical composition in order to determine the best suitable mixture to optimise conditions and reactor
size. It is furthermore advised to measure the daily fish residue generation in order to determine a suitable
reactor size. As showed by Heeb [60] and Vögeli et al. [138], several reactors do not run properly due to
much lower waste input than initially assumed.
The fish delivered to the station varied substantially over the year (see Section 4.2.2), with the lowest
quantities around May and June and most around January. This fluctuation determines the availability of
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
84
==PAGE:100==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
fish residue input to the digester over the year and poses a risk, as the yield is sensitive to fluctuating input.
The digester capacity was designed for the average fish residue flow over the year. An idea is to adjust
the amount of other waste input up and down depending on the fish input available in order to even out
fluctuations. This should be done gradually in order for the microbes to adapt to the shifting conditions.
Another potential problem is the maintenance of the reactor and whether or not skilled persons can be
found to fix the digester if needed. If not, there is a risk of e.g. seizing biogas production, biogas leaks and
digestate leaching. It is known after communication with two biogas reactor suppliers, Schenzen Teenwin
Environment Technology Co. Ldt [111] and Schenzen Puxin Environment Technology Co. Ldt [110], that
suppliers offer guidance and assistance with installation. It is also assumed that assistance on maintenance
is available by phone calls to the companies.
Digestate utilisation was not included in the Biogas scenario other than with the assumption that it can
be allocated to local farmers or be used for irrigation and as fertiliser in local parks or green areas. This
is what is favored, as the digestate produced has a nutritional content and holds a lot of water that can
be used for irrigation. There is, though, a risk that the approximately 6,500 kg digestate produced per
day (in last operation year), containing 58 kg VS and 5,800 kg water (Appendix D.1), is not possible to
allocate. If this is the case, several possibilities exist to avoid it being lead out in the sea water. A drying
pit is one option but is both space and labour demanding. As the capital and start-up costs are already
on the limit to what is initially assumed possible to fund (<50,000 USD according to Obara [88]), this
is not recommended because it adds additional capital and start-up costs. If disposed of to the sea, it is
advised to lead the liquid digestate through natural filter beds and depressions where it can be biologically
infiltrated before entering the sea. It is recommended to look further into if digestate can be used locally
for fertiliser and irrigation purposes. Otherwise, disposal options must be looked further into.
4.5.3
Scenario comparison
Both the Fish silage and the Biogas scenario offers a way to treat fish residues produced from Berbera
station and Berbera market. The scenarios, however, show different economic feasibility: the NPV of the
Biogas scenario without funding only reached 700 USD whereas the Fish silage scenario without funding
reached a total of 170,000 USD. For the Fish silage scenario, the capital and start-up costs were negligible
(1 % of the absolute NPV), however, the capital and start-up costs of the Biogas scenario without funding
was seven times higher and would demand a larger workload to procure. Fish silage payed itself in within
the first year, whereas for the Biogas scenario without funding, first payed itself back, in the last year of
the project. With both scenario’s capital and start-up costs funded, Fish silage still proved a more feasible
solution. Summary of categorical present value for both scenarios, with and without funding, is found in
Table 4.21.

RESULTS & DISCUSSION
From the risk analysis, when propagating uncertainties of the most critical parameters from both scenarios,
maximum and minimum values of NPV for the scenarios were found. Based on a triangular distribution
for the Fish silage scenario without funding, the maximum obtainable NPV was 1,100,000 USD, the mode
NPV was 94,000 USD, the mean value was 250,000 USD and the minimum obtainable NPV was -160,000
USD. Based on a normal distribution for the Biogas scenario with funding, the maximum obtainable NPV
was 100,000 USD, the mean NPV was 35,000 USD and the minimum obtainable NPV was -24,000 USD.
Thus, the Fish silage scenario’s NPV spans wider, thereby a chance of producing a higher NPV or lower
NPV than the Biogas scenario with funding. The risk of the Biogas scenario with funding and the Fish
silage scenario without funding to reach a NPV of zero was 4.9 % and 7.9 % respectively. The chance of
producing a negative NPV is thereby minor for both. The chance of the Fish silage scenario to reach the
Biogas scenarios’ most likely NPV or higher has been determined to be 89 % whereas, the chance of Fish
silage’s NPV to be above Biogas’ maximum NPV was found to be 80 %, thus indicating a substantial high
change for Fish silage to produce a larger NPV than Biogas.
A common issue with the two scenarios is the uncertainties associated with daily fish residue input and
composition.
The fish silage sales income was made directly dependent on fish silage production, thus on the composition
and availability of fresh fish residues, hence the quality and logistics of the collection.
For the Biogas scenario, multiple parameters have been linked to the daily fish residue input. Additionally,
the amounts of other waste streams and digester size is strongly dependent on fish composition. It is
for both scenarios recommended to specifically investigate fish composition and generation based on local
data over a longer period of time to obtain best achievable setup and reduce any uncertainties that are
associated with their parameters dependent on daily fish residue input and composition.
Not included in the economic assessment but an option for FairFishing to benefit their partners, is to
reduce the costs of ice. The share of revenues generated for FairFishing by selling fish silage or the savings
generated for diesel substitution from biogas production could go to cover a share of the ice price for the
fishers. Thereby an incentive can be created for the partners to cooperate, sort and donate fish residues
to the suggested setup. As Fish silage potentially generates a larger NPV, it is therefore the solution that
will benefit FairFishing partners most, besides supporting farmers and agriculture in the region.
4.5.4
Effect on previous weighting scores for technology ranking
Based on the results of the techno-economic assessment, the weights of specific categories in the previous
technology ranking could be updated. This since it for both options was found that labour and facility
size, equivalent to the categories labour need and space requirements, are large economic burdens. This
was not illustrated in their initial weighing scores in Section 4.3.2. If these categories had their weights
increased to (5) each and including the updates from Section 4.4.4, this would mean that Biogas would
score best in the ranking (151 points), Fish silage second (159 points) and Charcoal third (162 points).
This means, that even though new findings were not included in previous ranking, this would not change
the original ranking of the technologies to treat fish residues in Somaliland.
4.5.5
Additional benefits
The financial analysis performed for Biogas and Fish silage only considers costs and benefits related to
FairFishing’s activities.
Yet for a societal perspective additional and unaccounted benefits exists.
By
handling fish waste generated by FaiFishing’s partners (and market waste and manure for the Biogas
scenario), a general improvement of the waste management system is carried out: less transport and
hence less use of motor fuels (emissions) and collection; waste reduction, hence less space requirements
on landfills; less emissions (e.g. methane and smoke particles) related to degradation of organic matter
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
86
==PAGE:102==
RESULTS & DISCUSSION
and reduced landfill burnings. Furthermore, wastewater, that would normally be discharged in the harbour,
is treated in the Biogas scenario. During anaerobic digestion, volatile fractions of input streams may be
reduced with up to 81 % [37] and for wastewaster it means a substantially better state for discharge
compared to having no treatment. However, as other volatile fractions from the other wastes are added
(market waste, manure and fish residues), the volatile fractions in the final digestate may be higher than
the untreated wastewaster fraction. Therefore it can be worse to discharge digestate to seawaters than
untreated waste wasters from the station. It is recommended to ensure proper use or disposal of digestate
if biogas production is chosen as a treatment option.
4.5.6
Pilot scale implementation
A mean to test the suggested treatment option and obtain the recommended data to reduce uncertainty, is
by setup of a small pilot scale solution. A small pilot scale solution here means, for the fish silage solution,
a few drums and batches to investigate the quality of the feed and to test on animals for feed taste. For
the biogas solution, the biogas digester should only be a few cubic meters to observe the specific biogas
yields, and practically test for optimal digester conditions.
87
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:103==
5
CONCLUSION &
RECOMMENDATIONS
5.0.1
Conclusion
The given study investigates possible technical options for fish residue treatment in Somaliland for the non-
governmental organisation FairFishing and seeks to determine most suitable and economic viable options
that can function as a ”proof-of-concept” solution to FairFishing’s ”waste-to-resource”-project.
Fish residues generated by FairFishing’s partners in Berbera predominately originate from tuna processing.
Fish residues make up in average 49 % of the fish. Fish residue generation is projected to reach two tonnes
per day from Somaliland annually, where landfilling is the current management method for fish residues.
Seven different options for fish residue treatment in Somaliland are found possible and have been investi-
gated further. The seven technologies found are: Current management, fish meal production, fish silage
production, black soldier fly larvae production, biogas production, biochar/charcoal production, and com-
post production. Based on the produced ranking tool for decision making support, two options are found
most suitable for treatment of fish residues produced by FairFishing’s partners in Somaliland: Dried fish
silage and biogas production.
A functional unit of ”treatment of 640 kg fish residues, anno 2021 and its projected increase from 2021-2030,
generated at Berbera station and market” was formed and set the boundaries for the produced ”proof-
of-concept”-scenario for fish silage and biogas production. With a discount rate of 12 % and a project
life of 10 years, the financial analysis shows that the dried Fish silage scenario is more economically viable
than the Biogas scenario. The net present value (NPV) of the dried Fish silage scenario reaches 170,000
USD with and without funding of capital and start-up costs. The Biogas scenario shows a substantially
lower NPV of 700 USD without and 35,000 USD with funding. For both scenarios, different options exist
to improve the feasibility. Since Biogas’ NPV is relatively close to zero, it especially makes sense to look
into how feasibility can be improved for this scenario, e.g. by setup changes and waste collection fees.
Both technology options have multiple critical parameters that can, when altered, affect the NPV of the
scenarios. The risk analysis, with all critical parameter included, showed that the NPV distribution of the
Fish silage scenario has a higher deviation than for the Biogas scenario. It does, however, still have a high
chance (app. 90 %) of a greater NPV than the most likely NPV of the Biogas scenario. There are, though,
two major risks of producing fish silage; the input needs to be fresh and the demand is not known with
certainty. These risks are not associated with anaerobic digestion, as it is known that the output can be
used internally in FairFishing and that the input to anaerobic digestion is not needed to be fresh. Risks
with biogas production include uncertain accessibility, quantities and composition of all required in-going
waste streams. These all affect digester size and biogas yield.
As fish silage has a chance to generate the highest NPV of the two selected options, fish silage production
has the greatest potential to improve livelihood and quality of life for FairFishing’s partners. On the other
hand, viability of biogas production without funding is questionable if feasibility is not improved. Due to
the uncertainties of the results, it is highly recommended that risks are looked further into before one of
the options is implemented.
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
88
==PAGE:104==
CONCLUSION & RECOMMENDATIONS
5.0.2
Recommendations
Based on the results found from the study, it is recommended to look further into specific aspects of
relevance for each scenario and common data for the two treatment options suggested for fish residue
treatment in Somaliland:
To improve the Biogas scenario’s viability and obtain more certain results, it is recommended to:
• Investigate if other organic waste streams are available and in what quantities during the year;
• the waste streams’ chemical composition;
• how to make stakeholders participate and donate organic waste to a treatment system.
These recommendations are made as other organic waste streams can be incorporated into the biogas
production and possibly improve conditions in the reactor, e.g. by increasing Carbon:Nitrogen ratio, lower
the concentration of nitrogen, thereby lower the total solids concentration and needed reactor size.
For the dried Fish silage scenarios’ viability, the most uncertain factors are in relation to the demand of
the feed. Thus, for net profits to become more certain, it is recommended to further asses the demand on
alternative animal feed by gaining additional information from farmers on:
• The price they are willing to pay for fish silage;
• how often they are willing to buy feed and in what quantities;
• how willing their livestock are to eat it.
To ensure that all fish residues are delivered at the facility as fresh as possible for highest possible fish
silage yield, it is additionally recommended for fish silage production to:
• assess how fish residues today are disposed of;
• investigate how disposal can be improved.
For both options, a way to increase revenues is by introduction of a waste collection fee, e.g from the local
government. Furthermore, a common risk of the two scenarios lay within the fact that the input material,
fish waste composition and mass flow, are based only on literature values and communication. Better data
on these aspects will allow more specific setup configurations and thereby possibly lower capital costs for
the Biogas scenario, and proper price-setting and production volume for fish silage. Therefore, it is further
recommended to:
• Investigate the possibility to collaborate with the local government on economic support in form of
e.g. a waste collection fee paid by them to handle station generated fish residues.
• Collect fish waste samples over longer periods, and analyse for chemical composition (ultimate
analysis). This to find the specific composition of generated fish waste, for determination of specific
reactor conditions and fodder potentials.
• Measure the exact amount of fish waste generated at Berbera station and market. This in order to
determine mass flow and the fluctuations of the input for treatment and thereby also the amount of
output generated with higher certainty.

Data collection guide 
In order to assess the feasibility of different technologies that can convert fish waste into 
valuable products, the project requires information on the fish waste, mass flows in the local 
area, how the surrounding look, and what other waste types there may be produced locally that 
can be incorporated if needed. This document contains the different guides on how to obtain 
the required information.  In the Table of Contents, you find description of work tasks for each 
day and guides on how to do them.  Within each chapter there is a thorough timetable. Overall, 
we propose 7 days to conduct all work at the following locations:

Photo and Video Guide 
Why do we need photos and videos?  
With the current situation, we cannot ourselves conduct the field trip and data collection and 
must therefore, in order for the thesis to have solid data to calculate and evaluate on, have data 
collected by others. In this case photos and video will act as “our eyes” and show what we may 
ourselves have observed. They will be crucial as they provide us with an understanding of the 
surroundings and the environment the proposed management options will support, will include 
and be situated in.  E.g. we would like to, through the photos and videos, observe if there are 
other relevant waste streams that can be incorporated into the system, if there is space in the 
local area for a facility, how the current management looks and how the area is maintained, as 
this gives us an insight into the physical suitability of the technologies.  
How to take the “good” photo 
                          
 
The photos need to be taken in good quality and good lighting. Also, include the whole object in 
the photo. Describe when (date) and where (location or address) each photo was taken.   
 
How to take a “good” video 
The videos need to be taken in good quality and good lighting. Keep the camera still, preferable 
in a horizonal/landscape manner, and when turning around to show surroundings, turn very 
slowly with both hands on the phone to avoid blurring. Try to catch whole objects in the video 
rather than half buildings, half waste piles etc.  
“Good photo”                                    “Bad photo”

5 
Interview Guide 
Why do we need someone collect data through interviews? 
In order to obtain reliable and up-to-date data, interviews of local stakeholder have been 
planned. Their information will provide us with knowledge on where fish waste is produced and 
how much, alongside what other waste types they produce that may be incorporated into the 
system. These values will make it possible for us to estimate how much end-product the proposed 
technologies may in fact produce and thereby how much may potentially be sold and provide an 
income.  Equally important, their feedback provides us with information on what the locals need 
and want, and thereby what end-product from treating the fish waste they will value higher and 
has a higher chance at succeeding.  
How to conduct the interviews 
We have prepared different interviews for different groups involved with fish and other foods. 
The groups are: fishers, restaurant owners, fish venders, and fruit/meat/vegetable venders. It is 
important that the correct interview, made for the specific group, is used.  
The questions for the interviews are open questions. Under each question have been given some 
free space to write the answer. Print out empty copies for each new interview conducted, in order 
for us to avoid misunderstandings.  
The number of interviews needed per group are stated in the beginning of each interview guide.

1. Guide for “Measurements of fish residue density at station” 
Why do we need the fish residue density? 
The fish residue density will be used in various calculations and dimensioning of different 
treatment options of fish residue to produce valuable products from the fish residue, instead of 
sending it to the landfill. The data collected will therefore help correct dimensioning and 
calculations of the treatment options suggested, which will increase the sustainability of the 
system and make sure to get the most out of the treatments suggested as well as strengthen 
future decision making. 
How to measure density? 
The density is a measure of weight per volume (kg/m3). 
Therefore, we need the weight of a certain volume of fish 
residue. Find a round bucket or a squared box with fish 
residue, weight the fish and measure the volume. 
Furthermore, pictures are needed of the fish residue and 
the bucket.  
It is important that the residue is representative for what 
is normally wasted. Therefore, a big amount of waste is 
important to do a correct measure. Find a big box which 
is around the size of the one showed on the figure to the 
right, or a round bucket. It should at least be half full.  
 
The density are measured by the following steps: 
Weight of fish residue 
Weight the bucket with fish residues:   
 
_______________ kg 
Weight the bucket without fish residues:  
 
_______________ kg 
Volume of residue (choose either a round bucket are the square box) 
For a round bucket the following steps are to be done 
Measure det diameter of the bucket:   
 
_______________ cm 
Measure the height of the fish residues in the bucket:  _______________ cm  
For a squared box the following steps are to be done 
Measure the length of the box:  
 
_______________ cm 
Measure the width of the box:  
 
_______________ cm 
Measure the height of the fish residues in the box: 
_______________ cm 
Note: If the box/bucket has a handle (see picture) or something else that makes the dimensions 
odd, please measure the lengths of them and note them downwards: 
Odd items 
Description:  
_______________________________________________  
Length:  
 
 
 
 
 
_______________ cm

1. Guide for “Find fertilizer and NPK values” sold on market 
Why do we need NPK values of fertilisers sold on market? 
N (nitrogen), P (phosphor) and K (potassium) indicate the quality of a fertiliser. We are going to 
compare the fertiliser sold on the market with a compost that can be made from fish residues. 
The comparison will be made on background of the NPK value in the fertiliser and the compost. 
How to get NPK values? 
NPK values are often declared on the sold fertiliser. Therefore, find a place that sells fertilisers 
(i.e. on markets) and read the NPK values. They are often given in %. Note down the percentages 
of the what it contains (also write down if other values than N, P and K are declared). If the values 
are given in the format 1-3-5, note it down like that. If the values are given in another format note 
it down and remember to note down the units as well:

2. Guide for “Find animal feed” sold on market 
Why do we need to know what type of animal feed that are sold on the market or other 
places? 
It is possible to transform fish residues to animal feed. Thus, if we find out that there is a market, 
hence a need for animal feed in the area, we are going to examine different options of producing 
animal feed from fish residues. We therefore need to know if animal feed is sold in Berbera and 
Hargeisa. If it is sold, we need to know what type of animal feed that are sold and the prices of it. 
The different types of animal feed will be used to find out the energy and protein content that 
can be substituted with fish residues. 
Producing animal feed from fish residues, which are at the moment wasted on dumpsites, will 
create a more sustainable treatment system for handling of fish residues. It has a potential to 
create revenue (as it can be sold) as well as lower greenhouse gas and other emissions that are 
formed at the dumpsite as well as emissions related to production of the feed that is substituted 
with fish residues. 
How to find animal feed? 
We need you to go to the market (or somewhere else) to check if animal feed is sold. If you find 
something, then fill in the following steps: 
Animal feed 
1. Where is animal feed sold (i.e. on local market)? Note down all places where it is sold. 
_____________________________________________________________________________ 
2. What types of animal feed is sold? Note down all types. (e.g. soymeal, fish meal, maize, 
barley etc.) 
_____________________________________________________________________________

1.  Guide for “Registrar neaby industry” 
Why do we need to know about nearby industry? 
Several of the management options for fish waste requires co-treatment, e.g. biogas and 
composting. If a nearby industry produces large amounts of organic waste, e.g. manure, fruit 
waste, yard waste etc., it may be possible to collect and treat their waste simultaneously with the 
fish waste, which will result in better use of both waste streams alongside better and higher value 
end-product from the fish waste.   
How to find nearby industry? 
In order to find and select relevant nearby industry (in both Berbera and Hargeisa), hear locals if 
they know any relevant companyes or organisation nearby that produce waste that they do not 
treat themselves but instead landfill/dump. The waste must be organic and prefrably in large 
amounts. It may be from industries within the city, e.g. some food processing industry, or farms 
in the outskirts of the city.  When you have found relevant industries, fill out the below section 
for each: (note min. 3 relevant nearby industries)

Products they produce:__________________________________________________ 
Any observed waste produced:____________________________________________ 
If possible, take pictures and write date and time:   Date:_________________. Time:__________

1. Guide for “Find fertilizer and NPK values” sold on market 
Why do we need NPK values of fertilisers sold on market? 
N (nitrogen), P (phosphor) and K (potassium) indicate the quality of a fertiliser. We are going to 
compare the fertiliser sold on the market with a compost that can be made from fish residues. 
The comparison will be made on background of the NPK value in the fertiliser and the compost. 
How to get NPK values? 
NPK values are often declared on the sold fertiliser. Therefore, find a place that sells fertilisers 
(i.e. on markets) and read the NPK values. They are often given in %. Note down the percentages 
of the what it contains (also write down if other values than N, P and K are declared). If the values 
are given in the format 1-3-5, note it down like that. If the values are given in another format note 
it down and remember to note down the units as well: 
1. NPK and other contents 
APPENDIX METHOD
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
116
==PAGE:132==
 
 
21 
_____________________________________________________________________________ 
2. Name of the fertiliser (if any) 
_____________________________________________________________________________ 
3. What is the price of the fertiliser?  Note down all prices and the amount (i.e. 1 US$ 
per kg or full price and weight of the bag) 
____________________________________________________________________________ 
 
Photos (See “Photo and Video Guide”) 
1. Take a picture of the fertiliser, from the front and from the back and the labels on 
the fertiliser. 
 
2. Guide for “Find animal feed” sold on market 
Why do we need to know what type of animal feed that are sold on the market or other 
places? 
It is possible to transform fish residues to animal feed. Thus, if we find out that there is a market, 
hence a need for animal feed in the area, we are going to examine different options of producing 
animal feed from fish residues. We therefore need to know if animal feed is sold in Berbera and 
Hargeisa. If it is sold, we need to know what type of animal feed that are sold and the prices of it. 
The different types of animal feed will be used to find out the energy and protein content that 
can be substituted with fish residues. 
Producing animal feed from fish residues, which are at the moment wasted on dumpsites, will 
create a more sustainable treatment system for handling of fish residues. It has a potential to 
create revenue (as it can be sold) as well as lower greenhouse gas and other emissions that are 
formed at the dumpsite as well as emissions related to production of the feed that is substituted 
with fish residues. 
How to find animal feed? 
We need you to go to the market (or somewhere else) to check if animal feed is sold. If you find 
something, then fill in the following steps: 
Animal feed 
1. Where is animal feed sold (i.e. on local market)? Note down all places where it is sold. 
_____________________________________________________________________________ 
2. What types of animal feed is sold? Note down all types. 
_____________________________________________________________________________ 
 
3. What is the price of the animal feed sold? Note down all prices and the amount (i.e. 
1 $ per kg)

Chemical formulas of macro-nutrients considered for fish residue
composition
Macro-nutrients chemical formula considered: Protein C16H24N4O5, fat (lipids) C50H90O6, Carbohydrates
(C6H10O5)n [10]. Fibers were assumed to have same chemical formula as carbohydrate. Ash contents of
Sulphur (S), Potassium (K), and Phosphor (P) was included from other sources.
A.2
Technology screening
A.2.1
Yield estimations
Nutritional and feeding potentials
The nutritional potential was calculated for the three technologies producing animal feed: fish meal, fish
silage and black solider fly larvae. Nutritional potentials are commonly expressed as MJ Digestable crude
Energy per kg feed option or as gram Digestable Crude Protein per kg feed. Acid detergent fibre, neutral
detergent fibre, amino acids, fatty acids, vitamins, flavonoids, minerals, aflatoxins and net energy gain tend
also to be used for analysis of nutritional potentials [18, 115, 130], but was for this report out of scope.
Nutritional potentials were calculated based on macro-nutritional values; crude proteins (CP), crude fat
(CF), Crude Ash (CA). These values were either directly taken from the input fish residue composition if no
change in composition occurred during product generation or found from literature for the specific product
type. Based on following equations by Madsen et al. [79], the digestible content of each feed type was
determined: (DCP = digestible crude protein, DCE = Digestible crude energy, DCF = Digestible crude
fat, DCa = Digestible carbohydrates. TS= total solids equivalent to dry matter content)

For this analysis, feeding potentials were calculated feeding potentials of a Tropical Livestock Unit (TLU)
[45, 64]. A TLU is a livestock unit defined as a 250kg animal body weight (the average weight of a camel)
[45, 64]. The daily TLU feed requirements were determined based on the knowledge that 2.5% of animal
body weight per day is the expected feed intake, thus 6.25kg TS per day per TLU. Hereof 70% has been
assumed to be provided by hay/straw and 30% by concentrate i.e. 4.4kg hay/straw and 1.85kg concentrate
per TLU per day. TLU can be converted to following livestock by: 1 = Camel; 0.7 = Cattle; 0.10 = Sheep;
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
130
==PAGE:146==
APPENDIX METHOD
0.10 = Goat; 0.01 = Chickens [45, 64]. Assuming in Somaliland, that livestock are fed Rhodes grass hay
and maize grain as concentrate, the daily digestible energy and protein requirements were calculated as the
sum of hay and maize’s nutritional values. From these values, the TLU that the suggested feed options may
provide energy and protein for, are determined assuming that only energy and protein are required from
the feed and not fiber, vitamins etc, and that there is a linear relationship for the nutritional requirements
of the different livestock types. Here it is found that Rhodes Grass Hay, a grass type typical in East Africa,
has a dry matter content of 86.5% and a calculated DCP and DCE content of 0.06kg DCP/kg WW and
15.39 MJ/kg ww, respectively. Dried maize grain grown in east Africa has very similar values; 90% TS
and a calculated DCP content of 0.04kg DCP/kg WW and a DCE content of 16.59 MJ/kg WW. Hence,
A TLU requires 86.1 MJ/TLU/day and 0.317kg DCP/TLU/day. For calculation on reference values, see
section D.1
A new unit was formed, in order to determine how many TLU possible to feed with a given amount of ;
number of tropical livestock unit potentially fed per kg fish residues feed options (TLUF), which tells how
many TLU can be fed either only based on energy or protein content, as shown in below equations. In
general, as fish residues are high in protein, the energy content is the limiting factor. Dependent on desired
application, feeding to satisfy protein of energy need, this should be considered.

The TBMP is based on Boyle’s formula and assumes the following [2]:
1. The biogas digester is kept at constant standard temperature and pressure
2. The digester is continuously stirred for optimal mixing
3. There are ideal living conditions for the microbial community for biogas production
4. The feedstock only consists of the elements C, H, O, N and S
5. There are no other products generated than CH4, CO2, NH3 and H2S.
A methane content in biogas of 66.8% was applied in accordance with real life findings from Heeb [60].

A.2.2
Substitution potentials
Feed substitution
The substitution potentials of the suggested feed options was initially calculated based on the assumption
that the conventional feed in Somalia was either hay, maize grain, or soybean meal. It was later discovered
from surveys of limited number of farmers, that livestock in Somaliland are only fed hay and occasionally
oats and food scraps. Therefore, only results of hay are provided in the report, but substitution potentials
of maize grain and soybean meal can be found in section D.1.
Protein and energy substitution potentials (kg substituted feed/ tonne WW fish residues) were
found by multiplying Equation A.9 and Equation A.10 with the weight of produced fodder option from one
tonne WW fish residues.

Fuel substitution
For substitution of conventional fuels, it was assumed that charcoal made from fish residues can substitute
conventional wood based char 1:1, thus that there was no quality loss in the conversion process.
The methane is the only fraction of biogas that can be utilised for energy production and only diesel and
LPG substitution was considered in this report based on FairFishing wishes
When the yield potentials of the biogas technology was determined, the total substitution potential was
calculated by multiplying the diesel or LPG equivalent with potential biogas yield. The equivalents for
0.5kg kerosene diesel is 1 m3 biogas [138]. With a diesel density of roughly 0.85kg/L [121], this results in
a diesel equivalent of 0.59 L diesel/m3 biogas. Vögeli et al. [138] furthermore states an LPG equivalent of
0.20 m3 LPG/m3 biogas.
Fertiliser substitution
Fertiliser substitutions were calculated based on knowledge on conventional fertilisers in Somaliland. AD-
NOC UREA was found at local markets as the only commercial conventional NPK product and with its NPK
value of of 46:0:0 (46 mass% nitrogen), only the substitution potentials of nitrogen was determined. This
was done by by dividing compost nitrogen content (kg N/kg compost) with 0.46kg nitrogen/kg ADNOC
UREA.

A.3
Risk analysis
Method and equations for parameter and NPV distribution
For normal distributed parameters, mean values and standard deviation were estimated. For uniformly
distributed, min. and max. values were estimated. For triangularly distributed parameters, mode, min.
and max. values were estimated. The values of the critical parameters were dependent on the assigned
distribution.
Uniformly distributed parameter variables were determined as:
x = U(b −a) + a
(A.12)
where x is the parameter variable, U is a random value between 0 and 1, b is maximum and a is minimum.
Triangularly distributed random variables were determined as:
