B
STAKEHOLDER IDENTIFICATION
B.1
Stakeholder identification and description
In the following, each stakeholder is described in more detail.
FairFishing
: FairFishing is the NGO that have initiated the ”waste to resource” project and are the
project owners. They are responsible for all planning, funding, and implementation and they determines
the output of the project. The NGO is highly affected by the interest of the local community and their
partners needs. The project has the potential to make FairFishing’s activities more sustainable, in line with
their sustainability strategy and goal, which will benefit the local community.
FairFishing Partners
: FairFishing’s partners are local stakeholders in close contact with FairFishing.
It has been informed by Gulled [57] that at the moment approximately 50 partners are registered at
FairFishing. Partners are both highly affected by FairFishing’s decisions and are involved in FairFishing
decision making processes. It was informed by Schmiedel [112], program manager in FairFishing, that the
Producer Organisation FairFishing was formed in 2015 in order for FairFishing’s partners to discuss their
needs and proposals, to build a basis for decision making and to organise regular meetings. It was formed
as an attempt to make decision making more formal. Partners are often boat and car owners and are
communicators between FairFishing, fishers and fish mongers. They hire fishers and are responsible for the
distribution of fish to local markets. Often partners are fishers or mongers themselves, while also hiring
staff, but in some cases partners do not have any staff hired under them and are just fishers or mongers.
This project has the potential to lower expenditures related to the partners activities, with a potential lower
price on fish cooling and other FairFishing activities. The partners are relevant to communicate with during
the waste project, as they can communicate with their staff, especially fishers, about relevant aspects as
e.g. gutting at the station.
Fishers hired by FairFishing partners
: Local stakeholders hired by and in close contact with
FairFishing partners.
They take part in training programs set up by FairFishing and are part of the
organisation. There are two types of fishers, boat fishers and foot fishers. It has been estimated by Gulled
[57] that there in total are about 450 boat fishers distributed over 150 boats with on average three fishers
in each boat. There are less foot fishers and these are often partners themselves. Fishers contribute to the
project with all fish residues originating at the station. The greater the share of fish gutted at the station,
the greater the share of waste stays in Berbera as an easy accessible source. Fishers may benefit from this
project by lowered expenditures related to fishers activities, with a potential lower price on fish cooling and
other FairFishing activities. This can create an incitement for fishers to gut a greater share of fish at the
station. As fishers are the users with daily activities at the station and has the potential to generate a big
share of the waste included in the system, they will be the first hand users of a generated product, if a
product usable for them is produced. If animal feed on the other hand is produced, fishers will not benefit
in terms of the product produced. If that is the case, they should benefit economically e.g. by lowering
prices on fish cooling with the money earned from selling animal feed. This will also work as an incitement
for the fishers to gut a greater share of the fish.
Local non-FF fishers
: Non-FairFishing fishers located in Berbera is a stakeholder that is not currently
involved with either FairFishing or their partners, but could potentially be relevant to establish contact to.
Even though they are not a part of FairFishing, local non-FairFishing fishers can contribute to the system
and benefit from it like the FairFishing fishers. The advantage of including also non-FairFishing fishers, is
135
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:151==
APPENDIX STAKEHOLDER IDENTIFICATION
the potential improvement of a larger share of the waste system in Berbera. This will create value from
an instead wasted resource, to benefit a larger share of the population, including FairFishing. As for the
FairFishing fishers, they will benefit from the project if products produced are available for all fishers and
if the prices are lowered compared to alternatives. Local non-FairFishing fisher is however not the first
priority for FairFishing and as the amount of waste originating from them is not known at the moment, it is
not the plan to consider them in the implementation of this project. Fish residues generated at the nearby
fish-processing businesses not collaborating with FairFishing is an easy accessible additional fish residue
source and therefore non-FairFishing fishers should be considered in the next phase of implementation.
Local mongers
: Local fish mongers can be FairFishing partners as well, but are not necessarily directly
a part of FairFishing. They sell fish caught by FairFishing and non-FairFishing fishers. A share of the
fish delivered at markets is gutted and filleted before it is sold. Fish mongers is therefore another source
of fish residues. It is known form the later surveys conducted, that fish mongers at markets in Berbera
and Hargeisa uses ice regularly. A potential lower price on fish cooling, e.g. by ice, will therefore benefit
fish mongers, which could be an incitement for fish mongers to separate and give their fish residues to
FairFishing. Meat, fruit and vegetable mongers situated at markets in all relevant parts of Somalia are
stakeholders worth communicating with, as they could potentially provide the additional organic materials
to the waste treatment solution, that is needed in order to obtain optimal conditions and to create a
high quality product in some of the suggested technologies. These stakeholders are not currently part of
FairFishing’s partners and not a stakeholder FairFishing at the moment is in contact with. It is debatable
whether or not there are direct benefits for meat, fruit and vegetable mongers to participate in the system
by separating their organic waste to FairFishing, as it is unclear how the pricing on waste collection fees
for restaurants are based, e.g. by kilo or fixed. There must hence be looked into different scenarios of how
to make them benefit, in order to create an incitement for them to donate their waste.
Local households
: Local households include the households that purchase fish and generate fish
residues as they prepare, fillet and cook the fish in both Berbera, Hargeisa, Burco and in the area of
Puntland. Similar to fish mongers and restaurants, they are one of the potential sources for fish residues to
be incorporated and treated in a suggested management option. Fish residues are not the only generated
organic waste from households, and if possible to convince households to separate all organic waste, e.g.
scraps, fruit peels or even household animal manure, every single household is a potential point source
for fish residues and other organic waste that can improve performance of some of the waste treatment
methods. To include their waste streams though require additional effort as households are spread over
a vast area. Households are not in direct communication with FairFishing and not currently involved in
this project by FairFishing. Their interest may further be reduced as they cannot buy the products that
the potential solution may produce that are planned for FairFishing’s internal use. Only products from the
project that are planned to be sold, may be of relevance for the households. The first hand customers are
likely the stakeholders with daily activities at or around the station or FairFishing offices, hence, fishers
and partners. If the prices however become compatible with conventional products, house owners may find
interest in purchasing the fish residue based products as well and benefit from the this current project by
a lower product price and hence their own economic savings.
Local chefs and restaurant owners
: Owners of small, medium and large scale restaurants with
fish on the menu is a potential stakeholder worth communicating with. Restaurants with fish on the menu
is one of the potential sources of fish residues to be included in the waste treatment solution, as fish is
filleted, prepared and wasted here. Restaurants are however distributed over a vast area and they are not
currently suggested as part of the project, as big efforts are required in order to collect a relatively scarce
amount of waste. It is yet the idea to include fish and potentially other organic wastes from restaurants
in the system at a later point. Restaurants selling fish is dependent on cooling options for fish in order
to store it. Results from later conducted surveys of restaurant staff in Berbera and Hargeisa showed that
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
136
==PAGE:152==
APPENDIX STAKEHOLDER IDENTIFICATION
the majority, at the moment, uses freezers running on diesel or electricity for fish storage. The surveys
moreover showed that most restaurants uses ice every day and that some uses coal and some uses gas for
cooking. Restaurant owners can, thus, benefit economically from the system if produced fish-residue based
products are accessible for restaurants at a lower price than the current conventional products. Restaurant
owners are however not part of the stakeholder group with daily activities around the station, hence not
first hand customers. They may only benefit from the system if the amount of available product produced
is big enough to first cover the needs of the first hand customers. If this is not the case, another incitement
for restaurants to participate in the system may be created.
Farmers
: If a waste treatment solution with need of other organic waste inputs than fish residues is
suggested, this stakeholder may be relevant for FairFishing to establish contact to. In that case, farmers
include stationary crop and animal farmers located in the region. All types of organic waste, e.g. manure
and crop residues, produced here may be relevant to include. Nomadic farmers will likely not be relevant,
as these will not be located at the same location for long, and hence not provide a stable and reliable waste
resource. Cooperating with farmers providing stable and significant amounts of organic materials to the
treatment solution will be beneficial in terms of a greater and better output. Animal farmers may benefit
from the system, especially during droughts, if animal feed is produced. It is known from the later farmer
surveys that during droughts feed scarcity can be a big problem in Somalia leading to starvation and deaths
of farm animals. It is know from these surveys, that crop farmers uses fertilisers and crop farmers may
hence benefit from the system if fertilisers is produced and accessible free of charge or for a lower price than
the current market value. Dependent on the treatment solution, it may be relevant to establish contact to
farmers in the first implementation phase. At the moment, farmers are not a stakeholder FairFishing works
with and hence extra effort must be needed in order to establish contact.
Local waste collectors
: Local waste collectors are defined as the current waste treating companies
today handling municipal solid waste where fish residues are included. For this stakeholder, the importance
can be divided into a short term and a long term scenario. On a short term basis, as FairFishing’s project
initially unfolds, the fish residue flows will be redirected out of the masses that are normally handled by
the waste companies for collection and landfilling. The payment method is yet to be confirmed, however if
the waste collection fees are fixed and not part of taxation, all customers that still have other fractions of
waste will remain customers. On the other hand, if a customer pay according to weight of waste or if the
only waste they generate is organic or fish residues in near proximity to a FairFishing treatment plant, the
companies can in theory lose customers and income, when the organic waste is redirected. A diversion of
fish residues from the MSW could both reduce the amount of waste that the waste management companies
handle, thereby save them time and fuel, and slow down the filling of the already overloaded landfills. A
long time result of a FairFishing project can be co-operation with a waste company to collect the separated
organic waste of households and remote towns to be incorporated into a treatment system. In that way,
the project offers a solution for the companies to expand and help develop a more environmental friendly
use of their organic waste streams. The opinion and interest of the waste companies to be engaged in
such a project may therefore vary. At the moment, local waste collectors are not a stakeholder FairFishing
works with and hence extra effort must be needed in order to establish that contact.
Local city council
: The local city council is the provider of basic services such as waste management,
health and environmental conservation [39]. An important position within the city council is the local
Mayor, who is elected by the citizens. The mayor is locally anchored, has a big voice within the city
and is highly respected by the local community, as informed by Schmiedel [112], FairFishing Program
Manager. As they have the authority over projects within their directory, the city council and its mayor are
identified as important stakeholders. Therefore, good standing with the city council and mayor is extremely
valuable. The city council and mayor are not directly affected by the output of the project and not directly
included in it, however listens to their local community and their thoughts on impacts of such a project.
137
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:153==
APPENDIX STAKEHOLDER IDENTIFICATION
Additionally, the council may have their plans affected if large waste quantities are redirected from their
current waste management plans. As mentioned in chapter 2, Berbera city council is today highly invested
in environmental upgrade and is therefore affected if waste flows are redirected from their current plans.
The project may though potentially be incorporated into their plans of better local waste treatment and
thereby help to improve the local environment by redirecting it from the current burning of waste at the
landfill without energy recovery. This proposes that communication with the local city councils is of high
value as they are the ones approving a potential local project and the planning of it, while constituting
legal assistance.
Somaliland Government
: Somaliland Government includes the ministries that affect and to a degree
is affected by the results of FairFishing’s project. The ministries of relevance are; Somaliland Ministry of
Ministry of Planning and National Development, Somaliland Ministry of Livestock and Fisheries Develop-
ment and Somaliland Ministry of Environment and Rural Development. Somaliland Ministry of Ministry
of Planning and National Development is the Somaliland Governmental department in charge of leading,
facilitating and coordinating national development with the vision to achieve rapid sustainable development
of their population’s life quality. To achieve the visions, the main tools of the ministry are to procure and
provide accurate, timely and relevant data to their population and relevant internal and external organ-
isations; to coordinate, prepare and facilitate development projects; to form guidelines for their citizens
and businesses to achieve their national goals; and to register NGO and facilitate cooperation between
public, international and national organisations and businesses [120]. The ministry in closest contact with
FairFishing is Somaliland Ministry of Livestock and Fisheries Development, as NGO’s in Somaliland has
a registration and approval by the main ministry within their work area. Informed by Schmiedel [112],
FairFishing Program Manager, the specific ministry plays the major part in forming projects, agreements
and development between the local fishers, industries and NGO’s to improve the regional fishery sector.
Today, the ministry manage Berbera Station, that was formerly managed by FairFishing, and is now
in charge of all FairFishing’s previous activities at the station, including ice production and costs related
thereto. Similarly, the ministry may in the future affect FairFishing’s projects and their activities. Therefore,
clear, diplomatic communication and collaboration with this specific stakeholder is crucial. As all suggested
solutions either are usable by the fisher, farmers or to a degree households, restaurants and mongers, the
ministry benefit from FairFishing project as people working within their sector receives benefits from the
project. Somaliland Ministry of Environment and Rural Development may also show interest in such a
project, as they currently are running a project that works towards finding alternative sources of energy
to reduce local charcoal consumption and reduce deforestation. For them to cooperate on a project that
does in fact offers other means of cooking fuel from non-forest sources, supports their goals and vision
[119]. Both ministries, as they have high influence on foreign NGO’s in the region, also both have the
power to accept, reject or seize projects of this caliber in the state. As they, on the other hand, also are
the ones that know the nation and their people the best and can provide any necessary nationwide data,
communication with the ministry must be done very delicately. Somalia Government has not previously
been in contact with FairFishing, despite it being the ruling national body, and is therefore for the current
project not found relevant.

D.2
Additional description of fish silage production
All calculations were performed in the attached file: Appendix_Technology_Review.xlsx
Fish silage is a liquid or dry substance which consist of hydrolysed (broken down) fish proteins and micro-
nutrients. It is used as animal feed or as a fertiliser and is often made from fish residues. The process
is an enzymatic breakdown of the fish, initiated by addition of either an organic or inorganic acid (i.e.
formic acid or sulphuric acid) or a fermenting unit together with a bacterial culture (a single carbohydrate
source i.e. molasses). Enzymes from the skin, muscle, and the digestive system breaks down proteins in
the fish to form amino acids and peptides. Nutrients in fish silage are easily digestible as they are highly
bioavailable due to the hydrolysed proteins and micro-nutrients. The process also works as a preservation
method, which makes silage storable for years [127, 129]. The processing steps are the same for both
types of silage production. However, using a fermenting unit and a bacterial culture requires closer control
and is more complicated than using an organic or inorganic acid [129]. Inorganic acids are often of lower
costs compared to organic acids, but requires lower acidity, which implies that the product is diluted before
feeding to animals while also meaning greater corrosion problems during the process [127]. Pérez [97]
suggests addition of either 3.5 % acid or 20 - 30 % of a single carbohydrate source for fermentation, per
tonne fish residues, see Table 4.9.
Most of the enzymes which drive the hydrolysis are contained in the fish guts, which make guts essential
to include in the process [129]. Silage production requires a low pH, as the enzymatic breakdown is only
occurring in acid pH ranges. An acidity below 4 furthermore keeps flies away and prevent fungi and bacterial
growth. Ideally, the pH is between 3.5 and 4 (for inorganic acids even lower; around 2). A pH value lower
than than the recommended range is not advised, as it is more costly (addition of more acid), requires
dilution and causes greater corrosion problems [127, 129]. the pH value should therefore be measured
during the silage process, to ensure the pH value is in the right range. Due to the acidic environment it is
important to make silage in acid resistant containers, i.e. polyethylene [127]. Hydrolysis enzymes are active
between 5 and 40 ◦C and hence silage can be produced in that temperature range. High temperatures
speeds up the process and in tropical climates, as in Somaliland, the process only takes a few days [127].
Fish silage can be produced as a wet or a dry product. For wet use, silage is mixed with other feeds when
served, whereas dry fish silage is dried at the facility after the silage process, in order to obtain dry feeding
blocks [7].
D.3
Additional description of biogas production
Dewatering and scrubbing of biogas
Dewatering involves removing the water from the saturated biogas. This can easily be performed by letting
the biogas flow through cooler pipes to condense the water and introducing a valve at the lowest point of a
biogas pipeline, that will allow the condensed water in the pipe to drain out [138]. Scrubbing involves the
absorption of hydrogen sulfide, and can be performed in either a dry or a wet form. In the wet scrubbing
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
150
==PAGE:166==
APPENDIX TECHNOLOGY SCREENING
method, mainly used for medium-large scale plants, the gas is passed through a water spray counter-counter
under pressure where both hydrogen sulfide and carbon dioxide is absorbed. In the dry form, used for small
scale application, the gas is let through reactive ferrous materials (e.g. iron sponges) in a closed container.
The iron reacts with the hydrogen sulphide to from iron sulphide. The sponges can be ”cleaned” and
reactivated by introducing oxygen. Once it is scrubbed it loses it smell. Odourless biogas makes it harder
to detect leaks. Biogas leaks should be continuously checked as it in confined space with oxygen and an
ignition source pose a risk of explosion [138].
Differences of dry and wet mode anaerobic digestion
The dry mode offers shorter retention time, reduced water use and can treat a larger variety of feed stock
[13]. Dry anaerobic digestion is traditionally performed in batch sequences, meaning that a tank is not
continuously fed with feed stock for digestion but rather loaded and immediately stored for digestion process
in a closed container period without any interference. This additionally means that each batch has to go
through the microbial start up phase whereby the biogas production becomes very unstable and fluctuates
with time [138]. The wet mode, which is conventionally fed continuously or semi-continuously, obtains a
steady and high biogas yield with lower capital costs compared to dry digestion [13].
Elaboration and differences between different digester types
Three wet mode digesters types are commenly used in low-middle income countries; Indian floating drum,
Chinese fixed dome and balloon tube digester.
The Indian floating drum digester is made of a steel drum that lays as a lid on top of the slurry. As the
gas is produced it will lift and rise above the concrete walls of the digester. For the Chinese fixed dome, it
is fixed below ground and made commonly of bricks. The balloon digester is flexible as it is made of folded
polyethylene foils. Digester design affects the temperature stability. Here the most stable and naturally
isolated is the floating drum followed by the fixed dome. Lastly, due to high exposure to ambient air, the
balloon design. The balloon digester is commonly the smallest, however found by several companies to
be scale-able and constructed in combined units [19]. By observation, normal capacity of a unit is a few
m3 with a short lifespan of 2-5 years, due to its more fragile material, however can be prolonged by using
protective measures such as coverage with a green house or branches. The largest un-isolated digester that
have been observed purchasable today were made of the balloon digester type either made up of smaller
units to reach 250 m3 or as a app. 200 m3 large single balloon [110, 111]. The fixed dome is typically
constructed in the size of 6-16 m3 and has, due to its solid construction materials, a long lifespan (15-20
years). The floating drums, however, tend to be build in a larger range of sizes (1-50 m3), but has been
observed even at 150 m3 for municipal solutions [56, 138]. The typical lifespan for floating drums are 8-12
years in dry climate and less in humid areas [138]. Floating drums typically float directly on digester fluid,
however, to reduce odours from the digester fluid outside of the drum, a water jacket for the drum to float
on is recommended [138].
Elaboration on anaerobic digestion parameters
Methanogens, the microbes that produce methane in the last step of biogas production (Methanogenesis),
need a Carbon:Nitrogen ratio of roughly 25–30 for optimal methane production. Excess nitrogen can be
converted to free ammonia and cause ammonia inhibition, whereas excess carbon will cause fast nitrogen
depletion and slowing down the process [109]. Low methane production, ammonia smell and decrease in pH
indicates a low C:N ratio. To solve the issue of a low C:N ratio, an additional carbon source can be added
in the form of e.g. vegetables or fruit waste [60]. In the initial step of anaerobic digestion, the fats and oils
in the waste input is rapidly cleaved into glycerols and long chain fatty acids (LCFA). LCFA, that will fast
accumulate, is also known to have acute toxic affect on the incoculum, also when treating fish residues [37].
Wastes high in fat and oils should gradually be introduced to the incoculum to allow microbial adaption
151
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:167==
APPENDIX TECHNOLOGY SCREENING
[11]. Salinity of input material has too shown to be disadvantageous for anaerobic digestion, however can
be solved by microbe adaption and low OLR [80]. Biogas production requires a feedstock with a high
water content to ensure optimum microbe accessibility to substrate, but may also dilute possible inhibitory
concentrations of nutrients. In cases where dilution of the input waste is necessary, water, other organic
waste streams with high water contents, or recycled digestate can be added to the digester. Recycling
of digestate, however, as ammonia and LCFA are recycled, causes a risk of accumulating inhibitory levels
of nutrients, as experienced by Heeb [60]. As microbes adjust to certain temperatures, it is important
to keep operating temperatures in the reactor stable, as temperature fluctuations may change the biogas
production rate, which may ask for adjustment of the HRT for optimal methane yields [109]. To start up
a digester and the anaerobic process, the right microbe mix in the inoculum is needed. Here diluted cow
dung, as it contains the desired microbes, can be used with a 1:1 water ratio. It takes up to a few weeks
before continuously methane is produced. The organic loading rate (OLR) is how much volatile solids that
daily is feed to the digester. In a non-continuously stirred reactor, it is recommended for the OLR to stay
below 2 g VS/ L reactor / day, otherwise overloading of the system may occur and result in volatile fatty
acid (VFA) build-up [138]. HRT is a measure of how long the input feedstock stays in the digester before
it substituted by other feed stock and no longer produces methane. In a tropical climate, a 30 HRT is
recommended [138]. To digest the fibers of fruit and vegetable waste before feeding it to a digester for
biogas production, an installation of pre-digesters is advisable, otherwise there is risk of blocking [60].
Digestate production from applied technology
Digestate was considered a byproduct of biogas production and was therefore not showed in the main report.
However, calculations on digestate production was also calculated: The produced digestate would contain
the ash, water and the VS that was not consumed and converted to biogas. As the applied biogas potential
was 50% lower than TBMP, only 50% of VS was assumed removed in this setup. Water was also used during
the biogas process, mass determined by Equation A.7, and in total resulted in a digestate mass reduction of
234 kg/ d, when assuming 50% of optimal water consumption was removed. The daily digestate production
would therefore be 38,008 kg with a TS content below 1%. According to Sogn et al. [117], the nitrogen
value of the digestate is equivalent to the NH+
4 (ammonium) concentration, as this fraction is easy available
for plant uptake. As the ammonium concentration can only be determined experimentally, a representative
ammonium concentrations was adopted from Eiroa et al. [37]. As Potassium (K) and Phoshor (P) are not
transformed to gasses, they are assumed to remain in the digestate and be diluted with the water input
to the digester. The concentrations of N, P and K were expected to be 0.76, 0.18 and 0.081 g/L in the
digestate, respectively, equivalent to a NPK value of 0.076:0.018:0.0081.
D.4
Technology ranking and selection
In the following sections the reasoning behind the category scores is described and the used weighing is
explained. For calculations performed as part of Ranking, see attached file:
Appendix_Weightening_Ranking_Sales.xlsx
For simplicity, the technologies are in the following sections addressed as their individual outputs with
capital first letter: Current management, Fish meal, Fish silage, BSFL, Biogas, Charcoal and Compost.
D.5
Category scores
In the following appendix, an explanation of each category, each technology’s score (from 1-5), and the
weighing scores (from 1-12) that are displayed in the comparison section subsection 4.3.2 is explained. As
described in chapter 3, the technologies are scored in different categories from 1-5 where 5 is poor and 1
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
152
==PAGE:168==
APPENDIX TECHNOLOGY SCREENING
is good. Thereafter the categories have been given a weight score from 1-12, illustrating how important
they are considered for successful use and implementation by FairFishing. The categories are divided into
themes (Technical challenges, Capital costs, Operation costs, Revenue, Sustainability,and Demand) for the
sake of clarity.
D.5.1
Technical challenges
Climate sensitivity
This category was made to score each technology option according to how sensitive the proposed tech-
nologies are to present climate conditions in Somalia. Reactions and sensitivity to both daily and more
occasionally drastic changes in climate (e.g. temperatures changes and rainfall events) were included in the
evaluation. A low score, (1), indicates a robust technology that can handle and adapt to large fluctuations
in climate conditions (e.g. temperature and humidity). A score of (5), on the other hand, indicates a
sensitive technology that is affected low changes in climate conditions (e.g. temperatures and humidity).
Keep in mind that all technologies were initially chosen as they are currently used in warm, low humidity
zones and therefore the climate sensitivity of all of the suggested technologies are low.
It is here the
marginals that are assessed.
For Fish silage in its dry form, the hydrolysis enzymes function in a large temperature range (5.0-40 ◦
C) [129], hence given temperature fluctuations and droughts are therefore not considered an issue. The
process is operated in closed containers, hence extreme rain events and droughts are not an issue. A minor
issue may be storage of the final product mixed with hay, as it must be kept dry and thus, due the large hay
volume, requires large coverage. It was given a score of (1). The Current management describes the present
situation. The waste piles are occasionally burned and it is therefore assumed that high temperatures and
a relative low humidity is preferred, however drought may cause a risk of wildfires, and rainfall can result
in washout and disturbance of piles. The option still functions under large climate variations, but as there
is risk of uncontrollable environmental impacts and wildfires, a score of (2) was therefore given. Fish meal
production is a closed process and is currently produced in both warm, temperate and cold climates. It was
therefore not assumed to be sensitive to temperature variations. The machinery may however be affected
by high humidity, that can corrode the metal. A score of (2) was therefore given. Charcoal production, as
it prefers dry feedstock, benefits of a low humidity for both drying of feedstock, pyrolysis and later storage
of the charcoal. Rainfalls may affect production to a large degree, especially if the work area is situated
outside without coverage. Daily temperature changes are not assumed to affect production, and occasional
droughts have a positive effect, as it speeds up the drying process. A score of (3) was therefore given. In
the BSFL production, the Black soldier fly, eggs and larvae prefer stable temperatures around 24 - 33 ◦C
and high humidity (>60 %) [67]. Daily fluctuations are within an accepted range, however droughts may
drastically affect the production. Thus, a score of (3) was given. Compost functions most optimally at
internal temperatures of 40 - 60 ◦C, which are supported by the few degrees lower average air temperatures
in Somaliland. At a humidity of 50-60 %, the composting process has its decomposition optimum. A
low moisture content (<30 %) inhibits the activity and high moisture content (<65 %) slows down the
decomposition and increase leaching [134]. Roofed composting is an option to control temperature and
humidity, but not necessarily economical viable in this case, where more than 1000 m2 needs cover, hence
droughts and high temperatures are of special concerns. Therefore, a score of (4) was given. As for Biogas
production, a 70 % decrease is observed in production rate if temperatures within the reactor fall from 35
to 20 ◦C and may vary with 10 % as temperature changes from 30 - 35 ◦C [140]. If not proper isolated
the internal temperatures in the reactor may be affected by surface temperatures. To balance out the
reduced production rate, the retention time can be prolonged, however this means a larger digester volume.
Literature does not indicate that anaerobic digestion is sensitive to changing humidity, however it may
affect and corrode the equipment if a air is very humid. If the air is very dry, e.g. due to a drought, it is
imaginable that a incorporated water jacket may evaporate and that may cause emissions and odors in the
153
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:169==
APPENDIX TECHNOLOGY SCREENING
nearby surroundings. As the floating drum type digester is partly isolated, score of (4) was given.
Need of co-collection
Some treatment options need co-collection of other organic waste streams as they cannot be treated alone,
e.g. due to a required C:N ratio. This is a disadvantage due to the logistics associated with accessing other
waste streams as well as the dependence of them. The grade (1) was given to facilities where there is no
need of co-collection; (3) to facilities that can be managed without co-collection, but preferably with; and
(5) to facilities that cannot be managed without and where co-collection thereby is a need. As described in
chapter 4, Fish meal and Fish silage, as they can only use fish as input, does not function with co-collection
and Current management can easily be operated without any other inputs. They were thereby all given a
score of (1). Biogas and BSFL were given a score of (3), as they can be operated without co-digestion
(co-collection). For biogas, however, this process requires huge fresh water dilution if not co-digested,
which complicates the process. Compost and Charcoal cannot be operated without co-collection. These
facilities hence scored (5).
Nuisance from facility
This category covers all possible nuisances that can be associated with waste treatment (odours, vectors,
aesthetics), however special emphasis has been on odours. Waste treatment facilities are often associated
with odours to a greater or lesser extend depending on the specific treatment option, odour controls,
and the treated material [26]. Dealing with fish residues can be associated with severe odours from the
degradation of it [62]. Sever odours can cause problems for the surrounding community which can in
worst cases can end up in the facility getting closed. Odours is therefore important to consider. The
grading system for nuisances are as follows: (1) was given to highly controlled facilities with advanced
odour controls and restrictions on waste deliveries; (2) was given to facilities where waste is delivered fresh
and is being handled quickly in a closed environment with no severe odours (no waste drying) and no
potential attraction of vectors; (3) to facilities that includes drying of fish residues, but where the waste is
otherwise handled quickly; (4) to facilities with waste degradation associated with odours, but where some
odour control is included in the setup; (5) to facilities with severe odours and no odour control. The grade
(1) was not given, as none of the solutions are highly controlled facilities. Fish meal is known to cause
smell from the the drying process. The produced hot air will pass over the fish and spread vapours [144].
Minimal vectors and pests are expected, as there is no open drying of waste. A grade of (3) was therefore
given. Both Fish silage and Charcoal includes open drying of fish residues and BSFL includes open drying
of larvae, where all are associated with odours and potentially vectors and pests. Furthermore, additional
odours are associated with pyrolysis gases from Charcoal production. For these reasons, the facilities were
given the grade (3). Compost was given the grade (4), as open composting are often associated with
odours and potentially vectors [26]. It however includes some odour control. Similarly, Biogas is known to
potentially cause severe odours. Heeb [60] showed this to be the case in a biogas setup similar to what has
been suggested. As it is a closed system with a water jacket, some odour control is used and no vectors or
pests can assess the facility. Thus, it too was given the grade of (4). Current management was found from
data collection to have weekly burns of the waste piles at the landfills which functions as odor and pest
control, besides reducing the waste volumes. However, the data collection in Hargeisa also showed animals
living at the dump site and feeding from the waste. With the uncontrolled waste burning with large smoke
formations, additional odors are expected. The current management was therefore given the grade of (5).
Technology level
Technology level defines the ease of operating and maintaining the facility, e.g. do the workers involved
need to know about specific chemical processes or electrical installations to obtain maximum yield of
the input fractions or to handle fluctuations. Here a high score, (5), indicates an advanced technology
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
154
==PAGE:170==
APPENDIX TECHNOLOGY SCREENING
that requires technical knowledge to maintain, whereas a low ranking score, (1), indicates a technology
that is easily operated by non-educated employees.
Fish meal, as it is the only option with electrical
installations, was graded the highest score (5). Biogas and Charcoal both require background knowledge
to handle fluctuations in input/output, e.g. decreasing biogas production due to ammonia inhibition, or to
increase/minimise the char to oil fraction. Biogas, however, does not produce an output, if the operator
do not know which chemical process has ceased and how to start up the system again. Thus, Biogas has
been given a higher score (4) than Charcoal (3). Compost and BSFL, have been found simple relative to
Charcoal, as they involve known processes; turning of waste or feeding of insects (larvae). Therefore they
obtained a lower score (2) compared to Charcoal. Both Fish silage and Current management are almost
self-maintained with limited requirements of technical knowledge. They both obtained a score of (1).
Transportability of output
It is important that an output can be transported if the products are to be sold e.g. on local markets or
potentially being exported to other countries in the future. Thus, it is a benefit first of all that there is a
product and then that the product can easily be transported for long distances. Here (1) indicates that the
product can easily be transported without any complications; (3) that the output is transportable, but with
some complications; and (5) that the output is not transportable or that there is no product to transport.
Fish meal, Fish silage, BSFL, Charcoal & Compost outputs are easily transportable and were given a score
of (1), as they are comparably dry and in a solid form. Biogas is complicated to transport properly for
large distances, as it either requires a pipe system infrastructure or gas bags, there with low gas pressures
take up large volumes. This technology was hence given a score of (3). The Current management does
not produce any output and a score of (5) was given.
Experience and interest in East Africa
This category was made in order to investigate how established the technologies are in the region. If they
are not established it can be an obstacle as extra energy and effort should be made to inform the local
community of the benefits and use, and to form a viable clientele.
First, known cases or business that use the technologies in the region was investigated to determine if
there is local experience in the technologies. Online literature and company searches [1, 47, 70, 91, 101]
indicates that all of the proposed technologies exit in the East African region, but only Fish silage [71] was
specifically found in Somalia. However, Fish silage was in general extremely hard to find known business
cases on in the region and only that one case from Somalia was found.
A general issue with the searches is that Somalia’s first language is Arabic and therefore projects and
industries have been difficult to search online, but may in fact exist in the country. The proposed technolo-
gies were also ranked based on the current interest of each technology in East Africa, shown as number
of youtube searches in the region. Youtube.com was assumed to be a main media communicator in the
region, as observations show that youtube is widely used in East Africa as a common platform to share
and communicate business and ideas. These search hits do not only show how the interest of the different
technologies in specific regions is developing, but may as well indicate how established the technologies
are, under the assumption that non-established options in the region are likely not searched. Furthermore,
if there is no interest, investments should also cover campaigning to increase awareness. The number of
global searches on keywords that are associated with the different technologies were found for the last 12
years and analysed, see Appendix_Weightening_Ranking_Sales.xlsx for search hits. As the searches were
done in English, hits are predominately shown in English speaking countries, which can be the reason why
searches in Somaliland were low. Keep in mind that a limited number of keywords were used, but the
one used were those found best describing and known by the general public. The results showed that the
technologies with current highest interest globally are Biogas and Charcoal. In a East African context, both
BSFL, Biogas and Charcoal had largest concentrations of searches in the East Africa region. Composting
155
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:171==
APPENDIX TECHNOLOGY SCREENING
was marginally more searched than fish meal in the region. As composting can be performed at both
household and municipal levels, this could be the reason for the higher search number. Fish silage had
clearly the smallest amounts of searches globally now and in the last 12 years. Current management had
similarly few hits, but when searching ”dumpsites”, a small increase in the East African region appeared.
The current management stands out, as a fact is already established and used widely in East Africa. The
search hits however shows the present attention in the region to be low. Based on this relative qualitative
ranking, combination of these facts resulted in a score distribution of (1) to Biogas and Charcoal, (2) to
BSFL, a score of (3) was given to Compost and Fish meal. Fish silage received a score of (4), as it is not
of big interest, yet found in Somalia. For the special case of Current management, is was given as score of
(2) as it is established and must therefore be well-known in the region, but has little interest and attention
in the general public.
D.5.2
Capital and maintenance costs
Construction, material and maintenance cost
This category covers construction and implementation of the facility in terms of both materials purchase,
infrastructure construction and construction worker’s salary for building a facility to treat one tonne of fish
residues a day. Purchase of land is not included as it is represented in the Category Space Requirements.
A relative ranking has been used, where the technology with no construction and implementation costs is
represented by a score of (1) and the highest score (5) is given to the option where import of materials are
needed and there is a need for technicians and specialist for construction and installation. As all facilities
needs a waste delivery, storage, packaging, etc. as well as office and employee facilities, these expenditures
are assumed to be equal for all and was not included in the following discussion and ranking. The score
for this category is based on the construction and material costs, however, as ongoing maintenance costs
can be assumed to be a set fraction of material costs, they are in this way represented in the score.
Current management, as it is not an expenditure for FairFishing has been given the lowest grade (1). Fish
silage should preferably be placed close to the fish residue source as the input must be unspoiled. As fish
silage production is very simple, no specialized installation is found necessary. Initial cost includes purchase
of equipment and containers for production, but as they are all assumed available in Somaliland, they can
be assumed to have low cost since no import is required. Drying fish silage, however, includes purchase
and construction of a solar tunnel dryer. It was assumed to purchase one in Somaliland as it is a simple
construction and Fish silage was therefore given a score of (2). Equivalent, BSFL can be constructed
with local materials; The plant will need 800 larveros (plastic boxes) for one cycle, nets for mating cage,
buckets for feeding, tools for loading and unloading of waste, buildings with roof to keep undesired vectors
our and to control humidity and shade for the larvae. Due to considerably low building and equipment
costs, BSFL production has been given a score of (2) as well. Compost’s investment costs depends on
composting method, however costs for materials and construction are considerably low; it requires tools for
turning waste (no turning machinery used in this case) and possibly construction of a roof, a plastic liner
and drainage for the piles, which can be obtained and constructed locally due to simple and easy-available
building material. With the current setup, the facility spans 1467m2 for a daily intake of one tonne fish
residues mixed with other organic waste streams. This speaks for an outdoor open windrow composting
without roof cover, which can also keep the construction cost substantially low. The plant, both due
to nuisances and space requirements, should be placed outside of urban area and may therefore requires
additional infrastructure costs. With the current suggestion, no machinery is purchased but rather manual
labour is used for turning piles. It is an option to purchase a loader for waste turning to keep labour costs
low but was not considered here. As there is a large space demand and associated construction with it,
Compost was given a higher score than Fish silage and BSFL, here (3). Charcoal production requires drum
kilns or solar tunnel dryer, rotating drums and machinery for grinding of input. As no cases of charcoal
production are observed in Somalia, the more specialised equipment, rotating drums, are assumed to be
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
156
==PAGE:172==
APPENDIX TECHNOLOGY SCREENING
imported. As it is a rather simple setup, no specialist are assumed needed for setup. Thus, due to both
import of tools but no need of specialists for construction, the option was given a score of (4). Biogas
production in this case requires a minimum construction of app. 1600 m3 digester, that can be subdivided
between 8 floating drums with a volume of app. 200 m3 each (see section 4.3.1), and import of the metal
lids for the floating drum setup and specialist for construction supervision. As in the example by [60], the
plant can be placed close to the waste source, i.e. at a market or at the harbour side and does therefore not
require additional road works. However, dependent on desired use, a pipe system for distribution of fuel may
need setup. Overall, as there are both costs to specialized construction workers and import of materials,
a score of (5) was given to biogas production in the given scale. For fish meal production, no materials
for construction exists locally, and the plant, a finished constructed machinery of different process units
made of metal, must therefore be imported. Additional costs comes for transport and import of the plant
to Somaliland, set up by technicians and construction of a building with roof for protection. The fish meal
plant should preferably be placed close to the fish residue source and thereby does not require additional
infrastructure if placed in the city or harbour side. Similar to biogas production, fish meal production was
given a score of (5), as it also requires specialised workers for construction and import of equipment.
D.5.3
Operation costs
Labour needs
Assessment of labour needs between the suggested technologies are based on number of workers required to
treat one ton of fish residues per day. It does not include the daily transport of the waste to the treatment
facility and loading into the facility, as these are similar for all the proposed technologies. A low score (1)
translates into no workers needed to run the facility, (2) equals 1 worker for light work half to a whole day,
a score of (3) corresponds to 1 worker for physical work a whole day (8 hours), a score of (4) translates
into physical work for 2-5 workers a whole day and (5) is given only for the options that requires more than
5 workers for 8 hours work a day.
The Current management method of landfilling is simple; waste is collected by local enterprises or by
the Municipality’s waste collecters and unloaded at a dumpsite and occasionally burned. From available
literature, it was found that in Hargeisa there are loaders that can compress and distribute the waste [142].
Once the waste is collected and unloaded at the landfill, it is expected that light labour is used to surveil the
burning of the waste piles. However, as it for FaiFishing today does not require any workers, the Current
management was been given the lowest score (1).
Fish meal require surveillance, but is otherwise to a large degree self-maintaining. Fish meal production
is performed by a large fully automatic machine, as the one sold by [74]. As fish meal can be produced
at a central facility and only require occasionally cleaning it have received a low score (2). BSFL includes
several different process, e.g. oviposition, feeding, harvesting and drying, where there will be physical work
included. According to [34], running a facility with a fish residue input of one tonne/day can be done by
one worker, therefore it has been given a high score (3). Information on charcoal production is scare, but
FAO [43] estimated that one full time worker can produce 300 kg charcoal per day. From that number, it
has been estimated that to both dry the fish residues, load, control and monitor the pyrolysis drum kilns
and the rotating drums, there will be a need of 3-4 workers in total. A score of (4) was therefore given to
Charcoal. Fish silage also received a score of (4), as two workers is estimated for this process. The silage
process is fairly simple and only needs grinding, and then addition of acids or a fermenting unit to initiate
the breakdown process as well as occasional stirring and pH measurements. After the silage process, all
material are placed for drying. Due to manually transfers of heavy mass, 2 workers are estimated for this
process. The initial calculations on biogas production states that several biogas digesters are needed. If
placed together, it has been guesstimated that one worker can operate and surveil two digesters at a time.
This means that for eight digesters, four workers are required. Thus, Biogas has been given the score
of (4). Compost, as the proposed option includes weekly turnings of the piles, see section D.1, without
157
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:173==
APPENDIX TECHNOLOGY SCREENING
mechanical aid, there will be a need of up to 49 workers a day to receive waste and turn the piles that
accumulate over four months (under the assumption that piles are turned once ever week for the first two
months and once every second week the last two months). Thus, Compost was given a score of (5).
Need of continuous water input
The waste treatment options suggested require different water inputs. High water use is a disadvantage,
as water can be scare (especially during droughts) and be costly if needed in large amounts. It furthermore
complicates the process in terms of the logistics related to procuring the water. Alternatively, waste waters
can be used with almost 100% content, as it is a more sustainable option that does not to compete with
human consumable water. The issue in low-middle income countries is, however, that wastewater often is
uncontrollably is discharged, e.g. into water ways, as the case in Berbera section C.2. The given category
covers the need of water, may it be fresh water and wastewater, as both are found hard to procure.
The following grades were given: (1) to technologies with no water input; (3) to technologies with limited
water inputs; and (5) to technologies with a high water input compared to the other solutions.
Fish silage, Charcoal and Current management do not require any water input and were given the grade
(1); Fish meal, BSFL & Compost require some water input and were given the grade (3); Biogas requires
a high water input, depending on the specific treatment technology, and was given a score of (5).
Need of continuous fuel input
Some of the suggested treatment options has a fuel input (i.e. electricity or charcoal). In order to generate
monetary and environmental value, the fuel input must be lower than the output. The amount of used
fuel hence affect the value (economic and environmental) of the end product. A continuous fuel input
additionally complicates the process in terms of the logistics related to procuring it as well as the reliance
of it. A grade of (1) was given if no fuel input is required; (3) if an internal fuel input is required; (4) if low
amounts of external fuel is needed and (5) if large amounts of external fuel input is required. BSFL and
Compost do not include e.g. grinding the suggested setup and therefore do not require any fuel input and
was given the grade (1); FairFishing uses no fuel on e.g. loaders and compacters for Current management
and was hence given a score of (1). Charcoal and Biogas production requires fuel input to paralysis and
grinding, respectively. Charcoal from the production can be used internally for pyrolysis fuel, and electricity
from biogas generators can be used to fuel the electric grinder. They were hence given the grade (3). Fish
meal requires an external electricity input to drive the plant. It was therefore given a score of (5). Fish
silage requires energy for grinding as it cannot self produce. As the amounts of fuel needed for grinding is
assumed substantially lower than the need for external fuel for Fish meal and Current management, is was
given a score of (4).
Need of other continuous non-feed inputs
Other inputs than water and fuel are needed for some of the treatment options. This has been evaluated as
a disadvantage as the technology is then reliant on another input that needs to be purchased. The scores
given are as follows: (1) if no other inputs are needed; (3) if other inputs can improve the process, but are
not necessary; and (5) if other inputs are essential for the process. BSFL, Compost & Current management
were given the grade (1); Fish meal and Biogas were given (3) as antioxidants can prolong the lifespan of
the fish meal and base/acids can be used to control and maintain optimal pH for biogas production but are
not essential; An acid or a fermenting unit is essential in fish silage production & a binding unit is essential
in charcoal production. These technologies were therefore given the grade (5).

==PAGE:174==
APPENDIX TECHNOLOGY SCREENING
Space requirements
The suggested technologies requires different amounts of space.
In order to take into account these
differences, the category space requirements was made. It covers the total facility space requirements
including space for: office and employees facilities, the actual treatment unit, as well as waste delivery,
-storage, and -packaging etc. The more space a technology requires, the more land needs to be gained
access to (found, released, and purchased or rented). The ranking was set relative to the space required for
the facilities. A score of (5) was given to a technology that reduce waste volume but requires a comparably
large area, (4) to a facility with a space requirement in the range 200-300 m2, (3) to technology with
space demands in the range below 100 m2, and (1) to technology a technology whose space demands are
not a cost for FairFishing. The estimated space requirements for the suggested treatment technologies are
summarised in Table D.1. Waste is however burned at the facility, hence some volume reduction happens
at the facility. Composting has a long retention time compared to the other technologies, and thereby
requires the most space. It takes up 1500 m2, five times or more than the other facilities. Compost was
hence given a score of (5). BSFL take up 220 m2 and was given a score of (4). The space demands for
Biogas depends on the chosen feedstock mixture and dilution, but was for a fish residue mono-digestion
with 1 % TS found to be 280 m2. Similar to BSFL, it was given a score of (4). Equally, Charcoal was
as well given a score of (4) as it requires 220 m2. The least space demanding facilities are Fish silage &
Fish meal that take up l50 and 66 m2, respectively. As Fish meal only need half the space of Fish silage,
it was given a score of (2) whereas Fish silage was given a score of (3). Current management, as it does
not require any land purchase or rent by FairFishing, was given a score of (1).

D.5.4
Revenue
FF savings and sales income
Dependent on the product, FairFishing either wish to use it internally to save costs, or sell it to produce
a revenue. As Compost and the feed options are not possible to use internally, they are planned to be
sold. Biogas, on the other hand, will be used internally to generate electricity for their ice machine as
alternative to diesel and charcoal can periodically be used internally for their cooking classes and excess be
sold. Calculations of income and savings were based only on main products and therefore digestate and
undigested feed from BSFL that can be used or converted to fertiliser, were not included. The potential
sales income was calculated for the feed options and Compost as the amounts of produced outputs from
the suggested technologies (from management of one tonne fish residues along the required other waste
streams for Charcoal and Compost) times the price of a single unit of the output material. The unit price
of each product was found in literature or guesstimated. Keep in mind that literature values have not been
extrapolated to fit Somalian prices anno 2020, but newest values available from nearby countries or low-
to middle income countries were preferably used.
The potential FairFishing savings, here only from Biogas and Charcoal, was calculated based on their
quantitative substitution potentials of diesel and regular charcoal and was multiplied with current prices
of diesel and charcoal in Somaliland. As charcoal is expected to substitute regular charcoal 1:1, with no
loss of quality, potentials are equal to the charcoal quantity. For charcoal production other waste streams
are needed and therefore the quantity is high. The results are shown in Table D.2. Keep in mind that the
159
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:175==
APPENDIX TECHNOLOGY SCREENING
savings and sales income are rough estimates as they naturally will depend on location, local demand and
processing costs.
Prices of fish silage has been estimated as no available price online have been found. As oil is not removed,
and thereby obtaining a shorter storage time, and as operation costs of Fish meal is found higher, the
selling price of fish silage is expected lower than fish meal’s. Biogas’s diesel substitution potential depends
on chosen mixture and dilution of fish residue mixture, however here evaluated for a mono-digsestion with
a 1.0 %TS. For Compost the income was given as a range since it can be sold in small household bags for a
larger price than at large bulk quantities [156]. According to communication with PhD Susannna Bassi and
Associate Professor Alessio Boldrin from DTU Environment, compost often has a low to no price, which
is in line with applied unit price. However, as the quantities of compost produced with the fish residues
included, is relatively large, it potentially has a large income.
The scores for savings and income were given in a relative manner, so that a low score (1) indicates the
highest possible income or saving, whereas the high score (5) indicates the lowest income or saving. Based
on the results, as shown in Table D.2, the income and savings are provided in ranges and overlapping.
Average values where for these cases considered. The highest income and savings are found from Fish
meal, Fish silage, therefore a score of (1) was given. Charcoal and Compost scores second best, and were
given a score of (2). A score of (3) was given to BSFL and Biogas. Despite the fact that BSFL has a price
similar to the other feed options, the conversion ratio from fish residues to larvae is low which makes the
quantity low and thereby the potential income never reaches the one similar to Fish meal and Fish silage.
Current management does not generate any output and thereby has no value. Current management was
hence given (5).
