The income prices for the feed options are not local and up-to-date, and the price of feeds will high likely
increase during droughts as feed becomes high in demand. Therefore, their potential prices during such
periods were further investigated: Hay in those periods costs as much as 1.5 USD per kg. According to
protein substitution potentials, the feed options Fish meal, BSFL and Fish silage may substitute 2,800
kg, 310 kg and 3,500 kg Rhodes grass hay per tonne fish residues converted to feed, respectively, see
section D.1. The current prices of hay during droughts are unsustainable for the farmers, however, if the
prices of the animal feeds were set as high as hay for protein coverage, they could potentially be sold for
12 USD/ kg fish meal or dried fish silage or 8.4 USD/ kg BSFL, thus producing an income of 5,200 USD
161
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:177==
APPENDIX TECHNOLOGY SCREENING
for Fish silage, 4,300 USD for Fish meal and 440 USD for BSFL per tonne fish residues. This opens to
the possibility that the animal feeds in periods during high demand can be sold for higher prices, however
should not reach hay prices, as all surveyed farmers found the price unsustainable for keeping their animals
alive during droughts. For compost, it was sold based on same nitrogen price as ADNOC UREA, it may
be sold for app. 0.052 USD per kg compost, which is in line with the findings from Zurbrügg et al. [156]
Biogas is planned to be used internally. However, in neighboring countries, biogas is occasionally sold as
cooking fuel in transportable biogas bags in Ethiopia with a price of 0.40 USD/m3 biogas [65]. Had the
biogas been sold rather than used internally, a similar solution would yield an income of 53 USD per tonne
fish residues, which is higher but in line with the savings FairFishing may achieve from internal use.
Time before first revenue
The time before first valuable output is generated from each technology is relevant, as it tells when the
first sale income may start and how long initial funding have to support the project. The score range was
set to be relative. (5) is given to technologies that never generates an output, (4) to the technology that
takes the longest time to generate an output, and (1) to the fastest processing technology. As expected,
Current management was given a score of (5), as it does not generate an output. As Compost has the
longest processing time, four months, of all the studied options, the grade (4) was given. Biogas and BSFL
production both have a processing time of a couple of weeks (2-4), Therefore a score of (3). Charcoal and
Fish silage cycles only takes a couple of days (app. 6 days). Therefore, they were given scores of (2). Fish
meal, being the fastest option with almost instant production was given the lowest score, (1).
D.5.5
Sustainability
Waste hierarchy (Sustainability)
In today’s society there is a growing awareness of organisations’ responsibility to positively affect the en-
vironment, health, equality and economy. This category deals with how environmental sustainable the
different technologies are compared to each other. The ranking is not supported by life cycle assessment
studies, but on assuming that products high in the waste hierarchy overall has the most positive envi-
ronmental impact. The range is set to be relative, where a high score (5) indicates the least positive
environmental impact among the proposed technologies and is illustrated as those found at the bottom
of the waste hierarchy. The lowest score (1), indicate technologies with the most positive environmental
impact of the proposed technologies, hence options placed in the top of the waste hierarchy, e.g. feed
options. As feed options have the potential to create high environmental value, Fish silage Fish meal and
BSFL rank in the top of the waste hierarchy. However, as less feeding potential is produced from Larvae
compared to the other two options, Larvae was given a score of (2). Fish meal and Fish silage obtained a
score of (1). As Biogas and Charcoal both provide energy and/or fertiliser, they scored (3). Composting,
as it only produces fertiliser, is the option placed lowest in the waste hierarchy of all the options generating
an output. Compost hence scored (4). Current management, dumping, does not generate an output that
has a substitution potential. It was therefore given a score of (5).
D.5.6
Demand
Internal FF demand
FairFishing’s wish is first and foremost to produce a product from the fish residues that can support and
benefit the daily operations of their own activities. Therefore, the technologies have been ranked according
to the use of the products; if it can be used internally by FairFishing, it has been ranked (1), if the option
has a occasional internal use, is scored (3) and if no product or not possibly to be used internally for
FairFishing’s activities, it received a score of (5).
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
162
==PAGE:178==
APPENDIX TECHNOLOGY SCREENING
Biogas can be used to run either a diesel or biogas engine for electricity production that FairFishing can use
to generate their daily production of ice. It therefore received a score of (1). As Fairfishing occasionally run
workshops that educate the local community in fish preparations and cooking, Charcoal has a periodically
purpose as FairFishing to fuel their grills. Hence, the score of (3). The remaining, Fish meal, BSFL, Fish
silage, and Compost, all received a score of (5) as FairFishing do not themselves have livestock they can
feed fish meal, BSFL or fish silage to, nor a production of crops that can benefit from fertiliser. The Current
management, as it has no product and therefore no beneficial internal use for Fairshing, also received the
score (5).
Local demand
The local demand score is determined qualitatively based partly on interview results and FairFishing com-
munication. From data collection, fishermen, vendors and restaurant owners were asked what they desired
a lower price on and farmers were asked, as they are the main costumers of animal feed and fertilisers, if
they have a need to buy animal feed and whether or not they buy fertilisers. These factors were used for
discussion on demand.
From all surveyed fishers, mongers and restaurant owners it was found that 19 % find it most important
to reduce price on ice cubes, 55% find it most important to reduce price on diesel, whereas 26% find it
most important to reduce price on coal (See section C.1). As the majority naturally worked within the
food industry where cooling is necessary, this also reflects the results; to cool down the meat, diesel is
necessary to run the ice machine or electricity generator. To reduce price on ice, an option is to reduce
price of diesel. Biogas is the only technology that can substitute diesel and generate energy and has the
possibility to compete price-wise with diesel. The local demand for Biogas was therefore set to (1). Keep in
mind though, that households have not been part of the questionnaire and their opinions are not reflected
in the results, but as the stakeholder affected by the fish residue management improvements are first and
foremost FairFishing and their partners, their demand has been set to weigh the most. For these reasons
Charcoal’s local demand was set to (2).
From surveys of farmers around Hargeisa, see section C.1, it was found that hay is the main animal feed
sold and is only purchased as alternative feed-source during times with no available grass on the grazing
lands, e.g. during droughts. During normal periods, free grass and grazing is sufficient for their animals. A
Hargeisa goat farmer mentioned that when there are droughts farmers buy hay to the extent economically
possible but that their animals die when they cannot afford the feed. This was confirmed by FairFishing
staff. The prices of hay have been found extremely high; 1.0 kg hay costs 2.0 USD, which are app. 5 times
more expensive than hay sold in Denmark. Fish meal, fish silage and BSFL can substitute partial amounts
of hay and therefore save substantial costs during such events. As can be found in section D.1, the feed
options can substitute 5.9-8.2 kg hay per kg option for protein content and as the prices for op the options
range from 0.15-0.39 USD per kilo, large savings can become an incentive for farmers to purchase the fish
based animal feeds. Due to the occasionally observed (during droughts) high need, the local demand for
animal feed (Fish meal, BSFL or Fish silage) was set to (2).
Surveys of farmers showed that if the farmers have livestock, they will use the manure as fertilizer, otherwise
they will purchase ammonium nitrogen and ammonium phosphate fertiliser.
Manure is naturally free
whereas fertiliser costs 1.0-1.3 USD per kilo, see section 4.2.1.
The price of compost is lower than
commercial fertiliser and may therefore be of relevance to the farmers as it can substitute the more costly
fertiliser and benefit the soil in terms of structure. As manure however is freely available by the large animal
sector, the demand of Compost was set to (4).
The Current management concept of waste removal and reduction is in high demand, but as the manage-
ment option has no end products that can be a valuable resource for FairFishing, the local demand was set
to (5).
163
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
==PAGE:179==
APPENDIX TECHNOLOGY SCREENING
Market Volume
Market volume is determination of the number of people that may in fact buy the product. For scoring a
relative range has been used where (1) is given to the option with largest market size and (5) to the option
with no or a small market size.
For Compost’s market volume, the biggest issue is that the users, the crop producers in Somaliland, are
few and scarce. Controlled agriculture and large scale crop production is not established to a large degree
in the country and only few are found around Hargeisa. The farmers are few in numbers, especially in
the Berbera area, and not all use commercial fertilisers. Households in Berbera, on the other hand, may
find the compost relevant. From data collection it was found that fertiliser (mineral fertiliser, and nitrogen
fertiliser) can be bought at both Berbera and Hargeisa urban markets in small bags which support that the
urban population can be included in the market size. It was therefore given a score of (3).
As one of the largest contributors to Somaliland’s GDP is livestock export, the animal feed options have
been found to potentially have a large market. As the whole population does not depend directly on animal
feed, a score of (3) was given to all feed options Fish meal, Fish silage and BSFL.
Biogas and charcoal can both be used as cooking fuel which is used by the majority of the population.
Biogas can substitute both diesel and gas, however these fuel sources are used by a smaller share of the
population than charcoal is. Charcoal were therefore given a score of (1) where Biogas supports a score
of (2). Biogas, as FairFishing wish to use it, will be used for electricity production, which is not used by
the broad population but by FairFishing partners, who are relatively small in number compared to users of
coal. This additionally supports the lower scored compared to Charcoal.
Similar to previous category, the Current management sells its service which covers 50-65 % of the inhab-
itants in Berbera an Hargeisa. However, as the management option has no end products that can be a
valuable resource for FairFishing, the market size from FairFishing point of view was set to (5).
D.6
Weighing scores
In the following, an explanation of each weight score that is displayed in the comparison section subsec-
tion 4.3.2 is explained.
The category that has been assessed to matter the most is Internal use in FF. FairFishing has specifically
asked for a product from fish residues that can mainly be used internally to fuel their electricity generators
or otherwise be incorporated into their daily activities and be of value for the organisation itself. FairFishing
is the project owner, and therefore their needs come first. The weight score of 12 was therefore given to
the category, equivalent to 24 % of the total weights. Thereafter the remaining categories have been given
a weight score ranging from (1) - (5), equivalent to 2 - 10 % of the total weights.
The categories that received a weight score of (1) were found least important by FairFishing and authors:
Nuisances from facility, Transportability of output, Experience and interest in East Africa, Space require-
ments, Need of continuous other non-feed inputs, and Time before first revenue. For an NGO that aims
at producing a local product at a fish station in a low-to-middle income country, these issues are either
found easy solvable or not an issue to deal with. Nuisances from facility can often be a problem for waste
treatment facilities, but has in this context been considered as a minor problem. The Transportability of
the output is normally important, as if difficult or expensive to transport, it is hard to sell elsewhere than
the local community. This was however not valued as a big deal, as the first priority for FairFishing is
to use the input internally. Gaining access to land was not seen as an issue and Space requirements was
hence judge as a minor problem. Need of continuous other non-feed inputs was not assessed to cause
major problems, as these inputs are relatively small for all technologies, it is however associated with costs
and logistics when other inputs are needed. Time before first revenue indicates where high capital costs
A waste management system to treat fish residues in Somaliland
164
==PAGE:180==
APPENDIX TECHNOLOGY SCREENING
are placed and where the NGO should make additional effort to secure funding for. Funding was however
not found an issue for FairFishing. Experience and interest in East Africa indicates if similar projects are
present in nearby areas and thus how likely this project will succeed. All the suggested solutions are however
present to some extend in Somalia or nearby countries and with the right people on the project, this should
be manageable.
The categories that received a weight score of (2); Climate sensitivity, Need of co-collection, and Labour
needs are found to be of higher importance. Climate sensitivity to a high degree indicates how robust
the systems are in the specific geographical placement, thus how economically risky they are to invest
in. Co-collection is of higher importance as it suggests whether or not FairFishing should make additional
effort to procure waste streams they are not themselves freely producing. Labour needs are here valued as
a negative matter due to the costs; on the other hand, the project would create jobs in a country where
the unemployment rate is high. It was therefore only given a weight of (2).
Seven categories received a weight score of (3); Technology level; Construction, material and maintenance
costs; Need of continuous water input; Need of continuous fuel input; FF savings and sales income; Local
demand; and Market volume. The argument is that FairFishing wants a technology that to a large degree
is self-maintainable after implementation with easy operation that require as little expert knowledge needed
as possible; FairFishing do not wish for an option that has high ongoing costs as ongoing funding is not an
option; they desire an option that is able to generate an income that can possible maintain a system; they
want an option that do not depend on scarce and expensive resources. Market volume and Local demand
was by the authors given a weight score of (3). As the products that are in excess for FairFishing or cannot
be used internally need a relevant and substantial clientele to generate revenue from the produced product.
The Local demand, which can be narrowed down to the local community and their partners interests, is of
importance, as it tells what the community FairFishing is trying to improve and will appreciate the most.
Waste hierarchy (sustainability) received a score of (5), as it is used to show how sustainable each option
is, and as FairFishing is specifically seeking an improvement of their current management that is in line
with their sustainable agenda, this has been weighted highly.

E.1
Single inputs and final mixture composition for biogas
production
The final chemical composition of the mixture entering the digester was calculated based on waste streams’
weighted percentages and inserted into Equation A.8, for determination of mixtures’ methane potentials.
The Theoretical biochemical methane potential (TBMP) was thereafter adjusted to realistic values by
multiplying with a factor 0.8 in line with Achinas and Euverink [2]. Table E.1 summaries the chemical
composition of single input streams, the final mixture and its non-adjusted and adjusted TBMP and biogas
potential.

up costs
Construction was estimated to take take two days (14 hours) for five people,
training was estimated to take seven hours for two people, and transport to

per collection round.
Operation costs
Land is available in the precise size needed, Land estimated based on drying
time and processing time. Labour estimated based on first year annual FR
input, labour need and labour wage. Labour need assumed 12 hours/d (two
hours for delivery and grinding, six hours for processing and drying, two hours

Assume all other fish residues are given to the project for free.

from year 6-9 it is increased to 10%. Batteries (24 units) assumed needed and
to be changed four times a year.

Notes on assigned distributions:
• Labour need: Standard deviation is set to be equivalent to 3 hr/day, as a precaution
• Selling price of Fish Silage: The price is varied uniformly between the oat and hay price, as these
are known ranges on feed prices today
• First year fish residue input: Based on a known fish waste fraction that spans from 37-60 %, thus
app. 10 % higher or lower than applied value to determine waste quantities. An additional 10 % is
added due to literature uncertainties (240,00*20%/3 = 16,000)
• Sold fish silage ratio: Assumed that all is most likely sold (ratio = 1), that no more than 100 % can
be sold (> 1), and that the chance of 0 % is sold decreases linearly to 0.

Notes on standard deviations:
• Labour need: Standard deviation is equivalent to 3.5 hr per day as a precaution
• First year daily fish residue input: Based on a known fish waste fraction that spans from 37-60 %,
thus app. 10 % higher or lower than applied value to determine waste quantities. An additional 10
% is added due to literature uncertainties (640*20%/3 = 43)
• Diesel price: Based on deviation observed in neighbouring countries, a maximum spread of app. 22
% was set, thus a standard deviation of (0.54*22%/3 = 0.040)
• HRT: What we recommend as maximum standard deviation
• Biogas potential: Standard deviation is set based on a 3 times standard deviation of maximum 50
% of applied value, as biogas potential easily can be reduced, as shown in literature (Section 4.3.1).
Maximum is potentially overestimated, as a 3 times standard deviation means a substantially higher
potential than the theoretical maximum, however the composition may as well be more beneficial
than initially assumed
